# 3 Barings Brothers 1890 Sovereign Debt Crisis
# Contents
# Barings Brothers 1890 As Example Of Kindleberger "Model"
Let's use the 1890 Barings crisis to understand the Kindlberger model a little better
The Baring Crisis is the nineteenth century's most famous sovereign debt crisis. (Mitchner & Y Weidenemeir 2006)
Barings Brothers was one of the top-notch Merchant Banks - private banks - that dominated investment, banking, and wealth in London, and thus the world. Owned by members of the Barings family.
Two particularly interesting aspects
I. A private "bail-out" by market participants, very much like LTCM in 1998, and the transfer of deposits to First Republic Bank in March 2023
II. Argentina defaulting on sovereign bonds - sounds very modern
Displacement & Opportunity and Economic Growth & Expansion
Investors throughout 1800s believed there were unusual opportunities in Latin America
- Even back in 1720s, South Sea Bubble was about trading with Spanish America
- Kindleberger's table shows "clearing of southern lands" and "Brazil, co ee"...
# Background:
- Argentine loan, Barings 1824, default and consolidated in 1857
- In 1870s Argentina suffered bad crisis, saved by the Barings
- Problems in Argentina: 1885 suspended gold standard; faulty fiscal planning led to inflation
# Euphoria And Speculation
Speculation and over-investment in Argentine securities, particularly by Barings Brothers
Between 1881 and 1889, British banks took Argentine loans worth over £85,000,000. In most of these loans Barings was the leading house.
Under leadership of Edward Charles Baring (Lord Revelstoke 1885) "Barings had placed in the market nineteen million pounds' worth of Argentine loans in the past seven years. Apparently the philosophy of conservatism... were temporarily abandoned. Even among the firm's partners there was no agreement about Lord Revelstoke's methods."
In 1888, Argentina received £36,102,766 in various loans from Europe. 'No warning can save a people determined to grow suddenly rich,' said the great British banker, Samuel Jones Lloyd (later Lord Overston).
# Financial Distress
- Problems in Argentina: 1885 suspended gold standard; faulty fiscal planning led to inflation
In November 1888 a £3.5 million o ering of the Buenos Ayres [sic] Drainage and Waterworks Company failed, and Barings felt obliged to lend to Argentina through acceptance credits. Falling raw materials prices in 1890 made it impossible for the Argentine government to meet these credits as they came due.
- Bank of England raised Bank rate from $3\%$ to $4\%$ (to curb drain of gold) in June
1890. Raised to $6\%$ on Friday Nov 7.
    - Rumors about "a certain house" which might fail.
# Crisis And Recovery
- Wonderful details of comings and goings of bankers and politicians
# Goschen Conversion:
- George Goschen, 1st Viscount Goschen, was Chancellor of the Exchequer from 14-jan-1887 to 11-aug-1892.
- In 1888 Converted $3\%$ Consols to $2.75\%$ (£2 15s) through April 1903 and $2.5\%$ (£2 10s) thereafter.
- In other words, lowered the interest cost of the government (during a period of falling interest rates)
# Outline Of 1890 Barings Crisis Thomas Coleman
Taken from The Merchant Bankers, Joseph Wechsberg, Weidenfeld and Nicolson, London,
1967. Some from Kindleberger. More references at the end.
"The Baring crisis, the firm's worst ordeal and the most dangerous predicament in the whole history of merchant banking, occurred in
1968. The seeds of trouble had been planted much earlier. In the 1820's Argentina suddenly became a glamorous attraction for the investors and speculators in the City of London." (Wechsberg, p. 131)
# Background:
- First Argentine loan, $6\%$ £1mn Buenos Aires issue, at 85, o ered by the Barings in 1824, went into default and consolidated in 1857. (p. 132).
- In 1870s Argentina suffered bad crisis, saved by the Barings
- Tide turned, in 1870 40,000 immigrants; by 1889 260,000. In 1878 Argentina started exporting wheat
- "Between 1881 and 1889, British banks took Argentine loans worth over £85,000,000. In most of these loans Barings was the leading house." (p 132)
- Under leadership of Edward Charles Baring (Lord Revelstoke 1885) "Barings had placed in the market nineteen million pounds' worth of Argentine loans in the past seven years. Apparently the philosophy of conservatism... were temporarily abandoned. Even among the firm's partners there was no agreement about Lord Revelstoke's methods." (W p. 134)
# Problems In Argentina:
- 1885 suspended gold standard; faulty fiscal planning led to inflation. "In 1888, Argentina received £36,102,766 in various loans from Europe. 'No warning can save a people determined to grow suddenly rich,' said the great British banker, Samuel Jones Lloyd (later Lord Overston)."
# Proximate Details:
- "In November 1888 a £3.5 million o ering of the Buenos Ayres [sic] Drainage and Waterworks Company failed, and Barings felt obliged to lend to Argentina through acceptance credits. Falling raw materials prices in 1890 made it impossible for the Argentine government to meet these credits as they came due." (Kindelberger p. 122)
- Bank of England raised Bank rate from $3\%$ to $4\%$ (to curb drain of gold) in June 1890. (Wechsberg p. 133). Raised to $6\%$ on Friday Nov 7.
- Rumors about "a certain house" which might fail.
# Details Of Crisis And Bail-Out:
- Sir Everard Hambro met Sat Nov 8 with Rotschilds, then BoE. At BoE Hambro "told [Governor] Lidderdale that Barings was 'much involved.' Lord Revelstoke [of Barings] had said to him [Fri night], 'I shall be able to tell you on Monday whether we can go on or whether we have to stop.'" (W p. 136). Later that morning a meeting at Hambros with Lidderdale, Revelstoke, Francis Baring, not conclusive.
- Conclusion was that Barings basically sound, needed time.
- Monday, Lidderdale ascertained BoE reserves £10.815mn, inadequate. Lidderdale met with Chancellor of the Exchequer Goschen. Goschen helped influence Rothschilds to influence Banque de France to lend BoE £3.5mn in gold. (W pp. 137-138)
- Wednesday Baring Brothers presented statement (W. p. 138):
- Liabilities £21mn, of which £16mn were acceptances
- Assets worth £24.75mn, but £5.75mn Argentine loans and £2mn Uruguay which "at the moment' could not be realized." I.e. illiquid securities made up £7.75mn, and maybe they would have been worth less if sold.
- Friday afternoon "Barings' bills began to pour in at the Bank of England" (W. p. 139). Lidderdale obtained guarantee from PM "that the British government would bear 'half of any loss that might result between two P. M. Friday and two P. M. Saturday." (W. p 140)
- By Friday, idea of a private collective guarantee took form. (W. pp. 141-142)
- By 11am Sat Lidderdale had £6.5mn, enough to free the Treasury of its liability.
- By 4pm up to £14mn. Eventually, £17.5mn (given as £17,326,000 on p. 144).
- "It was decided among all guarantors that they would help Barings to discharge at maturity its liabilities existing on the night of November 15,1890 [Saturday], and to make good any loss that might appear at the final liquidation. As security the Bank of England took over from Barings 'all the Bonds and Documents representing value.'" (W. p 142)
- "Baring Brothers was reconstructed as a limited company with a capital of one million pounds, and was henceforth known as Baring Brothers & Co., Ltd." (W pp. 143-144)
- Discharge of obligations: "It proved impossible to wind up everything within three years – the time for which the guarantors had undertaken their guarantee – but it became apparent that within another year all commitments would be met... By 1894 Barings had paid o all obligations." (W p. 144) In October 1894 BoE issued circular to all guarantors notifying o cial ending.
Bottom line - guarantors took over the risk, taking collateral, and restructuring Barings in the process. It's not clear from this description what compensation the guarantors got.
# From Wikipedia:
The Panic of 1890 was an acute depression that was less serious than other panics of the era precipitated by the near insolvency of the Baring Brothers bank in London due mainly to poor investments in Argentina. The Bank of England bailed out the Baring Brothers which prevented a larger depression.[citation needed] Nathan Rothschild remarked that if this had not happened, perhaps the entire private banking system of London would have collapsed which would have made for a tremendous economic catastrophe. The panic was associated with call money reaching an astonishing 45 percent and a slump in the commodity market the world over.
The Baring Crisis is the nineteenth century ŠoS most famous sovereign debt crisis. Few studies, however, have attempted to understand the extent to which the crisis mattered for countries other than Argentina and England. Using a new database consisting of more than 15,000 observations of weekly sovereign debt prices, we first assess the extent to which the Barings Crisis a ected other emerging-market borrowers. Using event studies, we find empirical evidence of a regional crisis. Following the onset of the crisis in 1890, sovereign yield spreads for Central and South American borrowers increased by nearly 800 basis points in the year following the crisis and by nearly 1,400 basis points in the five years after the crisis, while interest-rate spreads in other emerging-market borrowers were generally flat. We next consider whether the regional crisis in Central and South American identified with time series data can be explained by country-specific information that was available to investors in the nineteenth century. Using a panel data set of 28 countries for the period 1886-96, we find Latin American countries saw their yield spreads during the crisis rise by between 150-470 basis points even after controlling for macroeconomic, trade, political-institutional factors, or other country-specific ects. We speculate that European investors may have experienced a wake-up call and sold or reduced their holdings of Latin American securities in the wake of the Baring Crisis.

Apparently the philosophy of conservatism... were temporarily abandoned. Even among the firm's partners there was no agreement about Lord Revelstoke's methods." (Wp. 134)
# 4 Calomiris & Haber: Framework
# 4.1 Introduction - Banking & Property Rights
# Contents
# "Property Rights" & "Political Economy"
Property Rights; Political Economy; Law & Economics; Public Choice
- Applying Price Theory (incentives, budget constraints, preferences) to large social questions
Chicago has a long tradition (Law & Economics)
Stigler (& Peltzman): regulatory capture interest groups and other political participants will use the regulatory and coercive powers of government to shape laws and regulations in a way that is beneficial to them.
- Coase: Externalities & Coase Theorem: with no transaction costs who owns property rights has no e ect on the economic ciency.
- Property rights matter for distribution of income but not e ciency
- Transaction costs, property rights matter to efficiency
For this (C&H on banking, A&R on nations) look outside Chicago
Property Rights; Political Economy; Law & Economics; Public Choice
I. This is a set of related terms & concepts, around the idea of applying Price Theory (incentives, budget constraints, preferences) to large social questions
II. Chicago has a long tradition in this, although interestingly with this specific area (Calomiris & Haber on Banking & Finance; Acemoglu & Robinson on di erential growth of nations) the interesting work has been done outside of Chicago. (Although now, with Robinson here, some of the important work is again being done here at Chicago)
(A) Aaron Director - came to Chicago Law School in 1946, trained "generations of law students and even his colleagues on the faculty in this then-new way of thinking about the law" (UofC press release on Director's death - http://www-news.uchicago.edu/releases/04/040913director.shtml)
Law and Economics as a field attempts to apply the scientific methods of economics—including statistics and price theory—to behaviors that in the past had been analyzed solely by appeal to the history and intuitions of the law. With coherent theory, precise hypotheses and a willingness to subject those hypotheses to empirical tests, it has transformed legal thinking in the United States and in many nations around the world. (UofC press release on Director's death - http://www.news.uchicago.edu/releases/04/040913.director.shtml)
1. Aaron Director also has the distinction of helping to bring Milton Friedman to Chicago (see paper by David Mitch in October 2016 JPE - Director was not in the economics department but was an ally of Knight and some of his students - Simons, Mints), and of being Friedman's brother-in-law (Rose Director sat next to Friedman in economics and they eventually married)
(B) George Stigler - Regulatory Capture
2. "Stigler is best known for developing the Economic Theory of Regulation, also known as capture, which says that interest groups and other political participants will use the regulatory and coercive powers of government to shape laws and regulations in a way that is beneficial to them."
3. Ideas that are crucial for Calomiris & Haber's argument, but they actually do not cite Stigler very extensively
4. Another important name for government regulation: Sam Peltzman (business school - noted for his distinctive sartorial style)
(C) Ronald Coase - Externalities & the "Coase Theorem"
5. The idea of externalities – that costs (or benefits) from an activity may be born by people who do not choose the activity – is central to understanding many issues in public policy, pollution, and many others aspects of economics.
6. Coase had the central insight – an insight that had escaped earlier economists – that externalities should disappear in a world of perfect information and no transactions costs. In such a world agents can negotiate side-payments and return to equilibrium.
7. This is not to say that we actually live in such a world, but it forces us to think about why externalities matter – not because of the externalities per se but because of the transactions costs or informational asymmetries
8. Coase Theorem says that in the absence of transaction costs the assignment of property rights has no effect on the economically e cient outcome. It will obviously have equity e ects.
9. It also forces us to think carefully about property rights, and is closely connected with the area of Law and Economics that has been important at Chicago since at least the 1940s.
10. "The Problem of Social Cost" published in the Journal of Law and Economics,
11. Apparently "the most-referenced article in both legal and economics scholarship." (http://www.law.uchicago.edu/alumni/magazine/fall11/lawandecon-history - also has the story of dinner at Director's house after the workshop where the group went from 20-1 against the Coase theorem to all in favor)
12. Example of cattle rancher & farmer (from paper, this quote from Wikipedia):
Coase argued that without transaction costs the initial assignment of property rights makes no difference to whether or not the farmer and rancher can achieve the economically efficient outcome. If the cost of restraining cattle by, say, building a fence, is less than the cost of crop damage, the fence will be built. The initial assignment of property rights determines who builds the fence. If the farmer is responsible for the crop damage, the farmer will pay for the fence (as long the fence costs less than the crop damage). If the rancher is responsible for the crop damage, the rancher will build the fence. The allocation of property rights is primarily an equity issue, with consequences for the distribution of income and wealth, rather than an efficiency issue.
# 4.2 Three Observations About Banking
# Calomiris & Haber Framework
Start with Three Observations about Banking:
I. Banking & financial institutions important for economic growth
II. Big differences across countries
(A) Stability of banking systems
(B) E ciency of banking services
III. Although highly regulated, outcomes often bad
How & why some country's banking systems have repeated crises
Banking structure & rules - result of implicit political bargains
# (1) Banking Important For Growth
Holland, Britain, US - financial revolutions before rise as economic superpowers (18th c, 19th c, 20th c).
We studied France, England, US
- 1720, early in Britain's financial revolution - but not France.
- Britain vs France in 1788
- France Debt%GDP about 1/3rd but debt service 1.5x larger
- Britain more debt - lenders believed they would pay back
- Britain lower interest - lenders believed Britain was less risk
US grew faster than Britain early 1800s (after 1790-92 US revolution)
The financial positions of France and Britain in 1788
(Macdonald p 241)

# Banking Important For Growth

Under-appreciated: innovations in standards, process.

Holland, Britain (18th c): Perpetual annuity

- Renaissance Italy: development of accounting
Contemporary economies: financial development faster rates of physical capital accumulation, more jobs, social mobility
# Real Transactions Requires

(Many) Financial Transactions If Financial System Fails, Economy Collapses

Example - I am buying a new bicycle

January: I order, pay $25\%$

Credit card payments processing, bank credit

January: store orders from manufacturer, pays $35\%$

Borrows from bank for extra $10\%$

- February: manufacturer builds
- Must pay workers, buy material. Borrows working capital
(bank) - March: manufacturer ships to store, store pays
- International payments; Store borrows (65% until I pay)
- Even such a simple transaction - buying a bicycle - requires complex & interconnected system
- We never think of those many financial steps
- But if any piece brakes, the real economy stops
- Money & Banks more important than we usually recognize
- April: I pick up bike, pay balance
Credit card, payments processing, bank credit
![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-15/c441f5b5-2f9d-4e4d-bc65-1301c02e96db/a3265b48e8f7aa3d7454c30906097a6e70bcb197460648cf92bee5a09e4a6989.jpg)
January: I pay by credit card, bank provides credit. April: I pay by credit card
January: Store borrows from bank to pay extra $10 \%$ borrows (until I pay)
February: manufacturer borrows
(WORKING CAPITAL) to pay workercnt.
WORKERS, etc.
# Calomiris & Haber Framework
# I. Three Basic Observations

(A) Banking & financial institutions important for economic growth

(B) Big differences across countries

- Stability of banking systems
E ciency of banking services
(C) Although highly regulated, outcomes often bad
# Ii. Banking & Economic Growth

(A) Economic historians have shown that Holland, Britain, US had financial revolutions before their rise as economic superpowers (Holland 18th c, Britain 19th c, US 20th c).

(B) We spent time on France & England in 1720, which was the early part of Britain's financial revolution – but not France. Consider Britain vs France in 1788 – slide with data from "Free Nation Deep in Debt" by James Macdonald (p 241) comparing France & England.

1. France had Debt%GDP about 1/3rd of England but debt service that was about 1.5x larger. Rate for France about $6.7\%$, for England about $3.3\%$.
2. It may not seem important, but I think it is - England's liquid, deep, standardized market (based on a single perpetual annuity issue, Consols) versus France's illiquid and complex market (based on combination of life annuities, lotteries, perpetual annuities).
3. We too often under appreciate innovations in things like standards, process. The perpetual annuity, that was standardized, transferrable, easy to calculate, probably contributed to this.
4. Saw this in Renaissance Italy with the development of accounting. A big impact from what we now think of just a process improvement.
(C) Looking at contemporary economies and using statistical methods people find the same answer: higher levels of financial development are associated with (and appear to be causally associated with) faster rates of physical capital accumulation, better job, mobility.

If such catastrophes were random events, all countries would su er them with equal frequency…. Some countries appear immune to the disease, while others are unusually susceptible. (p 5)

[only] three of the 21 crisis-prone countries [are developed]…. being crisis prone is connected to other undesirable traits and outcomes.

```

Chance of crisis each year $= 2.9\%$

# I. Divergence Across Countries In Stability Of Banking Systems

(A) Financial Stability (or lack): C&H argue that the pattern across countries is non-random ([Calomiris and Haber(2015)] 5&6), with some countries having crises and others not. They 1) comparing across 117 countries and 2) compare US & Canada (claiming 12 crises since 1840 in US, none in Canada)

1.117 countries 1970-2010 (41 years). 34 (29%) had no crisis. 83 (71%) had $1+$. US had 2 (1984-1991,2008). C&H claim this shows crises are non-random and that some countries are direent from others: "If such catastrophes were random events, all countries would suffer them with equal frequency." "Some countries appear immune to the disease, while others are unusually susceptible." (p 5) This sounds convincing but is wrong. We need to be careful in thinking probabilistically, and this is an example where failing to think carefully can mislead us. Say all 117 countries were independent, and each country had $2.9\%$ chance of a crisis each year (independent across years), then the predicted or expected number of countries with 0, $1+$ events would look like:

```latex
\begin{document}
\begin{tabular}{|c|c|c|c|c|c|c|}
\hline
 & 0 & 1+ & & 0 & 1 & 2+ \\ \hline actual count & 34 & 83 & actual count & 34 & 62 & 21 \\ \hline actual % & 29% & 71% & actual % & 29% & 53% & 18% \\ \hline pred % for p=2.9%/yr & 30% & 70% & pred % for p=2.9%/yr & 30% & 37% & 33% \\ \hline
\end{tabular}
\end{document}
```

This is a binomial process so the probability of 0 events is $\mathsf{P}[0 \text{ drawsoutof } 41 \mid \mathsf{p} \text{ eachdraw} = 0.029] = \binom{n}{k} p^k (1 - p)^{n - k} = \binom{41}{0} p^0 (1 - p)^{41}$. Here countries having no crisis are lucky; they are not fundamentally di erent. Observed di erences result purely from random fluctuations.

2. I happen to believe countries are di erent. But the evidence to support this is more subtle than simply looking at the comparison across 117 countries that C&H present. They emphasize the small number of countries with no crises $(29\%)$ and the large number with $2+$ crises ("18 percent of the countries in the world appear to have been preternaturally crisis prone.") But the simple binomial model (all countries independent) predicts a small number of countries with no crises, and an even larger number with $2+$ crises: $32\%$ (instead of $18\%$ ).

```latex
\begin{document}
\begin{tabular}{|c|c|c|c|c|c|}
\hline
 & 0 & 1 & 2 & 3 & 4 \\ \hline actual count & 34 & 62 & 18 & 1 & 1 \\ \hline actual % & 29% & 53% & 16% & 1% & 1% \\ \hline pred % for p=2.2%/yr & 30% & 37% & 22% & 8% & 3% \\ \hline
\end{tabular}
\end{document}
```

We could argue that these data show too few repeaters, not too many no-crisis countries.

 i. Here are formal statistical tests on the data.

 ii. On the two-outcome table (0 crises vs 1+ crises):

	- chi-sq test for obs=(34,83) vs p=(0.30,0.70).

	- The probabilities are the binomial probabilities (see table above) and they imply (for 117 total) counts of (35.1,81.9)

	- R-code: chisq.test(c(34,83), p=c(0.3,0.7)): chi-sq=0.0492, df=1, p-value=0.8244.

	- A very high p-value (0.8244) means that such an outcome is very likely and we cannot reject the observed data being produced from a simple binomial

	- This is obvious - the actual are almost exactly the same as probabilities. So test should have a very low chi-sq statistic and p-value far away from $1\%$ or $5\%$.

	- A p-value of $1\%$ would imply that the observations are unlikely to have been produced by the probabilities (0.3,0.7)

 iii. For three-outcome table $(0,1,2+$ crises):

	- chi-sq test for obs=(34,62,21) vs p=(0.3,0.37,0.33).

Predicted counts (35.1,43.3,38.6)

	- R-code: chisq.test(c(34,62,21), p=c(0.3,0.37,0.33)): chi-sq=16.1529, df=2, p-value=0.0003.

	- The data clearly reject simple binomial. But actually the problem is observing too few single crises, not too many $2+$ crises.

 iv. For five-outcome table $(0,1,2,3,4+$ crises):

	- chi-sq test for obs=(34,62,18,1,1) vs p=(0.3,0.37,0.22,0.08,0.03).

Predicted counts (35.1,43.3,25.7,9.4,3.5)

	- R-code: chisq.test(c(34,62,18,1,1), p=c(0.3,0.37,0.22,0.08,0.03)): chi-sq=19.87, df=4, p-value=0.0005.3. There are two strands of better evidence in favor of differences across countries. First, Looking at which countries are serial crisis countries the list [of crisis-prone countries] includes Chad, the Central African Republic, Cameroon, Kenya, Nigeria, the Philippines roughly one-third [of the 117 countries] are categorized… as high income nations. But only three of the 21 crisis-prone countries, 14 percent, are in this group. This suggests that, for the most part, being crisis prone is connected to other undesirable traits and outcomes. But that raises another troubling question. Why is the United States on this list?" [Calomiris and Haber(2015)] p 7

Serial crisis countries appear to be largely politically unstable countries. This is an indication that banking crises are associated with political structure (the major thesis of Calomiris & Haber)

But this actually doesn't help too much either. If we do a test of the 2x2 contingency table:

```latex
\begin{document}
\begin{tabular}{|c|c|c|c|}
\hline
 & 1 & 2+ & Total \\ \hline
Developed & 29 & 3 & 32 \\ \hline
Not-Develop & 67 & 18 & 85 \\ \hline
Total & 96 & 21 & 117 \\ \hline
\end{tabular}
\end{document}
``` we cannot reject that this contingency table results from random fluctuations: given the row sums (developed vs non-developed) and column sums (number of crises) the cell entries are not unusual enough to reject random variation from an independent sample.
    - We know the column entry for $2+$ (3 developed countries (Spain, Sweden, US) and 21 total)
    - We don't know the total number of developed. But I got the spread-sheet from the World Bank Financial Structure Database (FinancialStructureandDevelopmentDataset.xlsx) and counted the number of HighIncome OECD countries as 32 (27% of 117, so close to C&H's "roughly one-third"). The rest of the entries follow from that. (Entries that I have inferred are in italic.
    - Pearson's Chi-squared test with Yates's continuity correct is: Chi-sq = 1.4702, df=1, p-value = 0.225. I.e. the chi-squared is not large and therefore the data are actually not far from independent - we cannot reject independence.
    - Fisher's exact test has a p-value of 0.1808, so same answer.
    - If we calculate the expected cell counts we can see why we cannot reject independence: the low number of Developed $2+$ crisis (3) is just not low enough. The expected number is pretty low (5.7) and the observed (3) is simply not low enough to reject the possibility that it is simply random variation.
    - We calculate the row and column percentages and then the expected cell proportions from pi.*p.j. The predicted cell proportions, given the row and column proportions, are not different enough from the actual.
```latex
\begin{document}
\begin{tabular}{|c|c|c|c|}
\hline
Expected counts & 1 & 2+ & Total \\ \hline
Developed & 26.2 & 5.7 & 32 \\ \hline
Not-Develop & 69.7 & 15.2 & 85 \\ \hline
Total & 96 & 21 & 117 \\ \hline
\end{tabular}
\end{document}
```

```latex
\begin{document}
\begin{tabular}{|c|c|c|c|c|c|c|c|}
\hline
Actual proportions & 1 & 2+ & Total & Predicted proportions & 1 & 2+ & Total \\ \hline
Developed & 24.8% & 2.6% & 27.4% & Developed & 22.4% & 4.9% & \\ \hline
Not-Develop & 57.3% & 15.4% & 72.6% & Not-Develop & 59.6% & 13.0% & \\ \hline
Total & 82.1% & 17.9% & & Total & & & \\ \hline
\end{tabular}
\end{document}
```

# 4.3 Digression On Thinking Probabilistically
# Contents
# Birthday Problem

A few minutes to discuss probability and thinking carefully Humans are not, apparently, wired to think naturally about probability Birthday Problem

- You enter a room with 20 people, so you are the 21st.
- What is probability two people (out of the 21) have birthday same day of the year?
- Natural to think: low, since there are 365 days to choose from
Actual: high, 44.4%
Here's what I think is the mistake:
Natural: same birthday as me: something like $21 / 365 = 6\%$
- Need to think: how many possible pairs? Many pairs, so likely: $44.4\%$
# Thinking Probabilistically

Not easy, and something we need training in

A whole field built up around this - behavioral economics - pointing out that humans make mistakes

- Kahneman & Tversky - Kahneman 2002 Nobel, author of Thinking Fast and Slow
But many things are hard – quantum mechanics, relativity for example – but we use it every day (fiber optics, GPS)
Think carefully about probability (and economics)
Good book by Gigerenzer - Calculated Risks
# [Gigerenzer(2002)]
# Cancer Testing

Real-life problem - testing for cancer (prostate or breast)

Test is $99\%$ reliable - 1 chance out of 100 of false positive

- Doctor tells you test positive
What is the chance you have cancer?
Natural to think $99\%$, but that is completely and totally wrong
Think this way (simple way to apply Bayes rule)
- If 1,000 people take the test, how many people show up with positive?
10 (1%) from false positive - no cancer
- How many truly with cancer? Maybe 1 out of 1,000 (cancer rare)
- So 11 have positive test, only $1 / 11$ (9%) chance you have cancer
# These Things Matter
# Monty Hall Problem

Game show - Let's Make a Deal

- Three doors, 1 car & 2 goats
- You choose door 1, Monty opens one of the other doors to show a goat
- Do you stay or do you switch?
- Stay: 1/3 chance of winning car (same as no door opening)
- Switch: 2/3 chance of winning car
# Much Debate – Books Written About This – But Solution Is Right

I. Monty Hall problem & solution depends on the way Monty Hall opens the door, the rules that he follows. Here, assume Monty Hall follows these rules:

(A) Never open the door the contestant has chosen

(B) If the car is behind the contestant's door (so that the two non chosen doors have goats), randomly choose which goat door to open

(C) If the car is behind one of the two non chosen doors (so only one non chosen door has a goat), open that goat door

II. See my CFA book A Practical Guide to Risk Management, p 25, and also Rosenhouse (2009) The Monty Hall Problem.

(A) This came to prominence with Marilyn vos Savant (1990 in Parade magazine and 1996 book)

III. There has been a huge amount of controversy and debate about this, but take my word that this is the correct solution (given the rules stated).

# Probability Model:
- Assume two countries (Canad & US) the same, each with probability $2.9\%/\mathrm{yr}$ of a crisis
- P[0 crises in 171 years | p/yr = 2.9%] = PDF[BinomialDistribution[171,0.029], 0] = 0.0067
- P[5+ crises in 171 year | p/yr = 2.9%] = 1-CDF[BinomialDistribution[171,0.029], 4] = 0.550
Actually, $p / y_r = 2.9\%$ is too low for US because this predicts mean of 5.0 crises (mean $= p^* y_r s = 0.029^* 171$ ). $P[10 + ] = 0.027$. Need higher probability, something like $5\%$
- P[0 crises in 171 years | p/yr = 5%] = PDF[BinomialDistribution[171,0.05], 0] = 0.00016
- P[10+ crises in 171 year | p/yr = 5%] = 1-CDF[BinomialDistribution[171,0.05], 9] = 0.345
# Us Vs Canada, 1840-2010

Why this big difference?

- Both countries, particularly in the 19th c, were agrarian, experiencing westward expansion, sparse population
- Yet the outcomes were so di erent. Why?
Calomiris & Haber argue di erence in political institutions
US gave control over banks ("charters") to states
- A coalition of interested parties – agrarian populists & small bankers – captured control in early 1800s
Best explanation I've seen yet for
- Pattern of repeated crises: US since early 1800s, Britain 18thc & 19thc
- Stark differences across countries
I. Second, looking at much longer time spans – in this case the US versus Canada. Calomiris & Haber claim that the US is crisis-prone and Canada not. I find this much more convincing. They look at 1837-2008
(A) US: 14 crises: 1837-39,1857,1861,1873,1884,1890,1893,1896,1907,1920s, 1930-33,1980s, 2007-09
(B) Reinhart & Rogo count 9, not all the same: 1836-38,1857,1873,1884,1890 (but I think they mean 1893), 1907,1929-1933,1984-91,2007-09
(C) C&H say Canada: 2: 1837 &
1839. No severe runs or financial crises since
(D) Reinhart & Rogo count 1 (see table A-3 not A-4). Remember from Lecture 6 that Reinhart & Rogo define two types of banking crises: Type I
(severe) are bank runs & failures. Type II
(mild) are stress, closure or takeover of a large bank. Canada had many of Type II (8) but only 1 of Type I.
(E) Why this big di erence? Both countries, particularly in the 19th c, were experiencing westward expansion, sparse population, agrarian. And yet the outcomes were so di erent. Why? I think Calomiris & Haber provide the explanation

# 5 Property Rights & Implicit Political Bargains
# Contents
# 5.1 Fundamental Idea: Groups Following Interests
# Fundamental Idea: Implicit Bargains

Think carefully about the economic incentives and goals of di erent actors

- Politicians, bankers, taxpayers, shareholders, taxpayers, voters
Move away from idea that public o cials are perfectly rational and always doing the right thing.
- Not necessarily corrupt, just fallible and responding to incentives our goal… is not to blame…. People pursue their self-interest, and one of the ways they do that is by exercising their political rights. When they are able to form a political coalition in the pursuit of mutual advantage, they will do so. (p 213)
 deals are intended to improve the welfare of the members of those coalitions and of the group in control of the government, not of society at large…. This situation implies that many parties may be excluded from beneficial deals. (p 38)
Importance of looking across countries and at history
I. Calomiris & Haber fundamental idea: Implicit contracts or bargains between groups (politicians, bankers, taxpayers, shareholders)
(A) pushes us to think carefully about the economic incentives and goals of di erent actors. An application of micro-economics.
(B) Move away from idea that public o cials are perfectly rational and always doing the right thing.
1. Not necessarily corrupt, just fallible and responding to incentives: C&H state "Our goal in examining [the 2008 crisis] is not to blame"
(C) pp 17-20 provides a summary of the narrative for the US. p 20: "our focus is on the institutions that shape the incentives of individuals and groups, not the individuals and groups per se. People everywhere generally pursue their self-interest."
(D) This is only a partial story - banks, not all financial markets (see p 23 )
(E) They argue for the importance of looking at a long history for understanding complex systems (p. 25 )
 banks' strengths and shortcomings are the predictable consequences of political bargains and that those bargains are structured by a society's fundamental political institutions. (p x)
What this is not: Blame Game
Lest we be misunderstood, our goal in this chapter and the next is not to blame large banks, the GSEs, the urban poor, activist groups, politicians, or regulators for the subprime crisis. People everywhere generally pursue their self-interest, and one of the ways they do that is by exercising their political rights. When they are able to form a political coalition in the pursuit of mutual advantage, they will do so…. No individual or group of individuals is to 'blame' for what happens as a result of that coalition. Indeed, placing blame is an enterprise that is inherently unproductive because it trivializes the problem: it distracts us into thinking that our problems are caused by the moral flaws of other people rather than focusing on how our political institutions encourage socially costly coalitions. ([Calomiris and Haber(2015)] p 213)
 deals are intended to improve the welfare of the members of those coalitions and of the group in control of the government, not of society at large. Because the economic incentives of the bank insiders, minority shareholders, depositors, debtors, and taxpayers are not inherently aligned, the group in control of the government has ample reason to exploit those di erences in incentives for its own political or economic ends. In particular, participants in the bargaining know that unanimity is not required for a deal to be made: the government simply has to cobble together a winning coalition. This situation implies that many parties may be excluded from beneficial deals. ([Calomiris and Haber(2015)] p 38)
# 5.2 Unavoidable Risks In Banking
# Contents
# What Does A Bank Produce?

Output: debt contracts - IOUs payable to bank - loans

Input: also contracts - IOUs to depositors (checking account = loan)

Inherently unstable - subject to runs, crises of confidence, liquidity crises.

Walter Bagehot, "father of central banking", understood both the power and the risks of banking

A million [pounds] in the hands of a single banker is a great power… But the same sum scattered in tens and fifties through a whole nation is no power at all (Lombard Street 1873)

But in exact proportion to the power of this system is its delicacy I should hardly say too much if I said its danger. Only our familiarity blinds us to the marvelous nature of the system…. If any large fraction of that money really was demanded, our banking system and our industrial system too would be in great danger. (p 11)

# Digression On Liquidity Crises

An recurrent part of banking crises

What Henry Paulson covers in his book

A panic seized upon the public, such as had never been witnessed before: everybody begging for money - money - but money was hardly on any condition to be had. (London, 1825)

 terror reigned in the City, the banks were besieged, consols went down to 84 (London 1866)

Everyone wanted his money back. Burned by foolish speculation in Russia, investors were rejecting risk in any guise, even reasonable risk (New York, 1998)

# Fundamental Issue: Inherently Unstable

Two unavoidable risks - puts depositors at risk

- Credit
(solvency) Risk: borrowers may default
- Liquidity Risk: timing mismatch, can push to insolvency
Natural solution: bu ers and cushions:
- Cash on asset side, equity capital on liability side
Equity capital:
- Owners & shareholders put money in to get profit.
- But at risk of loss
# Managing Solvency & Liquidity Risks - Bu Ers

Two unavoidable risks in banking: Credit

(Solvency) & Liquidity

- Credit
(solvency) risk: loans or other business goes bad
- Liquidity risk: depositors (or other lenders) want money back now
Protection against both: Capital & Liquidity Bu ers
(Reserves) - Solvency problem: Assets $<$ Liabilities
- Liquidity problem: Not enough liquid assets to pay depositors who ask today
Consider these two banks - LEFT: Safe, RIGHT: Not Safe
FIRST bad solvency news (loans fall by $7\%$ )
Solvency - loans down $7\%$, can bank still pay depositors?
- Left: lots of equity, lots of cash - solvent, assets > liabilities
- Assets don't go down too much (more reserves, safer assets)
Equity enough to absorb losses - owners lose but depositors OK
- Right: less equity, less cash - insolvent, assets < liabilities
- Assets go down by more since assets more risky
Not enough equity - depositors lose
NEXT bad liquidity news (depositors want \$12)
Liquidity - depositors want \$12 back, can bank pay all depositors?
- Left: plenty of cash to pay depositors immediately - solvent, liquid
- Bank pays the \$12 out of cash reserves
- Right: not enough cash - solvent but illiquid
- Bank has enough assets (assets > liabilities) but not the right kind of assets
 - Depositors want \$12, bank can pay only \$10
-BANK RUN
High capital & liquidity bu ers
Low capital & liquidity bu ers
(insolvent)
(run) ```latex

```
```latex
\begin{document}
\begin{tabular}{|c|c|c|}
\hline
BILITIES & ASSETS & \\ \hline
Deposits & Loans & $ \\ \hline
Equity & Cash & $ \\ \hline
Total & Total & $ \\ \hline
Solvent & Depositor & \\ \hline
Deposits & Loans & $ \\ \hline
Equity & Cash & $0 \\ \hline
Total & Total & $ \\ \hline
\end{tabular}
\end{document}

# Equity Capital

Raise capital as part of business - bu er Fundamental conflict:

- Bank insiders need to bring in outsiders
(investors) - Outsiders need to ensure insiders don't steal (wild bets, embezzle)
- Issue of principle / agent or cash-flow rights versus control rights
Mechanisms to protect outside shareholders
- Partnership: insiders put wealth at risk - limits size
- Legal structures against fraud and embezzlement
- Limited liability - equity investors cannot lose more than they put in
- Ilc common now - early banks partnerships, 19thc 2x or 3x liability
Banks earliest limited liability companies
# Government Power & Value Of "Bank Charter"

Government power critical for banking

- Laws to control fraud, enforce Ilc
- Various rights - eg to issue notes or money
- Obligations - eg pay taxes, be supervised
These laws, rights, obligations are the "Bank Charter" that C&H talk about Valuable – Source of "Economic Rents" or Property Rights
- Incentive for the government and insiders to limit charters
- Government can extract rents from the bank
- Insiders (and shareholders) can extract monopoly or scarcity rents from customers
I. Start with what a bank produces
(A) Output: debt contracts - IOUs payable to bank - loans made to businesses and individuals
(B) Input: also contracts - IOUs to depositors. (Remember - when you put money into a checking account your are lending to the bank)
(C) Banking is inherently unstable, even under the best of circumstances subject to runs, crises of confidence, liquidity crises.
II. Digression on Walter Bagehot, the "father of central banking". He wrote Lombard St. in 1873.
(A) On the power of a bank, that aggregates savings:
A million [pounds] in the hands of a single banker is a great power… But the same sum scattered in tens and fifties through a whole nation is no power at all (p 2 or 3)
(B) Bagehot again:
But in exact proportion to the power of this system is its delicacy I should hardly say too much if I said its danger. Only our familiarity blinds us to the marvelous nature of the system. There never was so much borrowed money collected in the world as is now collected in London. Of the many millions in Lombard street, infinitely the greater proportion is held by bankers or others on short notice or on demand; that is to say, the owners could ask for it all any day they please: in a panic some of them do ask for some of it. If any large fraction of that money really was demanded, our banking system and our industrial system too would be in great danger. (p 11)
III. Digression on Liquidity crisis:
(A) This is what Henry Paulson describes in the chapters I'm having you read from his book. His description of being in the middle of a liquidity crisis or panic
(B) Some quotes
"A panic seized upon the public, such as had never been witnessed before: everybody begging for money - money - but money was hardly on any condition to be had."
Referring to the London panic of 1825, reference given in fn 57 of Kindleberger as "Thomas Joplin, 'Case for Parliamentary Inquiry into the Circumstances of the Panic, in a letter to Thomas Gisbourne, Esq. M. P.' (London: F. Rigeway & Sons, n.d. [after 1832]), pp0.14-15."
"On May 10,1866, the great London discount house of Overend, Gurney & Co. had stopped payments; its liabilities were almost nineteen million pounds. The following day, England's Black Friday, 'terror reigned in the City, the banks were besieged, consols went down to 84,' reports Sir John Clapham, historian of the Bank of England." Wechsberg p. 137) Kindleberger says preceding speculation was in Cotton, shipping in general.
Everyone wanted his money back. Burned by foolish speculation in Russia, investors were rejecting risk in any guise, even reasonable risk (Lowenstein, "When Genius Failed" p 153)
IV. Fundamental issue with banking: inherently unstable (subject to runs, crises of confidence, liquidity crises).
(A) Two unavoidable risks
1. Credit
(solvency) Risk: borrowers may default
2. Liquidity Risk: timing mismatch
(B) This put depositors at risk
(C) Natural solution: bu ers and cushions: some cash on asset side, some equity capital on liability side
3. Slides on liquidity vs credit
(solvency) risk
(D) Equity capital: owners & shareholders, who put money in to get profit. But at risk of loss
V. Equity Capital: Banks need to raise capital to function (as part of the two bu ers - liquidity & capital)
(A) but this raises a fundamental conflict:
4. Bank insiders (initial owners and managers) need to bring in outsiders
5. Outsiders need some way to ensure insiders don't steal the equity capital (and deposits) by taking wild bets or embezzling
6. Issue of principle / agent or cash-flow rights (the right to receive profits and cash flows) versus control rights
(B) Mechanisms to protect outside shareholders
7. Make insiders put large wealth at risk in the business
8. Legal structures against fraud and embezzlement
9. Limited liability - so that equity investors cannot lose more than they put in i. We think limited liability is natural, but early banks were often partnerships ii. And in 19th c, often 2x or 3x liability iii. Banks were earliest limited liability companies
10. Limited liability is one of the keys. But this is granted by the government i. Only the government has the power to grant various rights (to issues notes or money; allow the bank to hold government or other deposits; limited liability) and obligations (eg pay taxes, be supervised) ii. These rights are the "Bank Charter" that C&H talk about
11. Bank Charters are valuable i. Incentive for the government and insiders to limit charters ii. Government can extract rents from the bank iii. Insiders (and shareholders) can extract monopoly or scarcity rents from customers
# 5.3 Three Property-Rights Challenges
# Contents
# Three Property-Rights Challenges

Temptations to expropriate

(steal) Need mechanisms to reduce risk of expropriation, or compensate for risk

Government expropriation

- Reduce: constrains government - better banking and higher growth
Compensate: bankers will extract rents from shareholders and customers - worse banking and lower growth
- Insider expropriation:
- minority shareholders and depositors need mechanisms to prevent insiders from stealing (fraud, tunneling)
- Borrower Expropriation:
- Through default, borrowers take from insiders, shareholders, depositors tunneling - channeling bank money towards businesses owned by insiders)
# 5.4 Conflicted Government
# Contents
# Government Conflicts

Government has a stake - cannot be "honest broker"

Regulates, but often wants profitable banks (taxes or kick-backs)

- Make charters scarce - valuable - generate revenue
- Maybe political pressure groups have influence, voting their interests
Nothing evil, just people and groups acting in their own best interest
Various source of
(potential) conflict
- Gov't taxes or borrows from banks, so incentive to make banks profitable (at expense of social welfare - stable & e cient banking)
Gov't enforces debt contracts, but debtors are voters, may punish gov't
- Gov't allocates losses (insiders, shareholders, depositors, borrowers)
- In a democracy voters are depositors and borrowers, may punish gov't
BAILOUTS: Incentives for gov't
(politicians) to bail out
- Depositors who are not careful (deposit insurance schemes)
- Borrowers who take on debt and try to walk away (think 2008)
# Political "Bargains"

Institutions, procedures, laws, rules, regulations result from political "bargains" between competing groups – politicians, bankers, investors, depositors, tax-payers, voters.

 deals are intended to improve the welfare of the members of those coalitions and of the group in control of the government, not of society at large. Because the economic incentives of the bank insiders, minority shareholders, depositors, debtors, and taxpayers are not inherently aligned, the group in control of the government has ample reason to exploit those differences in incentives for its own political or economic ends. In particular, participants in the bargaining know that unanimity is not required for a deal to be made: the government simply has to cobble together a winning coalition. This situation implies that many parties may be excluded from beneficial deals. (p 38)

# Bleak View

Bleak view of banking and finance

But note: not a "blame game"

Indeed, placing blame is an enterprise that is inherently unproductive because it trivializes the problem: it distracts us into thinking that our problems are caused by the moral flaws of other people rather than focusing on how our political institutions encourage socially costly coalitions. (p 213)

Nor hopeless - Chapter 15 talks about how to reform

Readers should not mistake our unvarnished appreciation for the realities and ironies of the political world, and the virtues of bank regulatory reform, as cynicism or hopelessness. (p 505)

# 6 Taxonomy Of Regimes & Banking
# Contents
# Autocracy

Response to risk of gov't expropriation: control or compensate

Autocracy: cannot control. Must compensate An establishment of this sort [Banque Generale] may be good in itself; but it is only so in a republic or a monarchy like England, whose finances are controlled by those alone who furnish them, and who only furnish as much as they please. But in a State which is weak, changeable, and absolute, like France, stability must necessarily be wanting to it; since the King, or in his name a mistress, a minister or favorites, or still more, such extreme necessities as we find in the years 1707-1710, may overthrow the Bank - the temptation to which would be too great, and at the same time too easy. Duc de Saint-Simon (contemporary - quoted in Macdonald)

Differences depending on degree of central control

- Strong: stable but narrow - share rents
(Mexico) - Weak: Inflation tax - Brazil
# Democracy

Gov't expropriation risk much reduced

Debtors have political voice

Inflation taxation rare (voters won't agree)

Distinction between "Liberal"

(Representative) vs Populist Democracy. Liberal or Representative: voters choose representatives, let them make decisions - Ancillary institutions (multicameral legislatures, political parties, independent judi ciary)

Populist: Rousseauian popular will - Voters are right and should not be opposed Referendums

(Brexit) # Outcomes Liberal

(Representative) Vs Populist

Populism tends to give power to the majority

In the liberal conception [of democracy], freedom is enjoyed by individuals and is defined by the absence of tyranny; in the populist conception, freedom means the ability to use government to implement the collective will of the people. (p 55) an ironic fact about popular sovereignty [populism]: democracies that least constrain the political power of majority rule tend to disadvantage the majority. (p 494)

Populism tends towards politically determined credit – bailouts, housing policy (lead-up to sub-prime crisis)

Liberal

(Representative) : Broad credit, stable - Canada

Populist: Broad credit, unstable - US

# 1.1 What Is Government Debt? Not Always Simple
# Contents
# What Is Government Debt? Not So Simple Explicit Debt

Central Government Debt

- T-Bills, Notes, Bonds
- US: \$14,176bn total (2014)
0-4yrs \$8,388bn or 59%
4-10yrs \$3,845bn or 27%
10+yrs \$1,943bn or 14%
- Local Government Debt (very important for US)
-Muni bonds, about $$ 3,900bn$ (2017)
# What Is Government Debt? Not So Simple Implicit Debt – Often Huge

Federal: e.g. Fannie & Freddie

- When taken over (2008) GSE obligations $\sim$ 5tn, vs $9.5tn public debt
- State & Local: e.g. pension liabilities
- Illinois 2nd-worst (NJ 1st),
Total liabilities (2016): $236bn bonds only $40bn; Unfunded pension & health $182bn
Comes to $50,400 / person
- We now turn from narrative to analysis & synthesis
- The South Sea Bubble, the Mississippi Scheme, and the US 1790 transformation are all about one of the biggest issues in modern macroeconomics: the proper role and the appropriate level of government debt
- We have a number of issues to discuss before turning to details about the South Sea, Mississippi, and US 1790
- Start by looking at some of the modern issues and data concerning government debt
- What is government debt? Not so simple a question
- Explicit central-government debt.
Government issues bonds
(borrows) and promises to pay back on an agreed schedule. This is a promise to pay back out of future tax and other revenue.
Classic purpose: For funding unusual and generally short-term expenditures. Two classic examples: wars and (more recently) social spending during recessions.
Wars obvious: large expenditures for army and armaments, in what is intended to be short-term
Recessions: things like unemployment-insurance payments go up during a recession
Maintaining smooth government revenues
(taxation) in the face of sharp increases or decreases in expenditure
Modern markets are both short-term (US T-Bills) and longer-term (US T-Notes and US T-Bonds)
T-Bills 3,6,12 months. Borrow $99 today, pay back $100 in 12 months (no add-on interest - embedded in the discount at sale).
 T-Notes & T-Bonds – conceptually the same but for some obscure reason di erent legal names. Sell for $100 today, promise
(say) 3% interest ($3 per year, $1.50 every half-year), then $100 at stated maturity. T-Notes 2yr, 3yr, 5yr, 10yr. T-Bonds 30-yr.
Distribution of marketable securities by current maturity (Sep 30,2017):
[1] S14,176bn total, from https://www.treasurydirect.gov/govt/reports/pd/feddebt/feddebt_ann2017.pdf.
0-4yrs $8,388bn or 59%
4-10yrs $3,845bn or 27%
10+yrss1,943bnor $14 \%$
T-Bonds used to be "callable" – after 20yrs the government had the option to redeem the bonds at $100. This meant that if interest rates were really high (and the prices were low) then the gov't would do nothing. BUT if rates were really low (current rates below the bond coupon so the bond would be valuable and prices would be high) the government could redeem and then re-fund itself at the new lower rates. US gov't doesn't do this anymore but they did 30 years ago and we will see that this option to redeem was vitally important and one of the central driving forces in the South Sea Bubble.
Most governments worldwide have the same structure
- Explicit state and local government debts
For US, the "Muni Bond Market".
States and cities borrow to finance both current expenditures and capital spending (things like roads and bridges).
Repay out of future tax and other revenue
For US S&L borrowing, have both "General Obligation" bonds which pay out of general taxes and have first priority, and special revenue bonds where the borrowing goes to a special purpose (say building a hospital or a toll road) and the repayment is out of revenue from that project.
Again, classic purpose is to maintain smooth revenue
(taxation) in face of non-smooth expenditures
# - Implicit Central Government Obligations Or Debt

Implicit obligations where money is not borrowed and spent explicitly but rather implicitly. When problems occur the government may be tempted or obligated to pay some or all of the obligations. Commonly referred to as "bailing out…"

The classic example for federal would be Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac. We will encounter them when we talk about the subprime crisis but we might as well be introduced now. These were (and are) government sponsored enterprises

(GSEs) which were supposed to be private corporations but with some government benefits. One of the benefits was the presumption that if they got into trouble the US government would bail them out. Politicians said this wouldn't happen but nobody believed them, and in 2008 the government did bail them out, guaranteeing their debt and in e etc making it an obligation of the US government and thus US taxpayers.

The onor o -balance sheet obligations of the two GSEs, which are "independent" corporations rather than federal agencies, are just over $5 trillion, a significant amount when compared to the $9.5 trillion of o cially reported United States public debt at the time of the takeover. From https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Federal_takeover_of_Far referencing Barr, Colin (2008-09-07). "Paulson readies the bazooka: Big buyers of Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac debt have been shying away. The Treasury secretary wants to coax them back". Fortune. Retrieved 2008-09-08. Seems to be at http://archive.fortune.com/2008/09/06/news/economy/fannie_freddie_paulson.fortune/index.htm?p

For China this is a really big issue. There are State Owned Enterprises

(SOEs) , state banks, "shadow banks" all of which have large but opaque or unknown obligations which the central government may feel obligated to make good on. [Weinland and Wildau(2016)]

# - Implicit Local Government Obligations Or Debt

For US state and local governments this is a huge issue

Shows up largely in "unfunded pension obligations": promises to pay retirement benefits to current or past state and local employees, but without putting money into the retirement fund today to cover future obligations.

Illinois is one of the states with the biggest problems (but only 2nd-worst - New Jersey is worst).

From https://www.statedatalab.org/state_data_and_comparisons/detail/illinois

Total state liabilities (2016): $236bn of which bonds: $40bn

 Other liabilities: $28bn j Minus debt related to capital assets: -$15bn

 Unfunded pension benefits: $138bn

 Unfunded retiree health care: $44bn

Per taxpayer $50,400

Also Retirement debt (2017) – Total $203bn From https://www.illinoispolicy.org/reports/203-billion-and-counting-total-debt-for-state-and-local-retirement-benefits-in-illinois/

In many respects this acts like explicit government debt except that it is not properly accounted for.

This has a direct connection with the US 1790 events. Part of the "grand bargain" of the 1789 Constitution was the federal government explicitly taking over state debts

(bailing out" the states). Later, by the 1830s, states had accumulated new debts, and this time the federal government refused to take over or assume the debts – states defaulted and investors lost. BUT this pushed many states to write "balanced budget" conditions into their state constitutions. One way to view the implicit (say pension) liabilities is a way politicians can work around those balanced budget provisions by taking on implicit obligations that are not explicitly accounted for as debt.

But the Congress's decision not to bail out the states [in the 1840s] had other, arguably more beneficial consequences for the country. A legacy of the Congress's decision was that in the 1840s more than half of the U. S. states rewrote their state constitutions to require year-by-year balanced budgets. This is yet another example of fiscal crises that have produced the lasting institutional changes that we sometimes call revolutions. ([Sargent(2011)] p 26)

# 1.2 Modern Us Treasury Market
# Current Bond Market: Us Treasuries

Three (really two): Bills, Notes, Bonds

# Treasury Bills
- Issued: 1 year or less (1mth, 3mth, 6mth, 1yr)
 - Discount bill – pay $98 today, get $100 in 1yr
# Notes And Bonds
- Technically, notes issued 2-10 yr maturity, bonds $10+$ (30) yrs
- No di erence except in name
- Pay $96 today, get coupon of $3 every year for the next 30 yrs
- Coupon paid every $s_{6}$ mths ($1.50 every 6 mths)
# Holdings Of Us Treasury Bonds

Issuance and holdings of US Treasuries (bills, notes, bonds) 2014

- Total
(marketable) $12.996 trn
- Federal Reserve held §2.5trn - "Quantitative Easing"
- Much held overseas

# 1.3 Bonds, Rents, And Consols
# Contents
# Modern Bonds

Modern gov't debt almost always bonds (fixed maturity)

18th c government debt was always annuities - Perpetuities

- Modern - as in picture
- Fixed coupon, fixed maturity date
- Simple, transparent, standardized
- "Yield" or discount rate compares bonds
- Easy with computers, harder in 1700s
![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-15/c441f5b5-2f9d-4e4d-bc65-1301c02e96db/b7c9b5a92767260a5c767ec6cfb9a69c69a24c39e6310407df182102b53a6631.jpg)
# Yield - How Much You Earn

Givesthe right answer (goes up)when either coupon up or price down

- Higher coupon: good
- Lower price: good
Yield: balances the two. Example of 10yr bond, $1.5\%$ coupon, price $100
![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-15/c441f5b5-2f9d-4e4d-bc65-1301c02e96db/83f967d14a0bfa60c7fad6f0d2896ab8c6e84c8870a62499d0e50854447b4d39.jpg)
![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-15/c441f5b5-2f9d-4e4d-bc65-1301c02e96db/364cfaed9f5d50fb32404663ea4f87a37f8281c749410840b12ec69bc64c77bd.jpg)
![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-15/c441f5b5-2f9d-4e4d-bc65-1301c02e96db/3feadb06c0a87975ab141d1d507bdeba88602c478b6c24cc02b56d3bf5754d2e.jpg)
Yield up (you earn more) when coupon up or price down
Coupon to $2.50 or Price to $91.25 yield to 2.50%
Of course, when you already own bond higher yield not good
# Rentes & British Perpetuals

(Consols) French perpetual annuities, British Redeemable Perpetual Annuities

Most gov't debt at this time perpetual annuities

- Fixed coupon (per year)
- Never pay back principal
- Simple Valuation:
$$
\begin{array}{l} - \operatorname{PV} (\mathbf {S x} \text{annuity} @ \mathbf {y} \%) = \mathbf {x} / \mathbf {y} \\ - \mathrm{PV} (\S 5 @ 6 \%) = 5 / 0.06 = \$ 83.30 \\ \end{array}
$$

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-15/c441f5b5-2f9d-4e4d-bc65-1301c02e96db/a281924b6f3c774029c759dcf6346f89ea5ac683189897d8dbd097722772f0e4.jpg)

discount at y%

Let's see how valuation works:

1710: "interest on majority of rents issued since 1702 reduced to $5\%$

- Originally $6 \%$
- Prior to 1710: Lend 100 litres, get 6 litres: ${100} = 6/y = > y = 6\%$
- In 1710: PV(5 litres @ $6\%$ ) = 5 / 0.06 = 83.3 litres
Example of "haircut" - partial default
I. During 18th c France had some perpetuals (discredited from the the Mississippi bubble in 1720) but mainly a mish-mash of term loans with quirks, and life annuities. Hard to value, hard to trade, expensive. ([Macdonald(2006)] p 244)
(A) After 1720, aversion to issue perpetuals (which was the instrument other nations used). Insisted on specified repayment. So no simple perpetuals. Which meant issues could not be substituted - each issue unique and idiosyncratic.
(B) Usury limit: Fundamental problem for France - usury limit of $5\%$, but lenders demanded higher than $5\%$ for all the reasons discussed here (poor credit, illiquid market). So often France would issue at a discount. (Remember, if you buy a bond at a discount then the e-ective interest is higher. Say 5l coupon for a perpetuity. If $\mathrm{Prc} = 100$, $100 = \mathrm{PV}(5\mathrm{l} \text{@ } 5\%) = 5 / 0.05$. But if $\mathrm{Prc} = 70$, $70 = \mathrm{PV}(5\mathrm{l} \text{@ } 7.14\%) = 5 / 0.0714$. Lenders were smart enough to know this, and it was a cute way around the $5\%$ usury limit.)
(C) Life annuities (rentes viagères, Macdonald p. 244): this was the biggest part of French borrowing ("after 1771 they dominated public borrowing" p 244). But horrible for making a deep & liquid market. The annuity would die with the buyer (would it redeem or simply disappear? I'm not sure). Taking idiosyncrasy of issues to the nth-degree: how could anyone trade a life annuity because it would depend on the life expectancy of the seller. Adam Smith in Wealth of Nations: "No man will give for an annuity upon the life of another, whose age and state of health are nearly the same as his own, the same price that he would give for one upon his own… It can never, therefore, make so convenient a transferrable stock as a perpetual annuity, of which the real value may be supposed always the same, or very nearly the same." (Book 5,3, p. 7)
# British Redeemable Perpetuals - Consols

Britain had Redeemable Perpetual Annuities

(Consols) until 2015

- "Consolidated Stock" or "Consolidated Annuities"
- Originally issued 1750s to consolidate earlier issues, finally retired 2015
- Fixed coupon (per year), never pay back principal
- BUT, key feature, redeemable at £100
Two issues around redemption:
I. Gov't not tied to high coupon forever
- Eg £4 annuity, rates to 3, PV = £4/0.03 = £133
Gov't could "redeem" and convert coupon to £3 (£3/0.03= £100)
II. In 1720 South Sea conversion of iredeemables key
# My Consols

I know Consols well

- I bought $4\%$ Consols in 1990s.
Gov't had right to redeem at £100. I was annoyed
Rates in 2015 were about $2.5\%$
$$
\mathrm{PV} = 4 /. 025 = £ 160
$$
- You can see why I was annoyed - redeemed at £100
I. England had Consols (redeemable perpetuals) - every issue the same, freely tradeable, transparent market, low rates
(A) Consols first issued
1751. In 1752 all outstanding redeemables converted into $3.5\%$ Consolidated Stock
1752. According to Macdonald (p. 244) originally $\mathbb{£}9\mathrm{mn}$, by 1780s $\mathbb{£}100\mathrm{mn}$. Large, liquid market.

(GDP) - But does normalize debt to size of the economy
- Could also look at debt per-capita but Debt-to-GDP is the standard
- Huge debates about how much is "too much" debt.
- Been raging for centuries

# Debt-To-Gdp Ratio - How Debt Is Discussed
- Many argue that this is dangerous. Reinhart & Rogo.
- But not so simple - look at Japan
![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-15/c441f5b5-2f9d-4e4d-bc65-1301c02e96db/13b9a91657d88a0aeec48c768bd69e3c5203a7924c88ee62210478329d78d92b.jpg)
Debt-to-GDP Ratio for US, UK & Japan
- Debt-to-GDP ratio - the way debt is always discussed.
- This is not perfect - it compares a stock (dollar amount of debt outstanding at a point in time) with a flow (GDP - value of goods and services produced during a year). But it does serve the purpose of calibrating or normalizing the size of the debt to the size of the economy.
- We could also look at debt per-capita (per person) but Debt-to-GDP is the standard way – at least for central gov't debt
- Huge debates (to which we turn shortly) about how much is "too much" debt. These debates have been raging for centuries
There was a high-level financial policy debate of sorts in the mid-1790s. On one side was Albert Gallatin, a Republican congressman from western Pennsylvania and future treasury secretary in the Jeerson and Madison administrations. Gallatin was the Republican opposition's financial expert, a role that corresponded to Hamilton's role in the Federalist Party. [Sylla(2010), 74]
Like other Republican leaders, Gallatin thought most public debts were bad, and his principal charge against Hamilton's policies was that the assumption of state debts in 1790 had made the national debt larger than it needed to be by at least $10.9 million. [Sylla(2010), 75]
Gallatin closed his Sketch with a challenge to the view that the debt funding plan "had created a large productive capital which did not exist before." His view was that "every nation is enfeebled by a public debt," and that the best policy was to extinguish public debt as quickly as it was feasible to do so by cutting spending and increasing taxes. He lamented the fact that foreign investors had purchased so much of the U. S. debt and other American securities, which only led the American sellers "to consume, to spend more, and they have consumed and spent extravagantly."
Taking in the great number of elegant houses which have been built within a few years in all the large cities, and which, however convenient to the inhabitants, a ord no additional revenue to the nation, it may be asserted that the greater part of the capital thus drawn from Europe for purchases of stock has been actually consumed, without leaving in its stead any other productive capital, and thus as the nation still owes the whole, it has been impoverished even by the only consequence of the funding system that has made any temporary addition to the apparent wealth of the country. That wealth is, in a great degree, consumed and destroyed, and the whole debt remains to be paid. (Adams 1960, III, 149)
Substitute China for Europe in this passage, and Gallatin's message would seem rather similar to arguments one often hears today, more than two centuries later. Other passages in his essay indicate that he would subscribe to "crowding out"—the idea that public borrowing and spending reduces private investment—as well as Ricardian equivalence—the notion that people react to increases in public debt by increasing their savings in order to be able to pay the increased taxes necessary to service a larger public debt. [Sylla(2010), 79]
- Following the 2008 financial crisis government debt ratios increased dramatically and there were, and are, huge debates about the benefits or dangers of government debt.
- The US went from $40 - 50\%$ prior to the Bush administration and roughly $60\%$ for the Bush administration, up to roughly $100\%$ following the 2008 crisis
- The UK went from roughly $45\%$ up to $100\%$ following the 2008 crisis.
Congress just passed a spending bill which will push the US debt-to-GDP Last week's op-ed in the Washington Post (https://www.washingtonpost.com/opinions/the-debt-crisis-is-on-our-doorstep/2018/03/27/fd28318c-27d3-11e8-bc72-077aa4dab9ef_story.html)
![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-15/c441f5b5-2f9d-4e4d-bc65-1301c02e96db/7bcf1f587f15f4e73ba95b6716414ff913951a497363964e220d912e7377b78b.jpg)
Debt-to-GDP Ratio for US & UK
- Many argue that this is dangerous. Reinhart & Rogo.
- But not so simple - look at Japan - the debt-to-GDP ratio has been going up steadily since the 1990 crisis and they are at roughly $200\%$ and they are still doing "OK"
![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-15/c441f5b5-2f9d-4e4d-bc65-1301c02e96db/ad426fc25692ae1c2183ba6653ca4b94d55e8e479ce020a4e3a691fcf8224099.jpg)
Debt-to-GDP Ratio for US, UK, & Japan
Many economists and politicians have sided with Gallatin from the 1790s in arguing that "most public debts [are] bad" and that "the best policy [is] to extinguish public debt as quickly as it was feasible to do so by cutting spending and increasing taxes." ([Sylla(2010)] p 75 and p 79)
# 2 Government Debt Default

Default on government debt is both common and highly disruptive

# 2.1 History
# Contents

Default on government debt has been common throughout history. Modern developed nations defaulted regularly during their transformation into modern states.

Britain didn't default 18th c (or 19th c). France defaulted 18th, Spain 19th

# [Reinhart And Rogo (2011)] Figure 5.1. This Copy From [Reinhart And Rogo (2008)]
# I. History On Defaults
#

(A) Development Of Debt Markets

1. We discussed Britain & France in 18th c
2. Britain developed a functioning debt market during and in spite of the South Sea Bubble (1720s)
 i. Parliament provided the political mechanism that controlled the temptation for the government to expropriate
(steal) from borrowers ii. Britain converted all the funny debt (lottery, irredeemables) into transparent, standardized, simple redeemable annuities. Eventually Consols (1751) iii. Bank of England served as the intermediary that could both process the borrowing and lending (things like lending very short-term, backed by its own capital, against taxes) and also monitor and manage the government's debt service.
3. France, in contrast, did not develop a well-functioning debt market for another hundred years or more.
 i. No elective and long-lived institution that could discipline the monarchy ii. All kinds of non-transparent and expensive debt (life annuities, lotteries, small and non-interchangeable issues) iii. No smoothly-functioning market trading standardized bonds
(B) Dierences between Britain & France
(C) Show up in the debt the countries could sustain
4. The financial positions of France and Britain in 1788 ([Macdonald(2006), 241]).

```latex
\begin{document}
\begin{tabular}{|c|c|c|}
\hline
£mn & France & Britain \\ \hline
GNP & £280mn & £135mn \\ \hline
Public Debt & £183mn & £245mn \\ \hline
    - as % of GNP & 65% & 182% \\ \hline
Debt Service & £12.2mn & £8.1mn \\ \hline
    - as % of GNP & 4.4% & 6.0% \\ \hline
E lective rate & 6.7% & 3.3% \\ \hline
\end{tabular}
\end{document}
``` i. Britain could support more debt (£245mn vs £183mn) – lenders believed they would pay back and therefore were willing to lend ii. Britain did not have to pay such high interest rates - again lenders believed Britain was less risk and so charged a lower risk premium
2. This reflects the lower risk premium and better reputation of Britain versus France. This better reputation was earned in multiple ways.
 i. A market with simple, liquid, interchangeable instruments ii. A proven track-record of paying debts (refraining from default) iii. Better financial management, including transparency and publication of annual public accounts ([Macdonald(2006)] p. 232)
#
(D) Also Shows Up In The Defaults

5. I think Britain did partially default in the 1670s but on domestic rather than foreign debt. (See [Macdonald(2006), 170] - "In January 1672 Charles decided to stop payment on the receipts… Charles continued to pay $5\%$ interest from time to time on the principal. James reneged on even this commitment, but in 1703 the debt was reinstated at $50\%$ of its old value. It thus became the first, albeit involuntary, component of the long-term national debt.") But not since then
6. France just defaulted willy-nilly. 1701,1715,1770,1788. Also 1812.
# 2.2 Theory For Debt Crises
# Contents
# Corporate Vs Sovereign Default

Corporate Default & Bankruptcy

- Transfer of ownership
- Bankruptcy is a beautiful thing - allows firms to make mistakes
- But depends on national laws - legal ownership & transfer
Sovereign Default
- State refuses to pay
- But creditors cannot take ownership
- In general - except see Newfoundland 1928-1933 (R&R p. 81)
- "sovereign but bankrupt Newfoundland absorbed by Canada"
# Why Do Sovereigns Ever Repay?

Why don't nations always default? Debtors cannot take ownership.

Eaton & Gersovitz - benefits of continued borrowing (R&R p 54-58)

Sovereigns repay so can borrow in the future

- "countries may need to borrow to fight recessions…"
# Why Do Sovereigns Ever Repay?

Actually, countries borrow to wage war - look at US

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-15/c441f5b5-2f9d-4e4d-bc65-1301c02e96db/9559ba706fc5a70fee8e3c2ad1a60acd7abb4ff0e8e9e9cb5b2b78abbb572d7f.jpg)
