Sargent & Hall NBER 21799

"even if lenders cannot directly go in and seize assets as in a conventional domestic default, they still retain leverage su cients to entice a country to repay loans of at least modest size." (R&R p 58)

# 2.3 Debt Ratio, Growth, Default
# Contents
# Sovereign Default "Graduation"

Sovereign Default & "Debt Intolerance":

Countries tend to serial default

- Then "graduate"
- Idea of countries "intolerant" to high debt
Useful as empirical regularity
Want a structural of causal explanation
- Reinhart & Rogo do wonderful job of discussing regularities
- Do not provide economic framework
Is debt intolerance something a country can eventually surmount? Or is a country with weak internal structures…doomed to follow a trajectory of lower growth…?"
# "Clubs"

One attempt to examine empirical regularities

R&R define "clubs" IIR rating & debt-to-GDP ratio (p 27 - 29).

- Club A - high IIR rating, most access to markets, least debt intolerant (fewest defaults)
- Club B - some access, medium debt intolerant.
Further split by debt-to-GDP ratio
Club C - little-or-no access
But both tolerance and IIR ratings result of unobserved characteristic
- Serial-default nations - failed or struggling nations.
R&R discuss "serial default" as something a nation falls into or graduates from
- "once a country slips into being a serial defaulter." p
29. Useful first view, but need deeper framework for causes and mechanisms
I. Political structure and controls on "Expropriation" or stealing (we will come back in chapter
(A) Autocracy - absolute monarchy like in France - too large a temptation to steal from lenders. Even recognized at the time
An establishment of this sort may be good in itself; but it is only so in a republic or a monarchy like England, whose finances are controlled by those alone who furnish them, and who only furnish as much as they please. But in a State which is weak, changeable, and absolute, like
France, stability must necessarily be wanting to it; since the King, or in his name a mistress, a minister or favorites, or still more, such extreme necessities as we find in the years 1707-1710, may overthrow the Bank - the temptation to which would be too great, and at the same time too easy. Duc de Saint-Simon (contemporary - quoted in Macdonald)
(B) Popular Democracy - voting uncovers "the popular will" and this popular will (the will of the people) must be followed. This is an idea that came later than England and Parliament in
30. Risk that this kind of democracy can itself impose a tyranny of the majority. As Calomiris & Haber say, "an ironic fact about popular sovereignty: democracies that least constrain the political power of majority rule tend to disadvantage the majority." (p 494)
(C) "Liberal" or Representative Democracy - voting elects representatives, who then make decisions until the next election, when they may be kicked out. Representative democracy traces back to the Glorious Revolution in 1688 and the new-found power of Parliament to act as a constraint on tyranny. One of the particular constraints was constraint on the King to tax and spend. Since Parliament and MPs were both in charge of the government and themselves lenders, they internalized the costs and benefits of expropriation from lenders.
II. Reinhart & Rogo 's discussion of "Serial Default", "Debt Intolerance", "Serial Default Graduation"
(A) Two points we need to make
31. Knowing the history of defaults is absolutely necessary for understanding macroeconomics and financial crises. We need to know the behavior countries and be suspicious of debt and borrowing
32. But "Serial Default" and "Debt Intolerance" (p 28) are symptoms of more fundamental causes when people want to understand the factors that shape complex systems and shift those systems from one equilibrium to another, they undertake multidimensional analysis—and one of the dimensions on which they focus intently is time. This is what a physician does, for example, when she is faced with a di cult diagnosis. She starts with fundamental, time-invariant factors, such as the patient's genetic predisposition toward particular diseases as deduced from a family medical history. ([Calomiris and Haber(2015)] p 25)
(B) For example, R&R categorize countries by IIR rating & debt-to-GDP ratio (p 27 - 29)
33. Use IIR (Institutional Investor Review) ratings to classify into 3 categories:
 i. Club A - high IIR rating, most access to capital markets, least debt intolerant ii. Club B - medium IIR rating, some access to capital markets, medium debt intolerant. Further split by debt-to-GDP ratio iii. Club C - low IIR rating, little-or-no access to capital markets
34. This use of data is valuable - shows us some of the characteristics across time and across countries - but misses the point that both debt tolerance and IIR ratings are results of some other unobserved characteristic.
(C) Serial-default nations are failed or struggling nations. R&R talk about "serial default" as a state that a nation can fall into or graduate from ("once a country slips into being a serial defaulter." p 29). Although this is useful as a first view of the data, we need a deeper understanding of the causes and mechanisms that lead to default. C&H's framework of property rights, rent-seeking, self-interested political coalitions, and financial systems subject to expropriation by various parties, is more fruitful.
(D) The more fundamental causes are the underlying property rights and political institutions. Serial default and debt intolerance persist because the political structures (the underlying property rights) change slowly. But debt repayment and debt default levels are results, not causes. The political structures (Autocracy vs Democracy, Populist vs Representative
(liberal) democracy)
# "Growth In A Time Of Debt"

High debt ratio associated with low growth

- "Growth in a Time of Debt" - 2010 American Economic Review
- "Public Debt Overhangs" - 2012 Journal of Economic Perspectives
Reinhart & Rogo (2010) claim high debt ratio $(90\% +)$ associated with low growth
The paper argues that when "gross external debt reaches 60 percent of GDP", a country's annual growth declined by two percent, and "for levels of external debt in excess of 90 percent" GDP growth was "roughly cut in half."
# Problems With "Growth In A Time Of Debt"

Some minor problems:

"While using RR's working spreadsheet, we identified coding errors, selective exclusion of available data, and unconventional weighting of summary statistics." (Herndon, Ash Pollin, 2013, in Cambridge Journal of Economics

Reinhart & Rogo "Public Debt Overhangs: Advanced-Economy Episodes since 1800" (JEP 2012)

- Look at growth during $< {90}\% \& > {90}\%$
- Again, problems with data
Problem set on this paper
[Reinhart and Rogo (2010), Reinhart et al. (2012) Reinhart, Reinhart, and Rogo ]
 https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Growth_in_a_Time_of_Debt https://www.bloomberg.com/news/articles/2013-04-18/faq-reinhart-rogo -and-the-excel-error-that-changed-history
Politicians, commentators, and activists widely cited the paper in political debates over the effectiveness of austerity in fiscal policy for debt-burdened economies. (See https://www.nytimes.com/2013/04/26/opinion/debt-growth-and-the-austerity-debate.html) The paper argues that when "gross external debt reaches 60 percent of GDP", a country's annual growth declined by two percent, and "for levels of external debt in excess of 90 percent" GDP growth was "roughly cut in half."
# 3 Government Debt And Democracy
# 3.1 Serial Default - Symptom Of Deeper Political Issues
# Contents
# Reinhart & Rogo 'S "Serial Default"

Remember Reinhart & Rogo's (ch 1 p 46) - "serial default" and "graduation"

- Claim (supported by evidence) that countries "serially default" and then "graduate"
R&R discuss more in Chapter 2
- I have not assigned for reading - I have put it on Canvas if you want to read
- Statistical analysis of serial default & "debt Intolerance"
We are examining two cases:
- Britain defaulted 1594 (maybe 1670s) but not since
- France defaulted 1701,1715,1770,1788,1812, then "graduated"
Defaults are from [Reinhart and Rogo (2011)] chapter 6, particularly Tables 6.1 and 6.2.
# Sovereign Default "Graduation"

Sovereign Default & "Debt Intolerance":

Countries tend to serial default

- Then "graduate"
- Idea of countries "intolerant" to high debt
Useful as empirical regularity
Want a structural of causal explanation
- Reinhart & Rogo do wonderful job of discussing regularities
- Do not provide economic framework
Is debt intolerance something a country can eventually surmount? Or is a country with weak internal structures doomied to follow a trajectory of lower growth… Turn eventually to Calomiris & Haber - better job for underlying causes
# "Clubs"

One attempt to examine empirical regularities

R&R define "clubs" IIR rating & debt-to-GDP ratio (p 27 - 29).

- Club A - high IIR rating, most access to markets, least debt intolerant (fewest defaults)
- Club B - some access, medium debt intolerant.
Further split by debt-to-GDP ratio
Club C - little-or-no access
But both tolerance and IIR ratings result of unobserved characteristic
- Serial-default nations - failed or struggling nations.
R&R discuss "serial default" as something a nation falls into or graduates from
- "once a country slips into being a serial defaulter." p
29. Useful first view, but need deeper framework for causes and mechanisms
I. Political structure and controls on "Expropriation" or stealing (we will come back in chapter
(A) Autocracy - absolute monarchy like in France - too large a temptation to steal from lenders. Even recognized at the time
An establishment of this sort may be good in itself; but it is only so in a republic or a monarchy like England, whose finances are controlled by those alone who furnish them, and who only furnish as much as they please. But in a State which is weak, changeable, and absolute, like France, stability must necessarily be wanting to it; since the King, or in his name a mistress, a minister or favorites, or still more, such extreme necessities as we find in the years 1707-1710, may overthrow the Bank - the temptation to which would be too great, and at the same time too easy. Duc de Saint-Simon (contemporary - quoted in Macdonald)
(B) Popular Democracy - voting uncovers "the popular will" and this popular will (the will of the people) must be followed. This is an idea that came later than England and Parliament in
30. Risk that this kind of democracy can itself impose a tyranny of the majority. As Calomiris & Haber say, "an ironic fact about popular sovereignty: democracies that least constrain the political power of majority rule tend to disadvantage the majority." (p 494)
(C) "Liberal" or Representative Democracy - voting elects representatives, who then make decisions until the next election, when they may be kicked out. Representative democracy traces back to the Glorious Revolution in 1688 and the new-found power of Parliament to act as a constraint on tyranny. One of the particular constraints was constraint on the King to tax and spend. Since Parliament and MPs were both in charge of the government and themselves lenders, they internalized the costs and benefits of expropriation from lenders.
II. Reinhart & Rogo 's discussion of "Serial Default", "Debt Intolerance", "Serial Default Graduation"
(A) Two points we need to make
31. Knowing the history of defaults is absolutely necessary for understanding macroeconomics and financial crises. We need to know the behavior countries and be suspicious of debt and borrowing
32. But "Serial Default" and "Debt Intolerance" (p 28 ) are symptoms of more fundamental causes when people want to understand the factors that shape complex systems and shift those systems from one equilibrium to another, they undertake multidimensional analysis—and one of the dimensions on which they focus intently is time. This is what a physician does, for example, when she is faced with a di cult diagnosis. She starts with fundamental, time-invariant factors, such as the patient's genetic predisposition toward particular diseases as deduced from a family medical history. ([Calomiris and Haber(2015)] p 25)
(B) For example, R&R categorize countries by IIR rating & debt-to-GDP ratio (p 27 - 29)
33. Use IIR (Institutional Investor Review) ratings to classify into 3 categories:
 i. Club A - high IIR rating, most access to capital markets, least debt intolerant ii. Club B - medium IIR rating, some access to capital markets, medium debt intolerant. Further split by debt-to-GDP ratio iii. Club C - low IIR rating, little-or-no access to capital markets
34. This use of data is valuable - shows us some of the characteristics across time and across countries - but misses the point that both debt tolerance and IIR ratings are results of some other unobserved characteristic.
(C) Serial-default nations are failed or struggling nations. R&R talk about "serial default" as a state that a nation can fall into or graduate from ("once a country slips into being a serial defaulter." p 29). Although this is useful as a first view of the data, we need a deeper understanding of the causes and mechanisms that lead to default. C&H's framework of property rights, rent-seeking, self-interested political coalitions, and financial systems subject to expropriation by various parties, is more fruitful.
(D) The more fundamental causes are the underlying property rights and political institutions. Serial default and debt intolerance persist because the political structures (the underlying property rights) change slowly. But debt repayment and debt default levels are results, not causes. The political structures (Autocracy vs Democracy, Populist vs Representative
(liberal) democracy)
# "Serial Default" Is Symptom Of Deeper Issues

Debt intolerance is a syndrome in which weak institutional structures and a problematic political system make external borrowing a tempting device for governments to employ to avoid hard decisions about spending and taxing. (R&R Ch 2)

What makes a successful nation? Why do some nations fail and others succeed?

- These are deep and di cult issues in political philosophy, with contributions from Hobbes, Hume, Locke, Rousseau
- Recent important contributions from Daron Acemoglu & James Robinson - Why Nations Fail and The Narrow Corridor
- Also (less well-known) William Riker Liberalism Against Populism
- Focus on political and economic institutions, the rules and enforcement mechanisms that exist in society
- Why Nations Fail: focuses on extractive vs inclusive institutions
- The Narrow Corridor: how liberty and freedom follows from shackling Hobbes's Leviathan (the all-powerful state), requiring on-going race between the state and civil society
# Our Focus: 1) Democracy & Debt; 2) Banks & Political Institutions

We are going to focus now on debt and democracy, described in Macdonald's A Free Nation Deep in Debt

- Argues that democracy and political freedom lead to national debt, and that debt was the tool that allowed democracies to flourish and triumph in the 18th-20th centuries
- We consider Britain and France in 1720, and then the US in 1790s
- The financial crises of 1720 and 1790s were associated with growth and transformation of government debt markets
- Britain transformed, took on debt. France did not. Britain triumphed
Later in the course we turn to banks and banking crises, and Calomiris & Haber's Fragile by Design
- Explanations for crises and fragility rooted in political and economic coalitions that shape banking institutions
Not the full explanation, but an important part
# 4 Digressions
# 4.1 Short-Term ("Liquidity") Vs Long-Term ("Solvency") Issues
# Contents
# "Liquidity" Vs "Solvency"

Short-term: "Liquidity" or Roll-over Risk

Long-term: "Solvency" Risk

Example: Borrow $100

- Short-term: pay back $104, borrow another $100 each period
- Risks: 1) lenders decide not to re-lend $100; 2) cannot pay $4
- Long-term (perpetual annuity): pay back $4 each year
- Risk: cannot pay $4
Trade-o: short-term usually lower rates:
US 1yr: $1.08\%$
- US 30yr: $2.99\%$
![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-15/c441f5b5-2f9d-4e4d-bc65-1301c02e96db/7b06ed5e8a40b1f83aab2092fd1f13611f628c47e06d8263316dedfe87fe281c.jpg)
![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-15/c441f5b5-2f9d-4e4d-bc65-1301c02e96db/b75a4ef8a9321cfdf44791890c4677b0404af533fa347d6641af71f38b80ba93.jpg)
# Short-Term Vs Long-Term

This was problem with Argentina in

1888. In November 1888 a £3.5 million ering of the Buenos Ayres [sic] Drainage and Waterworks Company failed, and Barings felt obliged to lend to Argentina through acceptance credits. Falling raw materials prices in 1890 made it impossible for the Argentine government to meet these credits as they came due. (Kindleberger p. 122)

Lenders might prefer short-term - to force borrowers to behave

Why would borrowers want short-term?

Short-term rates usually lower rates:

US 1yr: $1.08\%$ US 30yr: $2.99\%$

Britain in 1700s: short-term debt to finance wars, then convert the debt to longer-term debt after each war's conclusion (R&R p 70)

US currently mainly short-term. Is this sensible?

I. Theory of why countries repay - ST debt & rollover risk

(A) Distinction between short-term rollover risk

(liquidity) vs long-term willingness to pay

(insolvency)

(B) Example of borrowing $100. Two possibilities

1889. Long-term – Annuity

(perpetuity) – pay back $4 each year forever. This was England in early 1700s

 1890. Short-term – one-year bond – pay-back $104 each year, borrowing $100 anew each time

(C) If have to re-borrow what happens when lenders decide not to

(D) This was problem with Argentina in

1891. Greece in 2012

# 4.2 Default Vs Re-Scheduling
# Contents
# Default Vs Re-Scheduling

Default is non-payment, Re-Scheduling changing the terms

Economically the same

- Default: usually get something back
- Re-Scheduling: change the terms (eg lower coupon) - e-ective default
Greece oc ially launched a bond swap Friday [24-feb-2012], aiming to wipe 107 billion euros ($142 billion) o its 350-billion-euro debt bill. The debt write-down, also known as haircut, hits private investors hard.
As part of the deal [debt swap] - also known as a haircut - private creditors are asked to trade in their Greek bonds for new ones or erging lower interest rates and longer maturities [pushing the €100 principal further away].
France: reduced coupon on rentes
Britain: often reduced interest on Consols - but redeemable
Rates 3%, £4 annuity worth £133.
- Redeem for £100, re-issue new £3 annuity. Or just reduce to £3
I. Default vs restructuring. Re-scheduling
(A) p
61. Greece: "As part of the deal [debt swap] - also known as a haircut - private creditors are asked to trade in their Greek bonds for new ones or ering lower interest rates and longer maturities [pushing the €100 principal further away]. Bondholders are expected to take a nominal loss of $53.5\%$ on their holdings, which equates to a real loss of 73 to $74\%$." (web, http://www.dw.com/en/greek-creditors-receive-o cial-haircut-notification/a-15767530, early 2012) "Greece o cially launched a bond swap Friday [24-feb-2012], aiming to wipe 107 billion euros ($142 billion) o its 350-billion-euro debt bill. The debt write-down, also known as haircut, hits private investors hard."
(B) France: reduced coupons on rents - a form of re-scheduling and thus default
(C) Britain: often reduced coupon on Consols
62. But this is interesting - not a re-scheduling.
63. Consols redeemable annuities - callable at £100. Often the government would find it easier to have investors take a reduce coupon rather than redeem the existing bonds and then re-issue at new
(lower) coupon
 64. Rates 3%: Annuity of £4 is worth £4/0.03 = £133. Can redeem at £100, then re-issue new annuity at £3 coupon, worth £100.
# 4.3 Credit Spreads
# Contents
# Credit Spread: Market Measure Of "Default"

Markets embed probability and loss from default

- Remember, "yield" measure how much you earn - trades o low price vs high higher coupon
High yield compensates for higher risk
More risky countries higher yield (3-oct-18 from FT)

```latex
\begin{document}
\begin{tabular}{|c|c|c|c|c|c|c|}
\hline
 & Maturity & Coupon & S&amp;P rating & Price & Yield & Spread \\ \hline
Peru & 3/29/19 & 7.125 & BBB+ & 104.40 & 2.60% & 34bp \\ \hline
Brazil & 1/31/23 & 2.63 & BB- & 91.40 & 4.89% & 195bp \\ \hline
Turkey & 3/23 & 3.25 & BB & 86.76 & 6.73% & 379bp \\ \hline
\end{tabular}
\end{document}
```

Peru $7.125\%$ of 3/29/19:

High coupon good - paid a lot each year

- But pay high price so only earn $2.60\%$ (if $100 earn 7.125%)
# Credit Spread: Market Measure Of "Default"

"Credit Spread": yield relative to low-risk yield - UST

```latex
\begin{document}
\begin{tabular}{|c|c|c|c|c|c|c|}
\hline
 & Maturity & Coupon & S&amp;P rating & Price & Yield & Spread \\ \hline
Peru & 3/29/19 & 7.125 & BBB+ & 104.40 & 2.60% & 34bp \\ \hline
Brazil & 1/31/23 & 2.63 & BB- & 91.40 & 4.89% & 195bp \\ \hline
Turkey & 3/23 & 3.25 & BB & 86.76 & 6.73% & 379bp \\ \hline
\end{tabular}
\end{document}
```

Measure relative to low-risk yield - UST

Market compensation for extra risk of default (over UST)

- Brazil 1/23: 2yr UST $2.94\%$. Spread = 4.89 - 2.94 = 1.95% or 195bp
Higher spread, maket says more chance of default
- Believe market before credit-rating agencies
Rating agencies say Brazil BB-, Turkey BB
- Market says Brazil 195bp, Turkey 379bp
# Credit Spread For Europe

Germany is the "safe" country

Measure France, Italy, Greece relative to Germany (24-apr-17 from FT)

```latex
\begin{document}
\begin{tabular}{|c|c|c|c|c|c|c|c|}
\hline
 & Maturity & Coupon & Price & Yield & Spread & Intensity & Prob.def) \\ \hline
Greece & 1/28 & 3.75 & 96.59 & 4.20% & 380bp & 5.35 & 41% \\ \hline
Germany & 7/28 & 4.75 & 141.47 & 0.40 & & & \\ \hline
France & 5/28 & 0.75 & 100.01 & 0.75 & 35bp & 0.50 & 5% \\ \hline
Italy & 9/28 & 4.75 & 114.54 & 3.04 & 264bp & 3.49 & 29% \\ \hline
\end{tabular}
\end{document}
```

- Higher spread gives investors compensation for possible default
- Also - market tells us how much risk
- Can use to fit default model
Market behaves as if Greece $20\%$ in 2yrs, $66\%$ in 10yrs
# Credit Spread For Europe

Germany is the "safe" country

Measure France, Italy, Spain, Belgium relative to Germany

# I. Digression On Credit Spreads

(A) Important point: default (or re-scheduling) does not mean investors are irrational or lose by investing in bonds that eventually default

1. Often investors receive a higher coupon or higher yield that compensates for the chance of default
2. Bonds, like any financial security, are simply a game of expectations - you pay today with some chance of getting money in the future.
(B) Credit spreads as of 24-apr-17 (from FT)

```latex
\begin{document}
\begin{tabular}{|c|c|c|c|c|c|c|}
\hline
 & Maturity & Coupon & S&amp;P rating & Price & Yield & Spread \\ \hline
Brazil & 1/18 & 8 & BB & 103.38 & 3.30% & 207bp \\ \hline
Peru & 3/29/19 & 7.125 & BBB+ & 109.83 & 1.92% & 69bp \\ \hline
Brazil & 1/31/22 & 12.50 & BB & 111.029 & 9.49% & 768bp \\ \hline
\end{tabular}
\end{document}
```

For Brazil 1/31/22 FT says 111.29 but price & yield don't match- $9.49\% - > 111.029$

\begin{document}

Greece & 4/1/19 & 4.75 & 97.07 & 6.38% & 709bp & 11.40 & 20.4% \\ \hline
 & 2/27 & 3.00 & 75.75 & 6.36% & 603bp & 10.93 & 66.5% \\ \hline
Germany & 4/19 & 0.50 & 102.39 & -0.71% & & & \\ \hline

Italy & 6/27 & 2.20 & 100.20 & 2.19% & 186bp & 3.04 & 26% \\ \hline
\end{tabular}
\end{document}
```

 & Maturity & Coupon & S&amp;P rating & Price & Yield & Spread \\ \hline

```latex
\begin{document}
\begin{tabular}{|c|c|c|c|c|c|c|c|}
\hline
 & Maturity & Coupon & Price & Yield & Spread & Intensity & Prob.def) \\ \hline
Greece & 1/28 & 3.75 & 96.59 & 4.20% & 380bp & 5.35 & 41% \\ \hline
Germany & 7/28 & 4.75 & 141.47 & 0.40 & & & \\ \hline
France & 5/28 & 0.75 & 100.01 & 0.75 & 35bp & 0.50 & 5% \\ \hline
Italy & 9/28 & 4.75 & 114.54 & 3.04 & 264bp & 3.49 & 29% \\ \hline

(D) "Yield" correctly measures how much you earn - it trades of the lower price (you buy the bond cheaper) and higher coupon (you earn more each year).

3. Take the Brazil $12.50\%$ of 1/31/22. Very high coupon is good (earn a lot each year) but you have to pay a lot up-front ($111.029). Net you earn $9.49\%$
4. Still, this is much higher than for US go'$v_{t}$ bonds - similar maturity UST is the $1.875\%$ of 03/22 with yield of $1.81\%$. Spread of 768bp.

(E) Credit spread incorporates both the probability of the default and the value of the loss - how much investors are hurt by the loss.

5. Incorporates recovery – default usually does not mean you lose all $100

# South Sea Bubble Mississippi Company

Thomas S. Coleman

# Extraordinary Bubbles, Extraordinary Accomplishments South Sea Bubble

(England) And Mississippi Company

(France) Two of the most celebrated financial bubbles of all time

- Stories of panic and mania (Mackay - Extraordinary Popular Delusions)
But part of more fundamental social and political transformations
(Macdonald) - Building capacity for government debt and borrowing, and market to trade
- Britain successfully transformed, France did not
Britain's political system was open to transformation, France's was not
(from Velde 2007, about France): When the first proposal [for Law's bank] was discussed in 1715, royal advisers objected that a bank would never work under an absolute monarchy, because the king would always be tempted to raid the bank.
"Political economy" issues of political systems, property rights, rule of law
- We read Macdonald, Calomiris & Haber. Also work by Acemoglu & Robinson

# 1 South Sea Co - London 1719-1720
# Contents
# Read Mackay & Kindleberger

The South Sea and Mississippi Co. episodes hugely important

- Immensely entertaining (so read Mackay - I discuss the boring parts)
- Profound cultural e ects
Newton; "Bubblers bubbled"; word "Bubble"
- Paris: distrust of bonds and markets
- Long-last legal e ects
- London: Bubble Act (forbidding joint-stock companies for 100yrs!)
Significant institutional and political developments
- Britain on path for deep & stable government debt market
- Debt & democracy
James Cole, 1715-1774
![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-15/c441f5b5-2f9d-4e4d-bc65-1301c02e96db/55406f5a5b040ce68e70632dbe314697217afcc974af960fbe6e9ddf647a8dfc.jpg)
# Debt Restructuring & Political Fight

Two stories:

I. Debt restructuring - debts from Nine Year's War (1688-97) & Wars of Spanish Succession (1702-1715)

- In 1717, England debt-to-GDP ratio about $80\%$
- In 1717 debt was mish-mash
- By 1721 debt was largely restructured and set on path for stability
II. Political fighting - 1719-1720
Bank of England - monopoly on managing gov't debt
- Whigs - new financial & merchant, urban interests
- South Sea Co trying to break Bank of England monopoly
- Tories - "old" landed aristocracy
- Sword Blade Bank
# What Happened: Short Version

Britain & South Sea bubble:

- South Sea Co. & Sword Blade Bank won competition to restructure debt
- Triggered a massive speculative bubble – largely supported by fraud
- Bubble and South Sea Co. collapsed, but debt had been restructured
- End result: stable gov't debt market, Bank of England agent
Contrast with France & Mississippi bubble:
John Law, Mississippi Co: restructuring French debt
- A bubble but not a fraud
- Law was truly innovative: gov't debt as "gov't equity"; paper money
- But after-e etc: no gov't debt market, France at disadvantage for 100yrs
Classic account - irrational bubbles: McKay & Kindleberger
Substantive account - building a stable gov't debt market: Macdonald & Velde
- Bank of England - monopoly on managing gov't debt
- Almost all the short-term debt (I think £2.5mn) plus redeemables from lottery loans (£16.5mn) plus its own £3.4mn of
(now) 5% annuities.
- This is about $47\%$ of total debt outstanding.
- The £2.5mn short-term plus £16.5mn redeemables was about $60\%$ of debt held by public
The South Sea Company had been founded by the Tories to overturn the Bank of England's increasing monopoly on government funding. But much to their irritation, after the initial success of the South Sea flotation, the Bank had resumed its progress. Harley's later lottery loans had been managed by the Bank, and it was now responsible for the redeemable $5 \%$ s which had been issued to refund them. By 1719 the Bank was responsible for managing almost the entire shortterm debt, as well as £16.5 million of redeemables and its own £3.4mn perpetual annuities. All this was in addition to its profitable private
# 1.1 South Sea & English Trading Cos
# Contents
# English Trading Cos

Trading Cos mixture of banking & business

- Trading Monopolies + Lending to Government
- Implicit bargain between trading companies and government
- Government wanted to borrow, o ered sweeteners in monopoly
Odd to our eyes, but normal in 1700
# Bank Of England, East India Co, South Sea Co

Bank of England, survived to today (much changed)

- founded 1694, monopoly on banking - private & gov't
- Monopoly rights that were gradually eroded through the 18th c
East India Co
- Founded 1600, monopoly on trade with India.
One of the most powerful entities in the world 19th c
South Sea Company:
- Founded 1711, monopoly South America trade
Trade virtually worthless - only value gov't annuities
I. Background on trading companies
II. Truly odd creatures to us. They were a combination of lenders to the government and trading monopolies.
(A) These just don't exist nowadays, or at least in developed countries.
III. Basically an implicit bargain, cooperation between trading companies and the government.
(A) In Britain, there was wide overlap between the government (MPs in Parliament) and the political classes (aristocracy, landed gentry, merchants).
(B) Much political conflict between parties (Tories landed gentry, Whigs merchants) but the power was dispersed throughout society
(C) This was critically important, as emphasized by Calomiris & Haber, Macdonald, Acemoglu & Robinson.
(D) Stark contrast with France, and I believe one of the key issues for why the financial transformation we are discussing was successful in England in 1720 and the US in 1792, but not in France in
1720. IV. The government wanted to borrow money from these companies
(A) The government granted exclusive trading rights or monopolies and borrowed from these companies
(B) An implicit bargain that the companies would lend (or give) money to the government in return for the privilege to trade.
(C) We can think of the trading rights, the trading monopolies, as sweeteners that induced the companies to lend to the government and lend at lower rates then they would otherwise
(D) To our minds this horribly mixes government borrowing and underlying trading business. But prior to 1720 that was the norm.
V. Three main companies we are concerned with - East India Co., Bank of England, South Sea Company
(A) Bank of England has survived to this day, although in a totally di erent form than we are examining in 1720
1721. founded 1694,2. monopoly on banking, banking to both the government (agent for buying and selling government debt) and private banking. Monopoly rights that were gradually eroded through the 18th c
(B) East India Co
1722. founded 1600,2. monopoly on trade with India. This was, obviously, a very valuable monopoly and probably made up the bulk of the company's value
(C) South Sea Company:
1723. founded 1711,2. monopoly on trading with South America - virtually worthless.
1724. The only value was in the government annuities it held.
# South Sea Co - No Underlying Business

Important to understand basic business of South Sea Co - none

- Right to trade with South America
Unless the Spaniards are to be divested of common sense… throwing away the only valuable stake they have left in the world, and in short, bent on their own ruin, we cannot suggest that they will ever part with so valuable, indeed so estimable a jewel, as the exclusive power of trade to their own plantations. (Daniel Defoe, quoted in Macdonald p. 207)
- "Gov't debt mutual fund" but no other underlying business
- Fight between South Sea Co and Bank of England
- Both bid for right to convert gov't debt
- South Sea Co won - with substantial outright bribery of MPs
- Bank of England bid stayed within reason - South Sea Co did not
# Comment On Daniel Defoe

Merchant, spy, novelist. Spent time in prison for political agitation and also for debts. And one of the great English novelists. Author of Robinson Crusoe, "second only to the bible in translations". Journal of the Plague Year, an amazing chronicle of the Great Plague of London in

1665. And one of my favorite novels, Moll Flanders - the story of the truly amoral but wonderful title character: "the fall and eventual redemption, both material and spiritual, of a lone woman in 17th-century England."

# Some Additional Thoughts On Britain & South Sea Co.
- Important to understand basic business of South Sea Co - none
- Right to trade with South America
But Spain controlled South America, and was never going to let England trade with its colonies
This was recognized at the time: Defoe
Unless the Spaniards are to be divested of common sense… throwing away the only valuable stake they have left in the world, and in short, bent on their own ruin, we cannot suggest that they will ever part with so valuable, indeed so estimable a jewel, as the exclusive power of trade to their own plantations. (quoted in [Macdonald(2006)] p. 207, reference to Carswell The South Sea Bubble 1993, p. 47)
- South Sea Co issued shares and bought gov't annuities
Before 1720, held £9.2mn of $6 \%$ annuities ([Macdonald(2006)] p 208)
Market rates $\sim 4\frac{1}{2} -5\%$: PV(£6 @ $4.6\%$ ) = £130
- "Gov't debt mutual fund" but no other underlying business
- Fight between South Sea Co and Bank of England
- Both bid for right to convert
- South Sea Co won - with substantial outright bribery of MPs
- Bank of England bid stayed within reason - South Sea Co did not
# 1.2 English Debt 1715,1717,1719
# Contents
# Restructuring Debt

In 1715, large debt, expensive, messy

- Debts from wars (Nine Years and Spanish Succession)
- Debt-to-GDP roughly $80\%$ - High by today's standards, horribly high for 1715
- Messy: Lottery loans, irredeemables
1717: Government had managed well enough rates down
- Could refinance some of the debt (Trading Co & Lottery). Not iredeemables
1719: Still trying to refinance iredeemables
- South Sea Co proposes plan to refinance irredeemables
- pure speculation
- Core of the bubble
# English Debt 1715 - Expensive Lottery & Irredeemables

End of wars. Four types of debt, some of it quirky and expensive

```latex
\begin{document}
\begin{tabular}{|c|c|c|}
\hline
 & Amount & Percent \\ \hline
Trading Co – Perpetual Annuities – OK & £16.4 & 34% \\ \hline
Lottery Loans – quirky and expensive & £11.4 & 23% \\ \hline
Irredeemables – 32yr &amp; 99yr – expensive & £12.6 & 26% \\ \hline
Short-term – OK & £8.4 & 17% \\ \hline
TOTAL & £49 & \\ \hline
\end{tabular}
\end{document}
```

I. Perpetual Annuities - Redeemable & "cheap"

Held by trading cos (East India $5\%$, BoE & SS $6\%$

- From medieval usury laws, automatically redeemable at option of borrower
- Option meant government could refinance when rates improved
II. Lottery Loans - expensive (& quirky by modern standards)
- £6 annuity (6%) for 32 years, but at end get £0 or £100
- £0, Annuity: 77 = PV(£6 annuity @ y = 6.86%)
- £100, Bond: 77 = PV(£6 bond @ y = 8%)
- Expensive - could be redeemed, but at £100
- Redeem at 15yrs @ £100: 77 = PV(£6 15yr bond @ y = 8.82%)
III. £12.6mn "irredeemables" - 32yr & 99yr annuities - expensive
- With fixed end, not automatically redeemable
- For financing, 99yrs as good as forever
- Expensive - maybe $6.67\%$, but could not redeem and lower rate
- Center-piece of South Sea Bubble - exchanging this expensive debt was a major motivation for 1720 activity
A Lottery is a taxation \ Upon all the fools in creation
And Heav'n be prais'd \ It is easily rais'd…
The Lottery (1731) Henry Fielding
English debt
1715. Generally, debt was a mess of different types (and expensive) [Macdonald(2006), 186]:

Macdonald P 186

I think distribution of annuities among trading companies:

```latex
\begin{document}
\begin{tabular}{|c|c|c|c|}
\hline
 & Type & Amount & Source \\ \hline
East India Co. & 5% annuities & £3.9mn & Implied from Bank of England &amp; South Sea \\ \hline
Bank of England & 6% annuities & £3.4mn & Macdonald p 208 for 1719 \\ \hline

I. GNP roughly $60mn. Total debt £49mn. Debt-to-GDP roughly 80%.

II. Much in annuities

(perpetuals) : £16.4mn. Held by trading cos:

	- East India Co (5% annuities)

	- Bank of England (6% annuities) (was this £3.4mn? Sounds like it from Macdonald p 208)

	- South Sea Company (6% annuities) (£9.2mn? Seems to be from Macdonald p 208)

(A) These trading companies are odd creatures to us. They were a combination of lenders to the government and trading monopolies.

(B) At this period – before 1715 – the government wanted to borrow money but when those companies were founded the government had to offer sweeteners of valuable trading monopolies to lower the interest rates

III. £11.4 mn Lottery loans - unusual

(quirky) by modern standards:

£6 annuity (6%) for 32 years

	- Issued at discount - £77 - only got £77 for the loans

	- Discount meant that it was more expensive - more valuable to the investor which was needed at the time to induce investors to lend to the government

	- At end, get zero

(annuity) or £100

(bond) , determined by lottery

	- Annuity: $77 = \mathrm{PV}(\mathbf{\Phi}$ 6 annuity @ y = 6.86%)

	- Bond: ${77} =$ PV(£6 bond @ y = 8%)

	- Expensive - could be redeemed, but at £100

	- Redeem at 15yrs @ £100: 77 = PV(£6 15yr bond @ y = 8.82%)

IV. £12.6mn "irredeemables" - 32yr & 99yr annuities

	- Expensive - maybe $6.67\%$, but could not redeem and lower rate

	- Center-piece of South Sea Bubble - exchanging this expensive debt was a major motivation for 1720 activity

	- From [Macdonald(2006), 187] issued 1702-1710 (call it 1705, making them maturity 1737 and 1804) and roughly coupon $6.67\%$

	- In 1720 they are roughly 17yrs and 84yr maturity.

	- With market rates @5%, PV(17yr 6.67% annuity @ 5%) = 75.198, PV(84yr 6.67% annuity @ 5%) = 131.186

	- To reduce coupon to $5 \%$, PV(17yr $5 \%$ annuity @ $5 \%$ ) = 56.37, PV(84yr $5 \%$ annuity @ $5 \%$ ) = 98.340

	- Replacing those 99yr irredeemables with a new £5 perpetual at par (100) would mean large loss for holders.

	- This was the crux of the problem

# V. £8.4Mn Short-Term
- perpetuals & redeemability: perpetual annuities had redemption features automatically written into the contract (Macdonald p 187). A holdover from medieval usury laws, where a perpetual (non-fixed) annuity had to be redeemable or else the borrower was "enslaving" themselves to the lender.
- 32yr & 99yr not redeemable. Because they expired automatically they did not have the redeemable feature automatically written in, but from an economic perspective there was no real difference between 99yrs and forever
- Important, because the perpetuals could be redeemed (or coupon lowered - same thing) and thus would not be so expensive when rates fell.
- The redeemables were hanging around costing the government.
# 1717 - Refunding Annuities & Lotteries

Interest rates fell sharply, gov't could refund

Rates fell to about $5\%$

Trading Co debt

BoE & SS had $6\%$ annuity

- Lowered to 5%
- Not "haircut" - redeemable
# Lottery Loans

Conversion to $5\%$ annuity

Perpetual, redeemable

- First public issue

# Big Accomplishments, But Not Finished
- Irredeemables not converted
By 1717 interest rates had fallen far enough that the government could start to restructure the debt. (Macdonald p 186)
Bank of England and South Sea Company persuaded to lower the coupons on their annuities from $6\%$ to $5\%$.
- Result of redeemability.
- Remember that $\mathbf{PV} =$ coupon / rate
- If rate down to $5\%$, then PV(£6 coupon @ $5\%$ ) = 6/0.05 = £120
- If coupon £5, the PV(£5 coupon @ 5%) = 5/0.05 = £100
- Government says: "We will either redeem (you get £100) or you lower coupon (you get £100).
- Not a haircut because it is part of the contract
- Subsequently prices rose to £104.
$$
- \mathbf {P V} = £ 104 = £ 5 / \mathbf {y} = > \mathbf {y} = 4.8 \%
$$
- End of year, short-term debt refunded with coupon £4.
- Implies rate had fallen to $4\%$.

# 1.31720 Exchange - The Bubble & Aftermath
# Contents
# Narrative
# 1720 Exchange Narrative

Public Motivation: re-structure public debt, irredeemables into redeemable

Private South Sea motivation: profit for insiders and power

- January 21, South Sea announces it will take over entire public debt of £31.6mn on favorable terms
- January 27, South Sea & Bank of England submit proposals
- February 1, South Sea wins - Financially irresponsible + bribery
Rest of the story is in the South Sea shares PLUS
- Only asset gov't $5\%$ annuities
£5 for shareholders (per 100 shares)
1720 market rates $\sim 4.3\%$
- PV(£5 annuity @ 4.3%) = £116

```latex
\begin{document}
\begin{tabular}{|c|c|}

April 14 & £300 \\ \hline
May 19 & £375 \\ \hline
End-May & £600 \\ \hline
End-June & £900 \\ \hline
August & £1000 \\ \hline
1721 & £100 \\ \hline
\end{tabular}

By September 1720 the bubble had burst, the South Sea Co owned most government debt, but it was nothing but a shell and the directors had fled, were in jail, or in disgrace.

Motivations: to re-structure the public debt, and particularly to convert the 32-year and 99-year irredeemables into redeemable annuities.

South Sea motivation: profit for insiders and power - breaking the Bank of England near-monopoly on banking and managing government debt

- January 21, South Sea announces it will take over entire public (non trading-company) debt of £31.6mn on favorable terms (Macdonald p 210)
- January 27, South Sea & Bank of England both submit proposals
Both South Sea and Bank of England o ered. (Macdonald 210)
- Initial proposal by South Sea Co, 21-jan-1720. Caught Bank of England by surprise.
- Motivated by watching what was happening in Paris with John Law & Mississippi Co.
- Allies in Parliament insisted BoE given chance to bid
- Both bid 27-jan-1720.
- Both had cash pay-0 s to the government
- Both would issue new securities, exchange for existing securities. Basically only way that irredeemables could be converted is if shares in company could be sold for more the 100 (say 130).
- South Sea Company won with its 1-feb-1720 bid both by pitching a good-sounding but financially impossible bid, and by massive bribery. "The next weeks [after the 1-feb bids] witnessed some of the most shameless acts of bribery in the history of Britain." (Macdonald p 213)
- By September 1720 the bubble had burst, the South Sea Co owned most government debt, but it was nothing but a shell and the directors had fled, were in jail, or in disgrace.

# South Sea Exchange As "Securitization"
# South Sea Co: Just A Shell Company - A Swindle

Some bubbles are swindles, some are not. The Mississippi Bubble was not a swindle; the South Sea Bubble was.

(Kindleberger) The South Sea Bubble is confusing.

I make sense by thinking of it as a "Securitization":

- Exchange existing debt
(irredeemables) for new $5\%$ redeemable annuities.
- Nowadays call this a mutual fund, a securitization, or a pass-through.
- The South Sea Co was nothing but government annuities.
Cash: coupons from government debt
- South Sea sells new shares to the public, uses the new money to buy the securities
- Take old securities, gives to the gov't in exchange for new $5\%$ annuities.
- In practice, South Sea Co. sold some shares to new investors for cash, and sold some shares in exchange for debt – the existing irredeemables (32-yr & 99-yr) and redeemables.
# South Sea "Exchange" As "Securitization"

Initially, Public directly own government debt (red = notional, blue = per year)

- Expensive (£1.765mn interest, 5.59%)
- Government wishes to refinance (5% or £1.56mn interest)
![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-15/c441f5b5-2f9d-4e4d-bc65-1301c02e96db/ef88feb15e2c17d42c3e3d3b953e35dd9153885e55014c5cc1f37687a730bb78.jpg)
South Sea wins right to sell shares to public, gives old bonds to get new annuities
- Sell shares for combination of cash and old bonds
- Trades old bonds for new annuities
![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-15/c441f5b5-2f9d-4e4d-bc65-1301c02e96db/0e63248a538b0a4c364a8328de5b244ed3b88d93c7477b01d6e0c3999fadb1ed.jpg)
Final result is a "securitization": South Sea takes in gov't payments, passes it out
- But only gets £1.56mn from gov't, needs to pay out £1.765mn (or more)
- Simple: use cash for "Other Activities" - If they exist!!!
![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-15/c441f5b5-2f9d-4e4d-bc65-1301c02e96db/2216c8f9bd3c88ff8eb81b31cbed4bc99f2dc6bf0d0ab94df44ddf340bf86ad9.jpg)
# Exchange Impossible - Kindleberger'S "Swindle"

South Sea had 31.6mn shares to sell

- Equal to nominal value of debt they were trying to exchange
- Two objectives, could not do both
NaN. Sell shares for a high price, so public would give old bonds
Particularly irredeemables
- Irredeemables paid about $6.67\%$, current market rates $5\%$
- PV 133 (6.67/0.05), trying to replace with new annuities PV 100
![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-15/c441f5b5-2f9d-4e4d-bc65-1301c02e96db/7f81d0bee54e4d613cfe918ad6dcc4bc3b2c8a7440a0f6831eac0112e53a35f8.jpg)
NaN. Sell shares for a "fair market price" supported by dividend they could pay
- Dividend paid from income from new $5\%$ annuities
- Implies price £100 (£1/share 31.6mn shares = £31.6mn = £1.58mn/0.05)
![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-15/c441f5b5-2f9d-4e4d-bc65-1301c02e96db/b64cd550a10ed1c919494da1832bc292e070e4d78bf2ef203746a7ef535a33e1.jpg)
# What Happens When Selling Shares At £100 Vs £300

Sell shares at £1 (100): 31.6mn "shares" for £31.6mn debt

- No dividend from "Other": Exchange - not ok; South Sea market price - ok
![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-15/c441f5b5-2f9d-4e4d-bc65-1301c02e96db/de36179bda1f14151cfbeff614d7e8b39e85637d9c6065bdb8a466d9cd9e6958.jpg)
Sell shares at £3 (300): 31.6mn "shares": £31.6mn debt, £63.3mn for "Other"
- "Other" was swindle: Exchange - ok; South Sea market price - not ok
![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-15/c441f5b5-2f9d-4e4d-bc65-1301c02e96db/ea369d7ce3fa2e8071202804e85ac2a1ee2304f45239e2f1c1ad763d34151b09.jpg)
# Eventually Had To Crash

Eventually had to come back to underlying value, underlying cash flow £1.58mn

CF £1.58mn / yr, market rates 5%, PV £31.6mn

- 31.6mn "shares" for £31.6mn debt, at price of £100 (£1 per share)
- PV of South Sea CFs was about £100
In the end, the South Sea Company did indeed emerge as the single largest holder of the national debt, with £33 million out of a total of around £54 million, but little good it did. (Macdonald p 226)

[Macdonald(2006)] p 216

Half its capital converted to annuities in 1723, most of the rest in

1733. Nonetheless, Britain's financial system survived – in fact grew and flourished

Understanding what happened with the South Sea Bubble is confusing. The way I make sense of it is to think as a "Securitization":

	- Idea was to exchange the existing debt (mainly the irredeemables) for new $5\%$ redeemable annuities.

	- What we would now call a mutual fund, a securitization, or a pass-through.

	- Really important to understand that although the South Sea Co was selling shares in itself, there really was nothing "there" except the existing and proposed government annuities. The only cash generated by the South Sea Co was the coupons from the underlying government debt

	- South Sea (or Bank of England) issue new securities, sells those to the public, and uses the new money to buy the securities

	- Take the old securities, gives them to the government in exchange for new $5\%$ annuities.

	- In practice, South Sea Co. sold some shares to new investors for cash, and sold some shares in exchange for debt – the existing irredeemables (32-yr & 99-yr) and redeemables.

	- No problem if they sell those 31.6mn shares for price of £1 each (or 100 as they are usually quoted). Then the South Sea Co simply has £31.6mn of old debt, exchanges it for £31.6mn of new debt, and has that $5\%$ coupon to pay out a dividend of $5\%$.

	- But what actually happened was that they sold for much more than 1 (100). 300 in May. 600 in end-May. 950 in June.

	- Say that they sold all shares at 3 (300). Then they would need to sell only 10.533mn shares to get in £31.6mn and use that for the debt. OK so far. But now they sell the remaining 21.0667mn shares to generate £63.3mn, making the whole company worth £94.8mn.

	- But the only revenue they have is the government income from the annuity, £1.58mn.

	- That only pays dividend of $1.7\%$ on the 394.8mn that people have paid in to the company.

	- This is way too low a dividend, when market rates are closer to $5\%$.

	- Basically, the future cash flows of the company make it worth only £31.6mn, the value of the government debt. The additional £63.3mn is thrown away unless the South Sea Co can figure out something useful to do with it. And that was not their scheme. Their scheme was to raise the money, not put it to any use (except buying government debt.)

# Aftermath - South Sea Co A Shell

After 1720 South Sea Co nothing more than a government debt holding company.

Largest holder of debt: £33mn out of £54mn

Half its capital converted to annuities in 1723

Most of the rest in 1733

```latex
\begin{document}
\begin{tabular}{|c|c|}
\hline end-1720 & Holding \\ \hline
South Sea Co & £33 \\ \hline
Bank of England & £9 \\ \hline
East India & £3.9 \\ \hline
Unknown & £8.1 \\ \hline
Total & £54 \\ \hline
\end{tabular}
\end{document}
```

British government on path for sustainable, liquid, transparent, deep debt markets

- Consols (Consolidated Stock or Consolidated Perpetuities) first issued
1751. In 1752 all outstanding redeemables converted into $3.5\%$ Consolidated Stock
	- Originally £9mn, by 1780s £100mn. Large, liquid market
I bought $4\%$ consols in 1990s because I wanted part of history
	- Although mine were from 1927, part of refunding WW I debt
	- Mine got redeemed Feb
1752. The 2 1/2s in July
1753. End of history

After 1720 the South Sea Co was nothing more than a securitized government debt holding company. Half its capital converted to annuities in 1723, most of the rest in

1733. In the end, the South Sea Company did indeed emerge as the single largest holder of the national debt, with £33 million out of a total of around £54 million, but little good it did. By this stage [September 1720 or shortly after] the company was largely a shell. In 1723, half its capital was converted into perpetual annuities since its new shareholders had little interest in anything more than the regular receipt of their reduced incomes. A decade later, most of the remaining capital was similarly converted, and in 1750, the company ceased trading and became a mere appendage of the Exchequer. [Macdonald(2006), 226]

England government debt markets set on path for sustainable, liquid, transparent, deep market

I. Consols (Consolidated Stock or Consolidated Perpetuities) first issued

1734. In 1752 all outstanding redeemables converted into $3.5\%$ Consolidated Stock

(A) According to Macdonald (p. 244) originally £9mn, by 1780s £100mn. Large, liquid market.

# Aftermath - Losses

Original (pre-1720) holders of government debt lost

- 99-year annuitants: worth $25\%$ less (their annuities had been so highly valued in the exchange)
32-year annuities: received only $43\%$ of their income, but now in perpetuity
- If mature 1737 (see p. 187): PV(£1 annuity for 17 years @ 5%) = £11.274; PV(£0.43 annuity for 17 years @ 5%) = £4.848. But PV(£0.43 perpetuity @ 5% = £8.60
- So lose roughly $24\%$. Roughly same as 99-yr?
- Redeemable bondholders lost nearly $50\%$
Who gained?
- Government, and those who bought South Sea low, sold for cash
Ultimately, transfer from one group (pre-1720 debt holders) to others (gov't, speculators)
# Losses ([Macdonald(2006), 225]
- 99-year annuitants: worth $25\%$ less (their annuities had been so highly valued in the exchange)
- 32-year annuities: received only $43\%$ of their income, but now in perpetuity
- If mature 1737 (see p. 187): PV(£1 annuity for 17 years @ 5%) = £11.274; PV(£0.43 annuity for 17 years @ 5%) = £4.848. But PV(£0.43 perpetuity @ 5% = £8.60
- So lose roughly $24\%$. Roughly same as 99-yr?
- Redeemable bondholders lost nearly $50\%$
# 2 Mississippi Co - Paris 1715-1720
# 2.1 French Debt & Political Turmoil
# Contents
# France: A Story Of Failed Financial Restructuring

Like Britain, France had debts

Like Britain, debt and political crises triggered a speculative bubble

Unlike Britain, Mississippi was not simply a fraud

Unlike Britain, France's financial system failed to transform

- expensive borrowing
- illiquid and opaque markets
Important long-term consequences
A wounded but robust British financial system survived the shock, although legislation passed during the crisis stunted the development of Britain's corporate sector for a century. Armed with its
(mostly) modern financial system, Great Britain won all its wars save one between 1688 and 1815, traversed the first industrial revolution, built a worldwide empire, and preserved constitutional government. Devoid of a modern financial system, France lost its wars with Britain, su ered through a protracted and bloody political revolution, lost a colony to slaves, and endured Bonaparte's dictatorship.
A wounded but robust British financial system survived the shock, although legislation passed during the crisis stunted the development of Britain's corporate sector for a century. Armed with its
(mostly) modern financial system, Great Britain won all its wars save one between 1688 and 1815, traversed the first industrial revolution, built a worldwide empire, and preserved constitutional government. Devoid of a modern financial system, France lost its wars with Britain, suered through a protracted and bloody political revolution, lost a colony to slaves, and endured Bonaparte's dictatorship. [Sylla et al. (2009) Sylla, Wright, and Cowen] p 63
# Debt And Political Turmoil

Colbert (1661-1683) had left France in decent fiscal position

By 1715, Louis XIV death (Wars of Spanish Succession) debt-to-GDP $100\%+$

- $100\%$ of GDP is big today but France in 1715 unsustainable
Partial default during 1710 & 1715/16
- "Traditional" methods of repudiating debt
- Reduce rentes
(annuities) to $5\%$ in 1710, then $4\%$ in 1715 - value down to maybe $67\%$
- Chamber de Justice "examined" debt-holders, repudiated debt
Debt down maybe 1/3 (down to 2,000mn litres) - not enough
Also manipulating money - example 1716 recoinage - $20\%$ debasement
- Bring in 1,000 coins (gold or silver) and get back 1,000 coins but only $80\%$ of the metal
Prior to 1710
- Colbert (Chief minister 1661-1683) had left France in a good positoni (Macdonald 184 )
- Wars of Spanish Succession left France virtually bankrupt, particularly burdened by short-term debt (Macdonald $p_{183}$)
1710-1716 - Debt & Default
- Substantial debts left over from reign of Louis XIV and Wars of Spanish Succession (NB - not Napoleonic Wars, as Aliber says)
- Roughly 3,000mn litres - almost certainly more than $100\%$ of GDP ([Macdonald(2006)] p 186 - GNP roughly 2.4bn litres or $\pounds 150mn$ at current exchange rates)
Debt levels of $100\%$ are big today (US about $125\%$ now, for 1955-2008 $60 - 90\%$ ) but France in 1720 (less-developed financial markets) this was impossible
- Partial default during 1710 & 1715/16
Reduce rentals to $5\%$ in 1710, then $4\%$ in 1715 - value down to maybe $67\%$
Chamber de Justice "examined" debt-holders and their debt, repudiated
Debt down maybe 1/3 (down to 2,000mn litres)
1716 recoinage - 20% debasement
- Bring in 1,000 coins (gold or silver) and get back 1,000 coins but with only $80\%$ of the original metal
# 2.2 John Law & Mississippi Co Narrative
# Contents
# Law Was Visionary - Monetary & Fiscal Objectives

France needed reform

John Law was not simply a gambler (he was – apparently a good one) or a scoundrel (he may have been). A vision for monetary and fiscal reform

MONETARY

- Introduce paper money
-E ciency - Supplement & substitute for coins
- Monetary stimulus - expand money (paper money)
- Banque Generale
- Later, Banque Royale
FISCAL (Gov't funding)
Government borrowing and taxation
- Reform taxation
- Consolidate debt
- Lower rates (from $10\%$ to $2\%$ )
- Mississippi Company (Compaigne de l'Occident, Company of the Indies)
Picture of Banque Generale, Banque Royal, Mississippi

```latex
\begin{document}
\begin{tabular}{|c|c|c|}
\hline
 & BANQUE GENERALE & MISSISSIPPI CO. \\ \hline
1716 & Founded. Issues Notes (first paper money). Stable, backed by specie, convertible & \\ \hline
1717 & Notes accepted for payment of taxes & Acquired by John Law. Little business \\ \hline
1719 & Converted to Banque Royale, public bank (gov’t bank). Notes not backed by specie. & Builds business: acquires Mint, trading cos (China, India, Africa). Taxes (Farmers General). Issues shares to redeem gov’t debt. Shares issued at 5,000, rise to 10,000 \\ \hline
1720 & Banque Royale and Mississippi Co. mergerd. Law tries to support shares at 9,000 by having Banque Royale buy shares. Inflates (and debases) money. By end 1720, Mississippi Co. shares back down to 100l & \\ \hline
\end{tabular}
\end{document}
```

- May 1716 - Law & Co. or Banque Generale
- 6mn livres, shares of 500 livres each - payable only 125 in specie
(coin) and 375 gov't notes (billets d'etat) which were really worth about 125
- Private bank, issuing private notes that served as "money"
- Notes redeemable into specie on demand
- Paper Money - See "Digression on Money"
- Seems to have been well-managed, with BG notes trading at premium to specie (more valuable than coins)
- Early 1717 - Banque Generale notes authorized for tax payments
Sign of notes being well-managed
Made notes more "money-like"
- August 1717 - Concession of Compagnie dl'Occident (Mississippi company) (Macdonald p 194)
- The right
(monopoly) to colonize lands in the Americas
- But in reality a gov't debt consolidation
- Took in billets d'etat, gave to gov't, got $4\%$ annuity (rente perpetuelle)
- 200,000 shares, 500 livres each, payable in billets d'etat
Not very valuable - really just the $4\%$ annuity
- Worth the same at the original billets d'etat - trading at about 200 litres (60% discount)
- Late 1718, Paris brothers buy Farmers General (tax authority)
- Taxation was privately run
- somewhat like privatizing Chicago parking
- Paid 48.5mn litres/year
- Had a true source of profits - real value (as opposed to Mississippi Co)
- Hold large amount of Banque Royale notes - could cause trouble for Law by presenting for conversion
- January 1,1719 - Banque Generale becomes Banque Royale
- Goes from private to public
- Original shareholders paid back full capital even though only paid part (83%) - big profits for shareholders
- BG notes private, backed by specie originally paid in
- BR notes public (gov't debt), not backed by specie, but still convertible
- For later reference, Banque Royale note issuance:
May 1719 160mn Massive monetary
Mid-October 1719 620mn expansion: will lead
May 1720 2,700mn to inflation
- Early 1719 - Law realizes he needs control of 3 levers of finance
- Monetary issuance (Banque Royale) which he has
- Coinage (converting silver into higher-value coins - seignorage)
Tax Collection
- Got these, by hook or crook (promise Regent and courtiers benefits)
- May 1719, acquired new sources of business - chartered trading companies (East Indies, China, Africa)
- July 1719, minting monopoly
(coinage) - June & July 1719 100,000 share issuance
- Increased shares from 200k to 300k
- 40,000 shares went to crown or couriers (political insiders)
- Issues price
(July) 1000 livres
- August, September, October 1719 - Buy taxes and gov't debt
- O ers 52mn livers/yr for Farmers General
(taxes) - O ers 1,200mn litres to gov't (paying $3\%$ ) to redeem rentes
- Need to pay $4\%$ dividend (since existing rents earn $4\%$ )
- Issue 300,000 shares, price 5000 livres (1,500mn livres)
Important - buy on credit - payable monthly (20mth or 10mth)

# Contents
- Mississippi prices (for later reference)

- Year-end 1719,10,000 livres - magic number that gets $2\%$ - see below
- Price of 10,000 livres not sustainable
- Mississippi Co revenue did not justify such a price - nor gov't debt (at $4\%$, not $2\%$ - see below)
- Started to fall early 1720
- Partly because people started to cash in as they had to pay for shares
- Early 1720, various edicts to support value of money
- February 1720, ban holding more than 500 livres of coins
- Caused big problems in business, disruption, opposition
- June 1720, coin
(specie) allowed back into circulation
- Interesting to contrast with 1933 in US, when the US went o gold and Roosevelt banned the private holding of gold - until the 1970s (I think)
In the US there was no big disruption, no push-back
Maybe because the banking system was better developed, with the Federal Reserve issuing notes and demand deposits at private banks
- By end-1720, shares at 100 livres (down from 1000)
# Older Material

I. August 1719 Law announced the plan to consolidate French debt

(A) The Mississippi Co (India Co by this time) lends the King money - 1,200mn I - and the King pays $3 \%$

(B) The crown uses the money to redeem its other debts

(C) The Mississippi Co gets the money from selling shares

(D) The Mississippi Co pays shareholders dividends out of revenue from the business

1. Have to or er roughly the same as existing rents - in other words $4\%$.
2. Pay this out of revenue - gov't debt + businesses (mint, taxes, trade)
II. Want to reduce yield down to $2\%$. But only way to do that is by having prices double after people buy them: speculation
# Law'S Fiscal Objectives - Debt Consolidation

Debt consolidation (August 1719) was the capstone of Law's "System" – and the trigger for the speculative bubble (Macdonald p 198)

Consolidate & restructure debt through Mississippi Co. Three objectives:

I. Convert existing debt into Mississippi Co. shares

II. Bring long-term gov't interest rate down to $2\%$ (from $10\%$ )

III. Ensure profits to crown & courtiers

(insiders) (2) impossible - risk premium for French debt - past defaults and expropriations

(3) we see with Calomiris & Haber - autocratic (but weak) governments hand out benefits to powerful political forces

These objectives fundamentally inconsistent

- For (1) & (2) needed to issue shares (1719) at low price
- For (3) needed to issues shares at high price - so don't dilute existing shareholders
(courtiers) August 1719 Law announced the plan to consolidate French debt
The Mississippi Co (India Co by this time) lends the King money - 1,200mn I - and the King pays $3 \%$
- The crown uses the money to redeem its other debts
- The Mississippi Co gets the money from selling new shares
- The Mississippi Co pays shareholders dividends out of revenue from the business
- Have to or er roughly the same as existing rentals - in other words $4\%$.
- Pay this out of revenue - gov't debt + businesses (mint, taxes, trade)
Extraordinary situation - Mississippi Co now runs the whole economy
- Collects taxes (Tax Farm)
Now let's look at how this might work.
Total revenue about 90mn I
I. Want to reduce yield down to $2\%$. But only way to do that is by having prices double after people buy them: speculation
# Mississippi Co Runs Gov'T Finances - "Gov'T Stock"

Debt Consolidation (August 1719) - also a "Securitization" - but not a fraud

- Mississippi Co raises 1,500mnl, lends crown 1,200mnl at $3\%$ (36mnl)
- Crown uses money to redeem debts
(rentes) Extraordinary situation - Mississippi Co now collects taxes, mint revenue, tari s
![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-15/c441f5b5-2f9d-4e4d-bc65-1301c02e96db/b4a9bdac00b95907d6d125888bef7cf90c6a33861d04fd4b13c257907c4536db.jpg)
Total profits (after giving some to crown) 90mml
# To Raise 1,500Mn: Sell At 2500|: Successsell At 5000|: Failure

Raise 1,500mn, need to pay $\sim 4\%$ (match rentes)

Profit from all businesses about 90mn

- With 900k shares (600k new at 2500l) this will work
- BUT - need to ensure profits to existing holders (crown & courtiers)
- Sell fewer new shares at higher price - does not work
Sell 600k at 2500l, everything works
300k existing shares
New money: $2500^{*}600 = 1,500mn$
Total value: $2500^{*}900 = 2,250mn$ required $4 \%$ dividend $= 0.04^{*}2,5001 = 1001$ actual dividend
(l) = 90mnl / 900k sh = 100l actual dividend $(\%) = 90\text{mml} /2,250\text{mml} = 4\%$
Sell 300k at 5000l, does not work
300k existing shares
New money: $5000 \times 300 = 1,500mn$
Total value: $5000^{*}600 = 3,000mn$ required $4 \%$ dividend $= 0.04^{*}5,0001 = 2001$ actual dividend
(l) = 90mnl / 600k sh = 150l actual dividend $(\%) = 90\mathrm{mnl} / 3,000\mathrm{mnl} = 3\%$

# Two Scenarios To Raise 1,500Mn
# Objectives (August 1719)

I. Convert debt, raise $\sim 1,500\mathrm{mn}$; pay $\sim 4\%$ (match rentals)

II. Bring yield to $2\%$ - shares have to double (low price)

III. Ensure profits to insiders - don't dilute (high price)

# Problem: Must Pay $4\%$ Dividend
- Roughly 90mn I profit in business
Hypothetical $\mathsf{P} = 2500\mathsf{I}$
- Would have supported div=100I, 4%
- better for 1 & 2 not for 3
Actual: 5000I
Cannot support div=200l, 4%
Even worse with $\mathbf{P} = 10,000I$ (after speculation)

# Bring Yield To $2\%$: Speculation & Bubble
# Objectives (August 1719)

I. Convert debt, raise $\sim 1,500\mathrm{mn}$; pay $\sim 4\%$ (match rentes)

II. Bring yield to $2\%$ - shares have to double (low price)

III. Ensure profits to insiders - don't dilute (high price)

# Only Way To Get Yield To $2\%$ Price Double
- Speculation
# From 5000I Price Must Go To 10,000I
- But original 5000/ price impossible
# Had To Crash Eventually

```latex
\begin{document}
\begin{tabular}{|c|c|}
\hline
August 1717 - begin-1719 & 2001 \\ \hline
July 29 1719 & 15001 \\ \hline
August 1719 & 30001 \\ \hline
Year-end 1719 & 10,0001 \\ \hline
Year-end 1720 & 1001 \\ \hline
\end{tabular}
\end{document}
```

I. Now let's examine the issue of new shares after August 1719. (Macdonald p 199)

(A) There were 300k shares existing - Law wants to give profits to those shareholders

(B) Need to raise $\sim 1,500\mathrm{mn}$ I.

1. Either lots of low-priced shares or fewer high-priced shares
II. Can raise either lots of new shares at low price (2500l) or few new shares at high price (5000l). Conflict
(A) Few new shares mean that insiders own more of the company and so get more of any profits that are distributed
2. High price & few new shares (5000l) gives $50\%$ owned by existing shareholders, but requires 120mn I of profits to get to $4 \%$ on original price
3. Macdonald (p 199) quotes existing estimates of profits from the business at 90mn I. That would have
(roughly) supported $4\%$ yield if o ered at 2500 I.
(B) Low price: Makes it easier to pay $4\%$ (convert, do (1)) & for price rise (2)
4. Less profit goes to the existing shareholders, so less profit needs to be distributed
III. In the end, Law went with the high price. Looks like he had to "buy-o" insiders.
(A) I think this is maybe the fundamental issue that doomed the undertaking
(B) Although there was a real lack of understanding of monetary economics
# 3 Britain & France Comparison
# Contents
# Extraordinary Accomplishments, Extraordinary Di Culties

Law's failure should not obscure his vision, or his extraordinary accomplishment

- For a short time (1719-1720) he had all government revenue (taxes, mint, trade) and all debt in his company
Britain's South Sea & France's Mississippi very di erent
Some bubbles are swindles, some are not. The Mississippi Bubble was not a swindle; the South Sea Bubble was.
(Kindleberger) But Britain's political system was open to transforming finance
When the first proposal was discussed in 1715, royal advisers objected that a bank would never work under an absolute monarchy, because the king would always be tempted to raid the bank. (Velde 2007)
Britain's Parliament was populated by those who banked and lent
# Contrast Mississippi With South Sea Cos.

Some bubbles are swindles, some are not. The Mississippi Bubble was not a swindle; the South Sea Bubble was.

(Kindleberger) ```latex

\begin{document}

\begin{tabular}{|c|c|c|}

\hline

 & Mississippi & South Sea \\ \hline

Underlying Business? & Yes. Tax. Mint. Trade & No. Spain wouldn&#$x_{27}$;t allow trade to South America \\ \hline

Monetary Reform? & Y - Banque Generale &amp; Banque Royale. Paper money & No \\ \hline

Bond "Mutual Fund" & Yes & Yes \\ \hline

Bribery & Probably & Yes \\ \hline

Need to "buy-o" autocratic gov't & Yes & No \\ \hline

Swindle & No & Yes \\ \hline

Economics stimulus & Yes & No \\ \hline

\end{tabular}

\end{document}

```
# Easy To Look Back & Laugh
South Sea & Mississippi Co both about consolidating gov't debt
- South Sea Co. was just "gov't debt mutual fund"
Hard to understand why such a big event
- We shouldn't laugh - We understand gov't finances & debt better
- Big transformation at the time
Back to Kindleberger's "Displacement & Opportunity"
How Kindleberger / Minsky model might fit
```latex
\begin{document}
\begin{tabular}{|c|c|}
\hline
South Sea Bubble & Mississippi Co \\ \hline
Displacement &amp; Opportunity & \\ \hline
South Sea Co (1711): trade with South Sea
(worthless) ; gov&#$x_{27}$;t debt restructuring (1720) & Monetary: notes &amp; paper money (Banque Royale, 1717-1719). Fiscal: Gov&#$x_{27}$;t debt restructuring; tax reform; trade (India, Africa, etc.); Louisiana colony (Mississippi Co, 1717-1719) \\ \hline
Monetary Expansion & \\ \hline
Sword Bank - lent money to buy South Sea shares & Banque Royale - printed &amp; lent money to buy Mississippi Co shares \\ \hline
Speculation &amp; Asset Price Inflation & \\ \hline
August 1720 up to £1000 - intrinsic value maybe £125 & Year-end 1719 10,000I - intrinsic maybe 2500I \\ \hline
Crisis &amp; Debt Deflation; Renewal &amp; Recovery & \\ \hline
1721 £100. Huge fortunes made &amp; lost. Lasting impact on gov&#$x_{27}$;t financing small. (Bubble Act until 1824) & Year-end 1720 1000I. Fortunes made &amp; lost. After-shocks for French finance throughout 18th c \\ \hline
\end{tabular}
\end{document}
```

Some additional thoughts:

- Bubble Act 1720 - forbade joint-stock companies (limited liability companies) except by royal charter (act of parliament).
- Originally to protect the South Sea Co against competitors who might take away speculative interest
- Big contrast with today: we think of forming a company as a simple administrative action: pay some fee (maybe $50) and the state will grant a charter. It's automatic.
- Among other things this helped entrench the Bank of England's monopoly on banking in London, since other banks could only be partnerships limited to 6 partners. This ensured other banks were weak and fragile.
# "Financial Crisis As Mania" Is Tempting

Should not be lured into thinking that mania and speculation is the whole story Macdonald & Velde approach to John Law & Mississippi Co

- Britain: consolidation & transformation of gov't financing and market structure
- France: Law's Monetary & Fiscal objectives - truly radical for the time
- This view of financial crises as manias and speculative bubbles is seductive but as economists we want to think more deeply and carefully
- The approach of Macdonald & Velde to John Law & the Mississippi Co. is instructive. They look beneath the surface painted by Mackay & Kindleberger to uncover the economic forces that helped to drive the crises.
- Remember Law's to objectives (lecture 3): Monetary & Fiscal

```latex
\begin{document}
\begin{tabular}{|c|c|}
