```latex
MONETARY & FISCAL (gov&#x27;t funding) \\ \hline
Introduce paper money & Gov&#x27;t borrowing &amp; taxation \\ \hline
- E ciency - supplement &amp; substitute for coins & - Reform taxation \\ \hline
- Monetary stimuli - expand money (paper money) & - Consolidate debt \\ \hline
Banque Generale & - Lower gov&#x27;t rates (10% to 2%) \\ \hline
Banque Royale & Mississippi Co - consolidate gov&#x27;t revenue into traded company \\ \hline
\end{tabular}
\end{document}
```

# Easy To Look Back & Laugh

South Sea & Mississippi Co both about consolidating gov't debt

- South Sea Co. was just "gov't debt mutual fund"
Hard to understand why such a big event
- We shouldn't laugh - We understand gov't finances & debt better
- Big transformation at the time
Back to Kindleberger's "Displacement & Opportunity"
- At the time, debt consolidation was both mysterious and very important
- Transformation of debt markets could (and did) mean great things
A wounded but robust British financial system survived the shock… Armed with its
(mostly) modern financial system, Great Britain won all its wars save one between 1688 and 1815, traversed the first industrial revolution, built a worldwide empire, and preserved constitutional government. Devoid of a modern financial system, France lost its wars with Britain, su ered through a protracted and bloody political revolution, lost a colony to slaves, and endured Bonaparte's dictatorship.
# Underlying Economic & Public Finance Forces

A story of war and government finance

- Nine Year's War (1688-97); Wars of Spanish Succession (1702-1715)
Transformation in public finances - changes in England and France borrowing
- Britain - deep, transparent, ec tive, low-cost market
- France - a rather idiosyncratic, costly, shallow, inactive market.
See in three ways:
I. Borrowing capacity by later 18th c
II. Type of instruments & market
III. Defaults during 18th c
Macdonald puts this forward as a major strength of Britain
This was a period of wars and strain on public finances
I. 1688-97, the Nine Years War. Wars of Spanish Succession 1702-1715
(A) Fighting is expensive, and states usually cannot finance out of current tax revenue. So they borrow today against the promise of paying back during peaceful times.
(B) The basis of borrowing today is the ability to make a credible promise of paying back in the future.
(C) This is the story of the US in 1790 and the move from the Articles of Confederation to the Constitution. The Constitution gave the central government the revenue to pay back, and paying back in full established a reputation that has stood the US in good stead.
II. The outcome, the transformation, was very di erent for France and Britain.
(A) France was the larger, arguably more prosperous, country. But ultimately Britain transformed more successfully.
(B) Britain transformed its borrowing into a deep, transparent, ec tive, low-cost market for borrowing from a wide lending public. London became the undisputed financial capital of the world in the 19th century.
(C) France maintained a rather idiosyncratic, costly, shallow, ine ective market.
# Borrowing Capacity

The financial positions of France and Britain in 1788

(Macdonald p 241)

```latex
\begin{document}
\begin{tabular}{|c|c|c|}
\hline
£mn & France & Britain \\ \hline
GNP & £280mn & £135mn \\ \hline
Public Debt & £183mn & £245mn \\ \hline
- as % of GNP & 65% & 182% \\ \hline
Debt Service & £12.2mn & £8.1mn \\ \hline
- as % of GNP & 4.4% & 6.0% \\ \hline
E lective rate & 6.7% & 3.3% \\ \hline
\end{tabular}
\end{document}
```

- Britain more debt – lenders believed they would pay back
- Britain lower interest – lenders believed Britain was less risk
I. The first way we see the difference between the transformation of France vs Britain is to look at public finance in 1788
(A) The financial positions of France and Britain in 1788 ([Macdonald(2006)] p 241).

```latex
\begin{document}
\begin{tabular}{|c|c|c|}
\hline
£mn & France & Britain \\ \hline
GNP & £280mn & £135mn \\ \hline
Public Debt & £183mn & £245mn \\ \hline
- as % of GNP & 65% & 182% \\ \hline
Debt Service & £12.2mn & £8.1mn \\ \hline
- as % of GNP & 4.4% & 6.0% \\ \hline
E lective rate & 6.7% & 3.3% \\ \hline
\end{tabular}
\end{document}
```

(B) Britain could support more debt (£245mn vs £183mn) – lenders believed they would pay back and therefore were willing to lend

(C) Britain did not have to pay such high interest rates – again lenders believed Britain was less risk and so charged a lower risk premium

II. This reflects the lower risk premium and better reputation of Britain versus France. This better reputation was earned in multiple ways.

(A) A market with simple, liquid, interchangeable instruments (see below).

(B) A proven track-record of paying debts (refraining from default - see below)

(C) Better financial management, including transparency and publication of annual public accounts (Macdonald p. 232)

# Market Depth & Defaults

England had strong, deep, liquid, active market. France a weak market with mis-mash of instruments

Consols: liquid, all same

- France: Few perpetuals, term loans, life annuities (rentes viagères)
# Defaults
- England: last default 1594 (or maybe 1688 - Glorious Revolution)
France: 1701,1715,1770,1788. Haircuts (partial defaults) 1710,1715
I. The type of instruments and depth of market
(A) England had Consols (redeemable perpetuals) - every issue the same, freely tradeable, transparent market, low rates
1. Consols first issued
2. In 1752 all outstanding redeemables converted into $3.5\%$ Consolidated Stock i. According to Macdonald (p. 244) originally £9mn, by 1780s £100mn. Large, liquid market.
3. France had some perpetuals (discredited from the the Mississippi bubble in 1720) but mainly a mish-mash of term loans with quirks, and life annuities. Hard to value, hard to trade, expensive. ([Macdonald(2006)] p 244)
 i. After 1720, aversion to issue perpetuals (which was the instrument other nations used). Insisted on specified repayment. So no simple perpetuals. Which meant issues could not be substituted - each issue unique and idiosyncratic.
 ii. Usury limit: Fundamental problem for France - usury limit of $5\%$, but lenders demanded higher than $5\%$ for all the reasons discussed here (poor credit, illiquid market). So often France would issue at a discount. (Remember, if you buy a bond at a discount then the e-ective interest is higher. Say 5l coupon for a perpetuity. If $\mathsf{Prc} = 100$, $100 = \mathsf{PV}(5\mathsf{I} \circ 5\%) = 5 / 0.05$. But if $\mathsf{Prc} = 70$, $70 = \mathsf{PV}(5\mathsf{I} \circ 7.14\%) = 5 / 0.0714$. Lenders were smart enough to know this, and it was a cute way around the $5\%$ usury limit.)
 iii. Life annuities (rentes viagères, Macdonald p. 244): this was the biggest part of French borrowing ("after 1771 they dominated public borrowing" p 244). But horrible for making a deep & liquid market. The annuity would die with the buyer (would it redeem or simply disappear? I'm not sure). Taking idiosyncrasy of issues to the nth-degree: how could anyone trade a life annuity because it would depend on the life expectancy of the seller. Adam Smith in Wealth of Nations: "No man will give for an annuity upon the life of another, whose age and state of health are nearly the same as his own, the same price that he would give for one upon his own… It can never, therefore, make so convenient a transferrable stock as a perpetual annuity, of which the real value may be supposed always the same, or very nearly the same." (Book 5,3, p. 7)
(B) Number of defaults during 18th c
4. England - last default was 1594 (or maybe 1688 Glorious Revolution). But nothing after. Britain had "graduated" from serial default
5. France another story entirely - the 18th century saw repeated defaults. According to Reinhart & Rogo, table 6.1, France defaulted 1701,1715,1770,1788. (But caution on exact dating - R&R sometimes not as careful as we would wish. I think France had haircuts - partial defaults - 1710 & 1715.1701 I have not looked carefully enough. And when we come to banking crises, R&R list 1890 for US (table A0.4.1) but it was actually 1893 for US. 1890 Barings crisis - we will look at this)
# Democracy, Autocracy, & "Expropriation" - Rule Of Law

Digression on politics & political systems

- Macdonald (Free Nation) increased borrowing under democracy
Calomiris & Haber (Fragile by Design) Democracy vs Autocracy
- Acemoglu & Robinson (Why Nations Fail) Inclusive vs Extractive
Same idea: importance of political system, temptation to expropriate
When the first proposal was discussed in 1715, royal advisers objected that a bank would never work under an absolute monarchy, because the king would always be tempted to raid the bank. (Velde 2007)
An establishment of this sort may be good in itself; but it is only so in a republic or a monarchy like England, whose finances are controlled by those alone who furnish them, and who only furnish as much as they please. But in a State which is weak, changeable, and absolute, like France, stability must necessarily be wanting to it; since the King, or in his name a mistress, a minister or favorites, or still more, such extreme necessities as we find in the years 1707-1710, may overthrow the Bank - the temptation to which would be too great, and at the same time too easy. Duc de Saint-Simon
(contemporary) I. Democracy & Enfranchisement - Inclusion vs Expropriation
(A) An important theme which crops up in various writings – here in Macdonald, the central theme of Calomiris & Haber, the central thesis of Acemoglu & Robinson – is the importance of political institutions, the rule of law, and the distinction between democracy and autocracy
II. US 1790 – one really important point: part of the reason it was made sense for the US to pay back (rather than just walk away from its debts) was because the lenders were voters – there was widespread enfranchisement – those lending to the government also had political power.
(A) This was the same in Britain following the Glorious Revolution of 1688
1. Not to say that there was a universal franchise - only men and only the upper classes could vote. But the important point is that the government represented interests of merchants and landowners, not just the king and courtiers.
(B) But not true in France.
(C) A problem with autocratic systems - temptation to expropriate. Democracy and widespread franchise tends to make this harder.
2. Two important quotes regarding France and the suitability of John Law's system.
When the first proposal [for a bank] was discussed in 1715, royal advisers objected that a bank would never work under an absolute monarchy, because the king would always be tempted to raid the bank. (Velde 2007)
An establishment of this sort may be good in itself; but it is only so in a republic or a monarchy like England, whose finances are controlled by those alone who furnish them, and who only furnish as much as they please. But in a State which is weak, changeable, and absolute, like France, stability must necessarily be wanting to it; since the King, or in his name a mistress, a minister or favorites, or still more, such extreme necessities as we find in the years 1707-1710, may overthrow the Bank - the temptation to which would be too great, and at the same time too easy. Duc de Saint-Simon
(contemporary) # 4 Digression - Securitization
# Contents
# Securitization - Pooling Assets & Cash Flows

Pooling of assets and repackaging of the underlying cashflows

Pooling Entity

Company or SPV

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-15/c441f5b5-2f9d-4e4d-bc65-1301c02e96db/167d056c81564fd2f9a6108621d13e09cc7be6ef96cd3bdf180b6e21e0a43094.jpg)

# Modern Examples
- Mutual Funds, hedge funds, Exchange Traded Funds
- Mortgage-backed and asset-back securities
Common Stocks
Reasons for Separation - in 1720 not strict legal separation
- Spreads & Diversifies risk (mortgage-backed bonds)
- Lowers cost (mutual funds); professional management (hedge funds)
- Helps monitor and discipline (maybe South Sea & Mississippi)
- Widens audience and market
# Securitization
- Pooling of assets and repackaging of the underlying cashflows
Often into bonds
- Sometimes "trancheing" to create di erent payment or credit characteristics from those of the underlying assets
# Securitization Assets:
- Assets that go into the pool
Often fixed income instruments such as mortgages or loans or corporate bonds or credit card receivables
# Securitization Liabilities:

Often bonds but also mutual funds, common stocks

- Sometimes structured in tranches ordered in terms of payment priority
# Examples
- Mutual Funds
Hedge Funds
Mortgage-backed securities
- Pass-through (Agency - Fannie Mae & Freddie Mac - pass-throughs). Simply pool together a group of mortgages and pass through the income - payment of income and principal.
- Tanched. Pool together a group of mortgages but sell di erent
- Other Asset-Backed securities - student loans, credit card receivables, even David Bowie's bond (1997 - revenues from 25 albums recorded before 1990)
Common Stocks
- Not usually talked about as a securitization or pooled assets, but in many ways that is exactly what a share in a publicly-listed company is – a share in the cash flows generated by the pooled activities from the company.
- Usually we talk about securitization of passive assets (things like other bonds or mortgages) and not active assets like for an on-going business.
- This ties back nicely to the South Sea Co and the Mississippi Co, although the South Sea Co was truly a pooling of passive assets - simply government annuities
# Legal Structure
- Nowadays strict legal separation between manager and pool of underlying assets
- Mutual Funds in US
- Investment Company which holds assets and sells shares in itself - proportionate share of cash flows. Investment Co Act 1940 - sets rules and regulations for Investment Co structure and operations
- Investment Manager who manages the assets of the Investment Company. But a legally separate entity that cannot take the assets from the Investment Company. Investment Advisors Act 1940 - rules and regulations for managers
- For South Sea Co there was not any legal separation – people gave the South Sea Co. their money (or their existing government debt) on the promise that the South Sea Co had a good on-going business plan. A promise which was actually a fraud.
# Reasons For Securitization
- Spreads & Diversifies risk
- Big reason for mortgage-backed bonds. If you invest $100k in one mortgage then if the homeowner defaults you are out. There may be only a 5% chance but on that you lose everything. But if you invest $100k in a mortgage-backed bond with 1,000 mortgages then there'sa pretty good chance that 5% will default but then you only lose 5%, and in any case you can price that in up-front because it becomes pretty certain how much you will lose (5% of your investment) rather than all or nothing.
- Lowers cost of access (mutual funds) or provides professional management expertise (hedge funds)
- One of the biggest reasons for mutual funds and ETFs
- Helps monitor and discipline (maybe South Sea & Mississippi)
- This may have been a reasons for the South Sea Co and the Mississippi Co. Although they did it very poorly - in fact the South Sea managers were really fraudsters.
- But the Bank of England that managed the government debt, while not really pooling the government assets, played this role.
- Not something we think is necessary today for government debt, at least not in developed countries. But in 1720 could have been very valuable.
- Widens audience and market
# References

2006. ISBN 978-0-691-12632-6.

2007. The Business History Review, 83(1):61-86,2009. ISSN 0007-6805. URL http://www.jstor.org/stable/40538573.

# Bubble And Panic

The Economist focuses on first Bank of the United States

(BUS) - Only part of the story, and maybe not most important

# Bus Shares
- $10mn shares, July 1791, $25 "scrip" for $400 share
- Scrip went from $25 in July to $300 in August 1791
Actually two panics (not highlighted by The Economist)
- Mini-panic or Bank Scrip Bubble, August 1791
- William Duer and "Company", spring 1792, attempted "take-over" of BONY and corner bond market
The Economist narrative for 1792, supplemented by [Sargent(2011), Sylla et al.(2009)Sylla, Wright, and Cowen, Sylla(2010)]:

# 1.1 Economist Story
# Contents
# Economist: Alexander Hamilton'S Vision For Us Finance

In 1790, US financial system rudimentary, five banks, few insurers

- "Hamilton wanted a state-of-the-art financial set-up, like that of Britain or Holland"
- Britain and Holland were examples of "Dutch Finance" - financing government through public borrowing
- Hamilton knew the story of Law & Mississippi bubble – weak French finance
Plan: "federal debt pull together individual states' IOUs" plus a central bank
- First Bank of the United States
(BUS) $10mn in BUS shares, $8mn to public, shares @ $400
- Buy partly cash, partly US bonds
- Initially o ered July 4,1791. Buy $25 share certificate or "scrip" - essentially option to pay additional$ 375 for full share
- Scrip prices went from $25 in July to $300 August 11th 1791
- Crashed back to $130 by mid-August
# Six Critical Components Of Modern Financial Systems

Sylla: When Hamilton left o ce in 1795, his adopted homeland harbored six critical institutional components that characterize modern financial systems:

I. stable public finances and debt management;

II. stable money;

III. an e ective central bank;

IV. a functioning banking system;

V. active securities markets;

VI. a growing number of business corporations, financial and non-financial

Six critical components: [Sylla(2010)] p 62

# Economist: Scheming William Duer Interferes

William Duer (old Etonian!) schemes to corner market in federal bonds

- Duer "knew that investors needed federal bonds to pay for their BUS shares, so they tried to corner the market"
Other problem: bank itself, injected money & credit - created "bubble"
- Bubble burst, March 1792
- Hamilton "bailed out" banks and investors
Economist's narrative sort-of (but not really) correct
- Two panics (1791 & 1792)
- Deeper (and more interesting) issues
![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-15/c441f5b5-2f9d-4e4d-bc65-1301c02e96db/f619134f5ac02c9c01d80deeaaf69493bc15ef70bc230fec1d99f850cb788b52.jpg)
# 1792 And Bank Of Us

The Economist narrative for 1792, supplemented by [Sargent(2011), Sylla et al.(2009)Sylla, Wright, and Cowen, Sylla(2010)]:

- Focuses on the first Bank of the United States
(BUS) - Only part of the story, and arguably not the most important part
- BUS shares
- $10mn of BUS shares, $8mn to the public.
 - Initially o ered July 4,1791. $400/share, needed to buy $25 share certificate or "scrip" - essentially option to pay additional $375 for full share
 - Scrip prices went from $25 in July to $300 in August 1791
- Actually, two panics. Not highlighted by The Economist but pretty important
- Mini-panic or Bank Scrip Bubble, August
1791. Primarily in scrip, which went from $25 up to $270 or even $300 by August
1792. Crashed back to $130 by mid-August
The BUS direct public o ering led to six weeks of heated financial speculation, the likes of which had never before been witnessed in America. Bank scraps purchased at $$ 25\$ quickly doubled in price and remained at that level for most of July. In early August, they soared, reaching a bid of 264-280 asked in New York on August 11, and reportedly more than 300 in Philadelphia that same evening. Then they tumbled, in Boston from 230 on August 12 to 112 on August 14, to 154-59 in New York on August 16, and to 125-37 in Philadelphia the same day, before rallying later that month. [Sylla et al. (2009)Sylla, Wright, and Cowen] p 71
	- William Duer and "Company" - spring 1792, speculation in bonds (rather than BUS scrip).
A rather complicated plan to borrow money, launch a new bank in NY and drive down the price of BONY and thus obtain control of BONY, and then corner the market for government bonds.
Traditionally, accounts of the bubble and crash of spring 1792 focus on William Duer, a New York speculator, well-connected businessman, erstwhile governmental o' cial in both the old and new governments, and personal friend of Hamilton. The scheming and operations of Duer and other members of his speculative "company" drove securities prices to new highs early in the year. In March, when Duer could not repay the large amounts of money he had borrowed to implement his plans, the market crashed and panic ensued. The scheming involved an attempt in January to launch a large new bank in New York City in order to drive down the price of BONY stock, which would allow Duer to obtain control of it. Then, Duer and his compatriots planned to corner the market for U. S. sixes, which subscribers to the BUS would need to meet their future payments for BUS shares. [Sylla et al. (2009) Sylla, Wright, and Cowen] p 73

# Winter / Spring 1792 - Duer Tries To Corner Bonds A Rather Complicated Plan By Duer & "Company"
- Borrow money
- Launch a new bank in NY
- Drive down the price of BONY and obtain control
- Then corner the market for government bonds.
# In March Duer Couldn'T Pay, Bankrupt, Prison Hamilton Dealt With Crisis Pretty Well Had It Not Been Dealt With As E Ectively As It Was, The Panic Of 1792 Might Have Destroyed America'S Financial Revolution And With It The Country'S Relative Prosperity And Political Peace. (Sylla P 63)
- William Duer and "Company" - spring 1792, speculation in bonds (rather than BUS scrip).
- A rather complicated plan to borrow money, launch a new bank in NY and drive down the price of BONY and thus obtain control of BONY, and then corner the market for government bonds.
Traditionally, accounts of the bubble and crash of spring 1792 focus on William Duer, a New York speculator, well-connected businessman, erstwhile governmental o cial in both the old and new governments, and personal friend of Hamilton. The scheming and operations of Duer and other members of his speculative "company" drove securities prices to new highs early in the year. In March, when Duer could not repay the large amounts of money he had borrowed to implement his plans, the market crashed and panic ensued. The scheming involved an attempt in January to launch a large new bank in New York City in order to drive down the price of BONY stock, which would allow Duer to obtain control of it. Then, Duer and his compatriots planned to corner the market for U. S. sixes, which subscribers to the BUS would need to meet their future payments for BUS shares. [Sylla et al. (2009)Sylla, Wright, and Cowen] p 73
- Hamilton's management of the crisis was masterful had it not been dealt with as e ectively as it was, the panic of 1792 might have destroyed America's financial revolution and with it the country's relative prosperity and political peace. ([Sylla et al. (2009) Sylla, Wright, and Cowen] p 63)
- Hamilton invented "Bagehot's rules" about central bank management of crises, some 80 years before Bagehot did.
# Deeper Story - Taxing, Bonds, Banks

The transformation - in 3 short years - into a functioning financial system

- Central government that could e ectively tax
- Respectable debt rather than bankruptcy
- Functioning bond (and stock) markets
- Growing banking system
US (north-east particularly) grew faster than Britain 1790-1830
Come back to this story later
# 2 1790-92 & Us Transformation
# 2.1 Transformation
# Contents
# Crisis & Transformation

Milton Friedman said that countries confront problems only after they have become crises (Sargent p 12)

The only time you can get major change is when there is a crisis. And the function of economists, like you and me, is not really to produce change, it's to make available solutions when a crisis arises. A crisis arises and you're going to take up some solution that is already there. At that time you're not going to get the bottom of it. (Friedman to Becker, 2002)

Government funding & fiscal crisis - not the "financial fraud" of The Economist

Before 1790, the [US] government was e ectively bankrupt.

(Sylla) Before 1790, the [US] government was e ectively bankrupt. ([Sylla(2010)] p 59)

# Hamilton - Author Of Transformation

Alexander Hamilton was the intellectual force shaping the transformation

- Essays on "political economy" written 1779-1781
Hamilton knew quite a lot about the successful financial revolutions of the Dutch and the British, and the aborted eorts of John Law in France. From those histories he drew the conclusion that finance was the key both to state power and economic growth.
(Sylla) Hamilton knew quite a lot about the successful financial revolutions of the Dutch and the British, and the aborted eorts of John Law in France. From those histories he drew the conclusion that finance was the key both to state power and economic growth. ([Sylla(2010)] p 61)
# Six Critical Components Of Modern Financial Systems

Sylla: When Hamilton left o ce in 1795, his adopted homeland harbored six critical institutional components that characterize modern financial systems:

I. stable public finances and debt management;

II. stable money;

III. an e ective central bank;

IV. a functioning banking system;

V. active securities markets;

VI. a growing number of business corporations, financial and non-financial

Six critical components: [Sylla(2010)] p 62

# 2.2 Constitution & History
# Contents
# Sargent ([Sargent(2011)]) And Analysis
# Pre-History
# History Over-View
- Prior to independence, US banking & finance limited, haphazard
- The War of Independence of 1775-1783 expensive (4-5x inc)
- By late 1780s the US free but e ectively in default
- Fundamental problem: Under Articles of Confederation States could tax, the central government not
- More fundamental, subtle, and important problem - British trade, discrimination, and playing o one state vs another
# Solution: New Constitution

To rearrange powers and incentives, the framers scrapped the original constitution, the Articles of Confederation, and wrote an entirely new one better designed to protect U. S. government creditors. The U. S. constitution realigned incentives and authorities in ways that

(a) let the central government devote enough tax revenues to service debts that the Continental Congress and the states had issued to pay for the war, and

(b) gave the central government exclusive authority to tax and regulate U. S. international trade. That gave the federal government the tools to implement a national trade policy (Sargent p 13)

The move from weak to strong central government a grand bargain

The story of 1792 and the Bank of the United States is as much, or more, a story about fiscal issues and war financing as it is about speculation and mania.

- Prior to 1780 and independence from Britain, US banking was limited and haphazard. Limited by the Crown
- The War of Independence (Revolutionary War) of 1775-1783 was expensive.
- Calomiris & Haber estimate it was 4-5x annual income. And income went down due to the disruption of the revolution [Macdonald(2006)] p 292 has estimate of cost
- Calomiris & Haber discuss some of the issues in resolving the debt. But Tom Sargent, in his Nobel lecture, does a really nice job: "U. S. Then, Europe Now": http://www.tomsargent.com/research/Sarg
- By the late 1780s the US was free but e ectively in default.
- The states and the central government had issued both paper money and interest-bearing debt. Money can often be thought of as non-interest-bearing government debt.
- In any case by 1790 the US owed about about $ $79mn $. 1/3 by states, 2/3 by central government
- But here was the fundamental problem: States could levy taxes, the central government could not
- The original structure of the US was the Articles of Confederation, ratified 1781.
- The central government was very weak, with power to levy taxes given to the states. The central government had to go asking for money from the states and the states, naturally, didn't give very much.
- This was a fiscal crisis - the central government could not pay its debts and the states
- There was an even more fundamental, subtle, and important problem - British trade, discrimination, and playing o one state vs another
- Most government revenue at this time was taxes on trade.
- Much trade was within the British imperial trading system - with Britain and its colonies.
- With tari s under control of each state, the British could play o states against each other, pulling trade away from a state that tried to raise tari s. The 13 colonies could not provide a unified front that would allow the states, or the central government, to raise the necessary revenue.
To rearrange powers and incentives, the framers scrapped the original constitution, the Articles of Confederation, and wrote an entirely new one better designed to protect U. S. government creditors. The U. S. constitution realigned incentives and authorities in ways that
(a) let the central government devote enough tax revenues to service debts that the Continental Congress and the states had issued to pay for the war, and
(b) gave the central government exclusive authority to tax and regulate U. S. international trade. That gave the federal government the tools to implement a national trade policy ([Sargent(2011)] p 13)
- The move from weak central government (Articles of Confederation) to strong Federal government (US Constitution) was grand bargain:
- States gave taxation to the Federal government,
- Federal government took over state debts (bailed out the states)
# Constitution As Fiscal "Grand Bargain"
- Move from weak central government (Articles of Confederation) to strong Federal government (US Constitution) was grand bargain:
- States gave taxation to the Federal government,
- Federal government took over state debts (bailed out the states)
War of Independence 1776-1783
Articles of Confederation
1776-1783
1777: finished drafting
1781: ratified
1789: superseded by Constitution
Debt in 1790, roughly \$79mn, of which states owed\$ 25mn (1/3). 40% of GDP (taxes small)
Problem central gov't no authority to tax, no source of revenue to pay debt New Constitution March 4,1789
Authority to tax taken from states and given to Federal
Government. In return, Federal gov't took over state debts: bailed out the states
"Measured at par (but not at the deeply discounted values then prevailing in the market), the ratio of Continental plus state debt to GDP stood at about $40\%$, a massive debt at a time when the government could raise at most only a small percentage of GDP in taxes." [Sargent(2011)] pp 12-13)
# Background - Us Debt Statistics, Revenue, Restructuring Debt Outstanding (As Of 1789, Sylla P. 66)
- Total, \$79.1mn, about 40% of GDP
- \$11.7mn foreign
- \$67.1mn domestic
US
(central) : \$54.1mn
 - State debts: \$25mn
- Debt Service estimated \$4.587mn (5.8% interest)
- \$4.045mn domestic, \$0.543mn foreign
# Revenue And Expenses (Sylla P. 67)
- Treasury collections
- 1789 (Sep-Dec) \$162,200
- 1792 almost \$4.7mn
- Expenses
- Operating expenses 1790 \$0.829mn
- Debt service (1789) \$4.587mn (prior slide) total \$5.4mn
In 1789, US clearly bankrupt. By 1792, more revenue, but still too much debt
- Need to reduce debt
- Reduced by 1792 to roughly \$3.3mn (next slide) total \$4.1mn
# Debt Restructuring (Sylla P. 68)

Foreign debt (mainly France) paid in full (?)

- Paid with new loans, primarily arranged by Dutch bankers. Completed 1795
- Domestic $6\%$ bonds exchanged for package of new bonds. Combination of
- $6 \%$ bonds
- $3\%$ bonds
- $6\%$ deferreds (10yr zero before paying interest)
- New package had e ective rate of $4\%$
- \$4mn of debt service down to \$2.7mn
- Domestic debt $67.1mn: $4mn is 6%, \$2.7mn is 4%
- Debt service roughly \$3.3mn
- \$2.7mn domestic, \$0.6mn foreign

Debt Restructuring [Sylla(2010), 68] 1790 and after
- Foreign debt (mainly France) paid in full (?)
- Paid o by taking new loans, primarily arranged by Dutch bankers. Completed 1795
- Domestic $6\%$ bonds exchanged for package of new bonds. Combination of
- $6\%$ bonds
- $3\%$ bonds
- $6\%$ deferreds (10yr zero before paying interest)
- New package had e c tive rate of $4 \%$
- \$4mn of debt service went to \$2.7mn
- Domestic debt $42.1 + 25 = $ 67.1mn$
- \$4mn / 67.1 = 6%; 2.7mn / 67.1 = 4%
# Us Debt History

Debt grew during the war, then after with "unpaid interest" and assumption of state debts

- Debt worthless
(almost) in 1780s
- Par above market.
- Only time gov't debt was deeply discounted
![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-15/c441f5b5-2f9d-4e4d-bc65-1301c02e96db/7f3197a098a0a4913c80a8ccdb7f4e3b092469c5810f62e67208f8c3a915c39b.jpg)
Figure 5: Market and Par Value of Gross Debt, as a Share of GDP
([Reinhart and Rogo (2011)] section 3.1): the US was victorious but essentially in default. Debts had been accumulated by both the states and central
# Us Debt History
- Debt trading at $30-$40 - virtually in default
 - With new constitution, debt shot up to $80-$90

# 3 Hamilton And Two Crises
# 3.1 Hamilton'S Masterful Management Of Crises
# Contents
# August 1791 (Bank Script) & Spring 1792 (Bonds & Duer) Founding Of Bank Of The United States
- \$10mn shares, July 1791, \$25 "scrip" for \$400 share
- Scrip went from \$25 in July to \$300 in August 1791
The BUS direct public o ering led to six weeks of heated financial speculation, the likes of which had never before been witnessed in America.
(Sylla) "Bubble" that popped and prices crashed to around \$130 in mid-August A rather complicated plan by Duer & "company"
- Borrow money
- Launch a new bank in NY
- Drive down the price of BONY and obtain control
- Then corner the market for government bonds.
Bond prices crashed March 1792
# Us Bond (6S) Prices - Three Big Price Rises

![](Https://Cdn-Mineru. Openxlab. Org. Cn/Result/2025-11-15/C441F5B5-2F9D-4E4D-Bc65-1301C02E96Db/6Cd10Df9D66291Baa9E048B355Bcb712Cfd47793E1F1F47E673269Adb8A9Cc8F. Jpg)

Sylla, Wright, Cowen, Business History Review 2009

1790 solid fundamentals, government & tax restructuring - to be discussed

Aug 1791 and crash - Bank Scrip Bubble

Winter 1792 and March crash - bonds and Duer scheme to take over BONY

BUS shares

- \$10mn of BUS shares, \$8mn to the public.
 - Initially o ered July 4,1791. \$400/share, needed to buy \$25 share certificate or "scrip" - essentially option to pay additional \$375 for full share
 - Scrip prices went from \$25 in July to \$300 in August 1791
- Mini-panic or Bank Scrip Bubble, August
1791. Primarily in scrip, which went from $25 up to $270 or even \$300 by August
1792. Crashed back to \$130 by mid-August
The BUS direct public o ering led to six weeks of heated financial speculation, the likes of which had never before been witnessed in America. Bank scraps purchased at 25\$ quickly doubled in price and remained at that level for most of July. In early August, they soared, reaching a bid of 264-280 asked in New York on August 11, and reportedly more than 300 in Philadelphia that same evening. Then they tumbled, in Boston from 230 on August 12 to 112 on August 14, to 154-59 in New York on August 16, and to 125-37 in Philadelphia the same day, before rallying later that month. [Sylla et al. (2009)Sylla, Wright, and Cowen] p 71
	- William Duer and "Company" - spring 1792, speculation in bonds (rather than BUS scrip).
	- A rather complicated plan to borrow money, launch a new bank in NY and drive down the price of BONY and thus obtain control of BONY, and then corner the market for government bonds.
Traditionally, accounts of the bubble and crash of spring 1792 focus on William Duer, a New York speculator, well-connected businessman, erstwhile governmental cial in both the old and new governments, and personal friend of Hamilton. The scheming and operations of Duer and other members of his speculative "company" drove securities prices to new highs early in the year. In March, when Duer could not repay the large amounts of money he had borrowed to implement his plans, the market crashed and panic ensued. The scheming involved an attempt in January to launch a large new bank in New York City in order to drive down the price of BONY stock, which would allow Duer to obtain control of it. Then, Duer and his compatriots planned to corner the market for U. S. sixes, which subscribers to the BUS would need to meet their future payments for BUS shares. [Sylla et al. (2009)Sylla, Wright, and Cowen] p 73
# Hamilton Invented Modern Central Bank Management

Hamilton's management masterful had it not been dealt with as e ectively as it was, the panic of 1792 might have destroyed America's financial revolution and with it the country's relative prosperity and political peace. (Sylla p 63)

Open market operations (1791, Sylla p 78)

- Used "sinking fund" to buy bonds - inject cash into system
Lender-of-last-resort operations 1792
- Invented "Bagehot's rules" 81 years before Bagehot
- First: lend freely and vigorously, but at a very high rate of interest (to discourage those who do not have liquidity problems)
- Second: lend on all good securities (securities used as collateral) to everyone who is solvent
- Hamilton's management of the crisis was masterful had it not been dealt with as e ectively as it was, the panic of 1792 might have destroyed America's financial revolution and with it the country's relative prosperity and political peace. ([Sylla et al. (2009) Sylla, Wright, and Cowen] p 63)
- Hamilton invented "Bagehot's rules" about central bank management of crises, some 80 years before Bagehot did.
- Central Bank policy during financial (liquidity crisis) - Bagehot's rules ([Bagehot(2012)] Chapter 7, paragraphs 57-58) but actually articulated & implemented by Hamilton 80 years earlier (1792)
- First: lend freely and vigorously, but at a very high rate of interest (to discourage those who do not have liquidity problems)
- Second: lend on all good securities (securities used as collateral) to everyone who is solvent in time of panic it [the central bank] must advance freely and vigorously to the public out of the reserve.
First. That these loans should only be made at a very high rate of interest. This will operate as a heavy fine on unreasonable timidity, and will prevent the greatest number of applications by persons who do not require it. The rate should be raised early in the panic, so that the fine may be paid early; that no one may borrow out of idle precaution without paying well for it; that the Banking reserve may be protected as far as possible.
Secondly. That at this rate these advances should be made on all good banking securities, and as largely as the public ask for them. The reason is plain. The object is to stay alarm, and nothing therefore should be done to cause alarm. But the way to cause alarm is to refuse some one who has good security to o er… No advances indeed need be made by which the Bank will ultimately lose. The amount of bad business in commercial countries is an infinitesimally small fraction of the whole business… The great majority, the majority to be protected, are the 'sound' people, the people who have good security to o er. If it is known that the Bank of England is freely advancing on what in ordinary times is reckoned a good security--on what is then commonly pledged and easily convertible--the alarm of the solvent merchants and bankers will be stayed. But if securities, really good and usually convertible, are refused by the Bank, the alarm will not abate, the other loans made will fail in obtaining their end, and the panic will become worse and worse. ([Bagehot(2012)] Chapter 7, paragraphs 57-58)
# 3.1.1 What Is A Central Bank? Introduction
# Contents
# What Is A Central Bank & What Does One Do?

Manage currency, money supply

- But what is money? We start next lecture
- For now, pretend we know what it is - dollar bills
Two things can go wrong with money:
- Too much inflation
- Not enough liquidity crisis, depression, & deflation
Two challenges for Central Banks:
- Inflation: Governments try to issue money to raise revenue (inflation tax)
- Germany, Cagan, & hyperinflation
- Liquidity Crisis & Deflation: Managing liquidity crisis
- Individuals, banks, companies shift, want more liquid assets
(money) - US Liquidity Crises 1893,1907,1930s, 2008
# Central Banks & Open Market Operations

Central Bank method for putting money into economy or taking out Actually very simple:

- Buying & selling bonds
- Put money in: buy existing bonds from banks (pay with money)
- Take money out: sell bonds to banks (take back money)
- Lending: Repo
(repurchase) operations
- Put money in: Lend money, taking in something
(bonds) as collateral
In normal times, these occur periodically, low levels
In liquidity crisis, can be larger (to inject large amounts of money) - particularly repo or lending
"Lender-of-last resort" & Bagehot's rules
- During liquidity crisis:
- Lend as much as banks want, but at "penalty rate"
-Lend on all good collateral
- Goal: provide liquidity
(money) banks & individuals demand
# Money & Liquidity Provided By Federal Reserve (18:Q2)

Currency to Public: direct currency issued to public

Reserves, repos, vault cash to banks: public as checking deposits

Bonds held: cash transferred to public ("open market operations")

Big Increases in Bonds & Reserves Since Financial Crisis

2007: \$0.7 of bonds; issued \$0.2 of reserves

 2018: §4.1 of bonds; issued §2.5 of reserves

U. S. Debt and Monetary Environment, end-2019, $tm

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-15/c441f5b5-2f9d-4e4d-bc65-1301c02e96db/0f7a7f62242fdb70336a48f712978ea3ee6391e82ef5905d9c9a7a29bc7192f7.jpg)

Arrows indicate the holdings of bonds issued by the Treasury (for example bonds issued by the Treasury and held by the Federal Reserve) or reserves issued by the Fed. Bonds are acquired by transfer of cash or other assets but those transfers are not shown in this diagram. - $Z_{1}$ table $L_{210}$ (&$D_{3}$); ** $Z_{1}$ table $L_{109}$; *** $Z_{1}$ table $L_{110}$
For detailed holdings, see lecture
3. Note two big changes post-2008:
I. Federal Reserves now holds large amounts of US Treasuries and Other Bonds (mainly GSEs)
2007: UST = 740.6, GSE = 0 ($z_{1}$, $L_{0}$0.108, lines 12 & 13)
 - 2018: UST + GSE = \$4,102 (2,378.3 + 1,723.7) (z1, L0.109, lines 12 & 15)
II. Reserves held by banks up massively

Note that the large holdings of US Treasuries by the Fed is new since 2008.2008
# Traditional Story Of Central Banking & Money

Currency & Reserves ("Liquid Assets") in short supply

- Comes from gold (19th c) or Central Bank (20th c)
"Money" creation limited by Currency & Reserves
- When more gold found or more reserves issued, money goes up
Central Bank issues reserves by:
- Repo: lending money and borrowing securities)
- Open Market Operations: Buying securities and giving money
# Repo Or Sell / Buy-Back - Borrowing Money

BUY: Buy today at $\mathbf{P_0}$ (give \$100), plan sell later at high price (get $?X?)

REPO (2%): Sell today at $\mathbf{P_0}$ (get \$100), agree to buy back at $\mathbf{P_1}$ (give \$102)

Buy today and hope price goes way up

Today: Buy & Repo all cancel

- Get bond from purchase, give it for repo
- Give \$100 for purchase, get it from repo
# Tomorrow: Cash Doesn'T Cancel
- Get bond back from repo, then sell in market - Bonds cancel
- Get?X? from selling, give away \$102 in repo
Profit $=?X? - 102$
- If $\mathbf{?X}$ is high, make money. If low, lose
# Where Does Repo Price Of \$102 Come From?

Borrow @2%: PV Pricing (Forward Pricing)

$$
\begin{array}{l} \mathbf {P}_{0} = \frac{\mathbf {P}_{n}}{(1 + y)^{n}} \\ \mathbf {P}_{0} = $ 100; \mathbf {y} = 2 \%; \mathbf {n} = 1 \\ \mathbf {P}_{\mathrm{n}} = $ 102 \\ \end{array}
$$
# Repo Is Borrowing Money (Collateralized Borrowing)

Here, borrow at $2\%$ for 1 year

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-15/c441f5b5-2f9d-4e4d-bc65-1301c02e96db/9ddb9884cb2dbe5812fded7246a2620ed255e31107f37fc400f3b8ff0734701b.jpg)

# Brief History Of Us Central Bank

From 1830-1913, no US Central Bank

- Money supply not managed
- 1893,1907: liquidity crisis depression because no way to expand money
1907 crisis led to founding of US Federal Reserve in 1913
1930s: Fed failed to provide liquidity
Fed created Great Depression:
Regarding the Great Depression. You're right, we [Fed] did it.
(Bernanke) 2008: Fed injected massive liquidity
- QE 1 (Quantitative Easing)
- Reserves grew massively
# 3.2 Debates: "Fairness" & "Evils Of Debt"
# Contents

In both of these we see echos of debates that have continued to this day

# "Fairness" & Federalists Vs Populists

Debate over paying back in full vs at market value

An especially fascinating part of Hamilton's report is his response to James Madison's proposal to discriminate among current owners of Continental bonds according to when they had purchased them. Motivated by concerns about fairness, Madison wanted to take away inordinate capital gains from people who had purchased Continental bonds at discount; he also wanted to compensate former owners who had sold them at discount. Hamilton convinced Congress that such ex post discrimination would adversely a etc the beliefs of prospective purchasers of government debt and would thereby damage liquidity and trust in the market for bearer government bonds. (Sargent p 15)

# "Fairness" & Federalists Vs Populists
- Paying back in full did two things:
- Established reputation to pay back debts - future low risk premium
- Helped establish liquid and deep market for tradeable debt
- Echoes of England vs France
- After Mississippi bubble, France discriminated - and also in 1710
- After South Sea, contracts generally honored in the courts
- Paying back in full vs at market value
-Decision to pay back full notional controversial
- In a fundamental sense "unfair" - many original lenders lost out
- Sargent:
An especially fascinating part of Hamilton's report is his response to James Madison's proposal to discriminate among current owners of Continental bonds according to when they had purchased them. Motivated by concerns about fairness, Madison wanted to take away inordinate capital gains from people who had purchased Continental bonds at discount; he also wanted to compensate former owners who had sold them at discount. Hamilton convinced Congress that such ex post discrimination would adversely a etc the beliefs of prospective purchasers of government debt and would thereby damage liquidity and trust in the market for bearer government bonds. ([Sargent(2011)] p 15)
- Did two things:
- Established reputation to pay back debts - future low risk premium
- Helped establish liquid and deep market for tradeable debt
If discriminate, investors would not have confidence they could sell and transfer bonds
- Echoes of England vs France
- After Mississippi bubble, France discriminated - and also in 1710
"The objective, as far as was possible, was to return to the status quo ante…. In February 1721 a commission was established to inspect all contracts and claims." (Macdonald p. 222)
After South Sea, contracts generally honored in the courts
"While the shareholders, creditors, and debts of the South Sea Company were not left to sort out their positions solely according to their original contracts, there was no attempt to return to the status quo ante." (Macdonald p. 224).
# "Evils Of Debt"

Hamilton

(Federalists) vs Gallatin (Republican, Je erson & Madison)

Like other Republican leaders, Gallatin thought most public debts were bad (Sylla p 75)

His view was that "every nation is enfeebled by a public debt," and that the best policy was to extinguish public debt as quickly as it was feasible to do so by cutting spending and increasing taxes. (p 79)

In fact, seems Hamilton was right - financial market transformation (including debt) transformed America

- Best evidence, US grew $>1\%$ per-capita for decades.
- Faster than UK
- One amazing statistic: 1800 life expectancy US 56yrs, UK 36yrs
There was a high-level financial policy debate of sorts in the mid-1790s. On one side was Albert Gallatin, a Republican congressman from western Pennsylvania and future treasury secretary in the Jeerson and Madison administrations. Gallatin was the Republican opposition's financial expert, a role that corresponded to Hamilton's role in the Federalist Party. ([Sylla(2010)] p 74)
Like other Republican leaders, Gallatin thought most public debts were bad, and his principal charge against Hamilton's policies was that the assumption of state debts in 1790 had made the national debt larger than it needed to be by at least \$10.9 million. ([Sylla(2010)] p 75)
Gallatin closed his Sketch with a challenge to the view that the debt funding plan "had created a large productive capital which did not exist before." His view was that "every nation is enfeebled by a public debt," and that the best policy was to extinguish public debt as quickly as it was feasible to do so by cutting spending and increasing taxes. He lamented the fact that foreign investors had purchased so much of the U. S. debt and other American securities, which only led the American sellers "to consume, to spend more, and they have consumed and spent extravagantly."
Taking in the great number of elegant houses which have been built within a few years in all the large cities, and which, however convenient to the inhabitants, a ord no additional revenue to the nation, it may be asserted that the greater part of the capital thus drawn from Europe for purchases of stock has been actually consumed, without leaving in its stead any other productive capital, and thus as the nation still owes the whole, it has been impoverished even by the only consequence of the funding system that has made any temporary addition to the apparent wealth of the country. That wealth is, in a great degree, consumed and destroyed, and the whole debt remains to be paid. (Adams 1960, III, 149)
Substitute China for Europe in this passage, and Gallatin's message would seem rather similar to arguments one often hears today, more than two centuries later. Other passages in his essay indicate that he would subscribe to "crowding out"—the idea that public borrowing and spending reduces private investment—as well as Ricardian equivalence—the notion that people react to increases in public debt by increasing their savings in order to be able to pay the increased taxes necessary to service a larger public debt. ([Sylla(2010)] p 79)
# Remember Six Critical Components

After transformation, US had:

I. stable public finances and debt management;

II. stable money;

III. an e ective central bank;

IV. a functioning banking system;

V. active securities markets;

VI. a growing number of business corporations, financial and non-financial

# 3.3 Follow-Up: 1830S State Non-Bailout
# Contents
# 1840S: States Not Bailed Out

Interesting follow-up from 1789 state debt "bail-out"

- By 1830s, states had accumulated debt (often for infrastructure projects)
Recession, end 1830s, many states defaulted
- Debates about bailing out states
- Federal government did not bail out states
Many
(most) states rewrote constitutions - required year-by-year balanced budgets
- Example of crises leading to long-term reform
# 4 Colonies And Inflation Tax
# Contents
# Debt And Taxes

Governments generally need to raise revenue

Historically, to fight wars

- Now, wars + social programs (unemployment insurance, retirement & pensions, roads & bridges, police,…)
"Anglo-Dutch" Finance (Britain slowly post 1720, US quickly post-1789):
- Borrowing (during wars) to extract current resources versus future repayment
Ultimately, must extract resources from population - Taxation
- Stable & reliable debt ("Anglo-Dutch") requires stable & reliable taxation
Various means of taxation:
- Now: Income tax, sales tax
(VAT) Historically: tari s
One important tax, hard to understand but important: Inflation Tax
- Introduce here, come back when discuss hyperinflation
US Colonies (prior to 1789) used Inflation Tax
# Remember Money & Inflation: Money Prices Quantity Theory Of Money: Money Causes Inflation
- Virtually 1-for-1 when consider huge changes in money
-Across countries, $\mathbf{R}^2 = 0.97$;Absolutely 1-for-1: slope 1.00
![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-15/c441f5b5-2f9d-4e4d-bc65-1301c02e96db/2c699fb44d910e1de9fcaecaa7f617e9d5014b0e0d30fc15ea0c64e6b08322bd.jpg)

Median Inflation Rate All Countries 5-Year Moving Average: 1500-2006

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-15/c441f5b5-2f9d-4e4d-bc65-1301c02e96db/d1ef8d486d08f37d51efda7369e4ca3b6b327fb06105b80b61c60825fb1e0f68.jpg)

- Post 1945 - World moving to paper money (fiat money)
[Reinhart and Rogo (2008), Figure 13]
# "Inflation Tax" - Raising Revenue Via Inflation

Why inflation? Why do governments print "too much money"?

- Inflation Tax: a way to raise revenue, even if other taxes do not work
- In 1700s (and War of Independence), American colonies preferred inflation tax
- Often, weak or troubled states turn to inflation taxation
John Maynard Keynes's A Tract on Monetary Reform is the best I have ever seen
A Government can live for a long time, even the German Government or the Russian Government, by printing paper money. That is to say, it can by this means secure the command over real resources, - resources just as real as those obtained by taxation.
The burden of the tax is well spread, cannot be evaded, costs nothing to collect, and falls, in a rough sort of way, in proportion to the wealth of the victim. No wonder its superficial advantages have attracted Ministers of Finance
# Keynes'S Example Illustrates Inflation Tax

Country with \$36mn in currency

- Using dollars because it was a "hard currency" - tied to gold
- Let's call that \$36mn of gold (or say that is 36mn cups of co ee)
- 9mn notes, so each worth \$4 or 4 cups of coee
Government decides to print additional 3mn notes
- 12mn notes, but still worth 36mn cups of co ee
Each note now worth \$3 of gold or 3 cups of coee
The original 9mn notes, held by public, now worth \$27mn of gold, 27mn co ees
- Public has given to the gov't §9mn of gold or 9mn cups of cofee the Government has transferred from the public to itself an amount of resources equal to \$9mn, just as successfully as if it had raised this sum in taxation
In fact, the Gov't has raised it through an Inflation Tax
[Keynes(1923), Chapter II0.1], [Macdonald(2006), 285]
# Government Can Continue To Tax

Government can play same game again

- Print further 4mn notes
- Now 16mn total, each worth \$2.25
(gold) or 2.25 co ees
 - Those "new" 12mn of notes worth only \$27mn
(gold) or 27mn co ees
- Government again took \$9mn
(gold) How & Why does this work?
- We assume people want money, it is useful for daily life
- Assume public wants to hold \$36mn
(gold) or 36mn co ees
- Every year, to still hold 36mn co ees in money, must "buy" extra notes
- Must give government 9mn co ees
Every year, transfer 9mn co ees to government - real resources, a true tax the Government can continue to raise resources by inflation…. the conveniences of using money in daily life are so great that the public are prepared, rather than forego them [the conveniences of money], to pay the inflationary tax, provided it is not raised to a prohibitive level.
# Inflation Tax For U. S.

Inflation Tax = Real Balance Inflation Rate

- US M1(Dec 2019) = \$4,000bn
US inflation about $2.5\%$ per year
- Inflation Tax about \$100bn per year
Compare with regular taxes (income, corporate,…) of roughly \$3,863bn
# Hyperinflation - Inflation Out Of Control

This whole scheme, however, is rather unstable

- Governments cannot always plan steady money creation
- Government may not fully understand relation between inflation and tax
- Public tries to avoid tax by reducing holding of money - say reduce note holdings from 36mn co ees to 20mn co ees
Most importantly, Government may try to raise more revenue and print even more money
- As gov't prints more, public holds less, gov't prints more, and things spiral out of control
German hyperinflation August 1922 - November 1923, prices up by 6.6bn.
- If a cup of co ee cost \$3.50 August 1922, it cost\$ 23,000,000,000 in November 1923
# Inflation Hugely Damaging

Modest inflation

(maybe) tolerable

- But inflation rarely stays "modest"
Keynes once again said it eloquently (Economic Consequences of the Peace):
Lenin is said to have declared that the best way to destroy the Capitalist System was to debauch the currency. By a continuing process of inflation, governments can confiscate, secretly and unobserved, an important part of the wealth of their citizens. By this method they not only confiscate, but they confiscate arbitrarily; and, while the process impoverishes many, it actually enriches some.

# Introduction To Money & Inflation

# The Enduring Puzzle Of Money & Inflation

What is money?

What causes inflation?

Two of the most enduring puzzles in macroeconomics

Particularly relevant today

- And why was it so low post-2008?
- Burst of inflation 2021-2022 - highest in $40+$ years
- Only to surge in 2022?

# What I Want To Cover - Facts, Theories, Puzzles Of Money & Inflation
- Fact: "Inflation is always and everywhere a monetary phenomenon" (Milton Friedman)
- What is inflation? Some history and a little theory.
- Evidence & Theory say Money & inflation move up & down together. M & P
- Theory
(theories) :
- Quantity Theory of Money
(Friedman) : Money growth causes inflation: MP
- New Fiscal Theory of the Price Level
(FTPL) - Puzzle 1: What happens during a "liquidity crisis"?
- In fact, what is a "liquidity crisis"?
- Puzzle 2: Standard Monetary Theory did not work in 2000s
- Post-2008: M, rates low, inflation low (should have been high)
- Pandemic: M, rates low, inflation high (but why di erent from post-2008?)
- Puzzle 3: What happens when traditional "money" disappears?
- How to think about inflation with electronic money, blockchain?
Solution: New Monetary Theory: Fiscal Theory of the Price Level
- NOT MMT (Modern Monetary Theory) - a load of hooey

# 1 Inflation Is Always And Everywhere A Monetary Phenomenon
# 1.1 Inflation Background - History, Hyperinflation, Inflation Tax
# Historical World-Wide Inflation

Median Inflation Rate All Countries

5-Year Moving Average: 1500-2006

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-15/c441f5b5-2f9d-4e4d-bc65-1301c02e96db/0117f6739070834356841e12a3662190d4e1703e0411a66f98e16cc8bcde9788.jpg)

From Reinhart & Rogo (2008 NBER paper) Figure 13

Worldwide inflation took o after 1945 (with big spike in 1920s - discuss later)

- Post 1945 - World moving to paper money (fiat money)
[Reinhart and Rogo Reinhart and Rogo 2008, Figure 13]
The figure shows the median inflation rate across a large sample of countries, covering much of the world.
- Because it's the median it excludes hyperinflations - it provides a reasonable estimate of the average or central tendency for inflation across the world.
- The most striking observation is that over a long span of history, up until roughly the 1930s, inflation was sometime positive, sometimes negative, with no strong tendency for positive inflation. That changed dramatically in the middle of the 20th century; since then a decided positive global inflation rate.
- Remember that this is a median so it excludes the hyperinflations of Germany, Hungary, Greece, etc.
- The positive inflation since the middle of the 20th century is really the curse of paper money – the ability and willingness of governments to print money and cause inflation. Because whatever anyone may tell you, the single and only cause of long-term, sustained, high inflation is printing money: high money growth means high inflation.
- The trade-o is that central banks now have the ability to provide liquidity and prevent disasters such as the Great Depression of the 1930s.
- There is some evidence that the world has learned something from the sustained inflations of the 1970s and central banks are better-behaved.
- Personally, I think that is true. I certainly hope so.
- Side-note. There was little inflation risk, in 2008-2009, even with the huge liquidity injections. In the US the monetary base doubled between $Q_{3}$ 2008 and $Q_{2}$ 2009.
- Little inflation risk because at the time there was a huge increase in the demand for money. All that extra liquidity got sucked up by banks, corporations, and households.
- During financial crises of the 1800s, before central banks and paper money, a financial crisis would inevitably be followed by a fall in prices - price deflation. That was due to the large increase in demand for money and liquidity, without any concomitant increase in supply.

# High Inflation Is Common - And Damaging

Inflation has two e cts:

- Default - "Inflates away debt" - makes debt disappear
- May benefit government, but private debtors really important
- Transfer from savers to borrowers
Lenin is said to have declared that the best way to destroy the capitalist system was to debauch the currency. By a continuing process of inflation, governments can confiscate, secretly and unobserved, an important part of the wealth of their citizens. By this method, they not only confiscate, but they confiscate arbitrarily; and, while the process impoverishes many, it actually enriches some. (Keynes, "Economic Consequences of the Peace" 1919)
- "Inflation tax" - on-going revenue for government
Lenin is said to have declared that the best way to destroy the capitalist system was to debauch the currency. By a continuing process of inflation, governments can confiscate, secretly and unobserved, an important part of the wealth of their citizens. By this method, they not only confiscate, but they confiscate arbitrarily; and, while the process impoverishes many, it actually enriches some. The sight of this arbitrary rearrangement of riches strikes not only at security, but at confidence in the equity of the existing distribution of wealth. Those to whom the system brings windfalls… become 'profiteers', who are the object of the hatred of the bourgeoisie, whom the inflationism has impoverished not less than the proletariat. As the inflation proceeds… all permanent relations between debtors and creditors, which
"Inflation Tax" - Raising Revenue via Inflation - Repeating from last lecture Why inflation? Why do governments print "too much money"?
- Inflation Tax: a way to raise revenue, even if other taxes do not work
- In 1700s (and War of Independence), American colonies preferred inflation tax
- Often, weak or troubled states turn to inflation taxation
John Maynard Keynes's A Tract on Monetary Reform is the best I have ever seen
A Government can live for a long time, even the German Government or the Russian Government, by printing paper money. That is to say, it can by this means secure the command over real resources, - resources just as real as those obtained by taxation.
The burden of the tax is well spread, cannot be evaded, costs nothing to collect, and falls, in a rough sort of way, in proportion to the wealth of the victim. No wonder its superficial advantages have attracted Ministers of Finance
Keynes's Example Illustrates Inflation Tax – Repeating from last lecture
Country with \$36mn in currency
Using dollars because it was a "hard currency" - tied to gold
- Let's call that \$36mn of gold (or say that is 36mn cups of co ee)
- 9mn notes, so each worth \$4 or 4 cups of coee
Government decides to print additional 3mn notes
- 12mn notes, but still worth 36mn cups of co ee
Each note now worth \$3 of gold or 3 cups of coee
The original 9mn notes, held by public, now worth \$27mn of gold, 27mn co ees
- Public has given to the gov't §9mn of gold or 9mn cups of cofee the Government has transferred from the public to itself an amount of resources equal to \$9mn, just as successfully as if it had raised this sum in taxation
In fact, the Gov't has raised it through an Inflation Tax
[KeynesKeynes1923, Chapter II0.1], [MacdonaldMacdonald2006,285]
# Government Can Continue To Tax - Repeating From Last Lecture

Government can play same game again

- Print further 4mn notes
- Now 16mn total, each worth \$2.25
(gold) or 2.25 co ees
 - Those "new" 12mn of notes worth only \$27mn
(gold) or 27mn co ees
- Government again took \$9mn
(gold) How & Why does this work?
- We assume people want money, it is useful for daily life
- Assume public wants to hold \$36mn
(gold) or 36mn co ees
- Every year, to still hold 36mn co ees in money, must "buy" extra notes
- Must give government 9mn co ees
Every year, transfer 9mn co ees to government - real resources, a true tax the Government can continue to raise resources by inflation…. the conveniences of using money in daily life are so great that the public are prepared, rather than forego them [the conveniences of money], to pay the inflationary tax, provided it is not raised to a prohibitive level.
# Inflation Tax For U. S. - 2019

Inflation Tax = Real Balance Inflation Rate

- US M1(Dec 2019) = \$4,000bn
US inflation about $2.5\%$ per year
- Inflation Tax about \$100bn per year
Compare with regular taxes (income, corporate,…) of roughly \$3,863bn
