# Hyperinflation - Inflation Out Of Control

This whole scheme, however, is rather unstable

- Governments cannot always plan steady money creation
- Government may not fully understand relation between inflation and tax
- Public tries to avoid tax by reducing holding of money - say reduce note holdings from 36mn co ees to 20mn co ees
Most importantly, Government may try to raise more revenue and print even more money
- As gov't prints more, public holds less, gov't prints more, and things spiral out of control
German hyperinflation August 1922 - November 1923, prices up by 6.6bn.
- If a cup of co ee cost \$3.50 August 1922, it cost \$23,000,000,000 in November 1923
# 1.2 Evidence That Inflation Is Always And Everywhere A Monetary Phenomenon
# Contents
# "Inflation Is Always And Everywhere A Monetary Phenomenon"

(Friedman) One of Milton Friedman's most famous quotes

Graph shows striking evidence of strong Money / Price association

- Virtually 1-for-1 when consider huge changes in money
- Across countries, $\mathbf{R}^2 = 0.97$; Absolutely 1-for-1: slope 1.00
- "one of the oldest and most reliable conclusions about macroeconomic behavior"
(Barro) - When M, P
# Important Question:
- Does money cause inflation?
- Does Money Prices
# Friedman Developed Quantity Theory Of Money: Money Causes Inflation

![](Https://Cdn-Mineru. Openxlab. Org. Cn/Result/2025-11-15/C441F5B5-2F9D-4E4D-Bc65-1301C02E96Db/7Fa3Bb555F125728Cddd2E5E9073C1D32E3E082153D3E7Cde1A3D9D418Deb022. Jpg)

After Macroeconomics, Robert Barro,

1997. Data from IMF International Financial Statistics. Growth rates annual averages, 1960-1995 for most countries. P/P: growth of consumer prices; M/M: growth of currency.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-15/c441f5b5-2f9d-4e4d-bc65-1301c02e96db/23b1b619c00c07f7ce2fb6367afce2430ff5256083e03e59d9fd3c062234f0f8.jpg)

Also holds for moderate growth - not just extremes

The horizontal axis shows currency growth less $0.67^{*}$ output growth. This adjusts very roughly for fast-growing economies needing more money, and so growth in money will have a lesser impact on price growth

(inflation) .

	- With relatively constant Y

(output) and V (velocity or the way we use money) we now have a theory of the relation between money and prices:

	- $\mathbf{MV} = \mathbf{PY}$ money, inflation

	- This theory works pretty darned well. We can look at this graph for inflation across the world for a long period – and changes in money are really the only thing that can explain changes in prices

	- Robert Barro in a macro textbook calls this "one of the oldest and most reliable conclusions about macroeconomic behavior".

	- Some of these inflations are huge - $91.2\%$ per year (Brazil, 1960-1994) means that something costing 1 Cruzeiro in 1960 would have cost 17bn Cruzeiro by

1998. This is a huge hyper-inflation.

	- Note that there were various new currencies (Cruzeiro Novo, Cruzado, Cruzado Novo) introduced, in 1964,1967,1970,1984,1986,1989,1990,1993, and finally the Real in 1994 which has been relatively stable, with inflation since 1994 only $7.9\%$ pa - accomplished by controlling the money supply - $M_{1}$ $19.5\%$ pa. (From IMF eoy CPI $1994 = 60.379$, $2007 = 162.262$, from www.bcb.gov.br $M_{1}$ Dec $1994 = 22,773$ Dec $2007 = 231,430$ )

	- The relation is dominated by the extreme cases (Brazil, Argentina, etc.) but as we'll see shortly the relation still holds pretty well even for the mass of cases below $20\%$ inflation

	- Nor is it only increases in money that are associated with increases in prices – in the US there have been a small number of periods where the Fed has cut the money supply, and prices have duly fallen (sometimes by huge amounts)

From September 1920 to January 1922 money stock fell by $9\%$ (a big fall in a short period, due to Federal Reserve actions) and price consumer prices fell by $20\%$ (a huge fall) by March 1922

Most importantly, and something we'll get back to later in the course, from 1929 to 1933 money supply fell by $31\%$ and prices by $25\%-27\%$. These are huge falls.

	- The conclusion is inescapable - changes in prices and inflation are the result of changes in money. An incredibly important but oft-ignored result

Well-documented from Hungarian and German hyperinflations of post WWI and WWII

Well-documented from Latin American in 1950s, 1960s, 1970s

But US and European politicians had to learn this all over again in the 1970s, when they did not pay attention to money growth and let inflation grow to $10\%$ during the 1970s.

Here is the money / inflation picture for "moderate" inflation up to $20\%$ per year. Still a strong and consistent relationship

Figure 2 Inflation and Money Growth 1960-1995 for ModerateInflation

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-15/c441f5b5-2f9d-4e4d-bc65-1301c02e96db/8cbd915fedb0413cb461b87eaa3a4a9cba4b77ab1d3d9e90de47216c5284e837.jpg)

The horizontal axis shows currency growth less $0.67^{*}$ output growth. This adjusts very roughly for the fact that fast-growing economies need more money, and so growth in money will have a lesser impact on price growth

(inflation) .

# 2 What Is Money?
# 2.1 What Is A Chair?
# Contents
# What Is Money? One Of The Hardest Economics Questions

Money is all around us, should be easy to define

But money is hard to pin down

- Certainly cash (\$1 and \$10 bills)
- Yes checking account
- But what about credit card?
We need to address three questions
I. What is money? How do we define it?
II. Why do we use money (what is its value to us?)
III. Unit of Account: What is the price of money? What do we even mean by the "price" of money?
# "What Is Money"

This has to be one of the hardest questions in economics: money is all around us, shows up in so many di erent ways, and is so deeply embedded in everything we dit is hard to define. A little bit like asking "what is gravity?" Galileo standing at the top of the leaning tower of Pisa had no answer. Isaac Newton standing under his apple tree started to answer. Einstein with his big bang, expanding universe, and black holes starts to get to an answer. But we still don't know. And for money we are still back with Galileo or Newton.

We want to think about three related questions:

- "What is money?"
- "Value" - why is money valuable? Why do we use it and what services do we get from it?
- "Price" - what is the price of money
# Let'S Ask "What Is A Chair?" Attribute Vs Functional Definition

Money is hard, so let's ask an easy question:

What is a chair?

Seems silly toask"whatisachair"-weallknow But let'stryto defineachair:

- seat
- four legs
- arms back
- wooden
That seems like a decent definition, except it just doesn't work well
# Define A Chair By What It Does - Functionally

Instead of attributes, define a chair by what it does, what we use it for:

- Something we sit in
- Functional definition rather than attributes (arms, legs, wood,…)
Try the same with money - what does money do?
We use money for transactions - to buy and sell
- Liquidity is primary - confidence that others will take it, quickly, easily, without question, for the same value as when we took it
Usually we ask "what is money?" thinking that we go from "something" -> "money", as if something (gold, pieces of paper) are the idea we should start with and then we look at those to understand what is money
Instead, we should think about "money" as something we need in business and trade, in our everyday lives. We look around to find something that works well as "money" (something that does 1,2,3 above) and then we use that and call it "money"
But the underlying idea, the necessity to have something that helps us in our everyday transactions, is the important thing. Then sometimes gold, sometimes pieces of paper, sometimes bank accounts, work well. Di erent things at di erent times, and di erent things more and less well for (1,2,3).
# Money - Functional Definition

Move away from attributes to function Attributes gold

- banknotes
- bank checking account
# Function

I. Acts as a medium of exchange (liquidity & transactions)

II. Stores value (others take it for same value)

III. Acts as a unit of account (price of money)

For an economy to function, for us to buy & sell, work & play, we need something which performs those 3 functions

- Call that something Money
Money will be di erent things at di erent times
Gold
Banknotes
Federal Reserve notes
- Checking accounts
# 2.2 Textbook Definition: 1) Payments; 2) Store Of Value; 3) Unit Of Account ("Price Of Money")
# Contents
# What Does Money Do? Rather Than What Is Money?

What is Money? Hardest question I've come across

- Change from "What is money?" to "What does money do?"
- Money as an idea rather than a thing
From Textbook: Society needs something that is and does the following:
I. Transactions & Means of Payment: Makes payments fast and easy
II. Keeps value over time (store of value - does not decay or melt away like ice or snow)
III. Serves as unit of account - this is really asking "what is the price of money?"
Discuss each of these in turn
# Means Of Payment

Focus on payments process

- So important for real economy
- Involves technology as well as "money"
Before electronics and good communications
- Moved things from one person to another - say gold
With electronics and modern communications
- Move records - of ownership
The thing of money changes (what we use), but payments still necessary and important
- Also see "liquidity" below
# Store Of Value

Means two things:

I. Something that lasts, does not decay or melt

Gold works well - does not rust

- Ice or snow works poorly - melts quickly
- With electronic records, can make them last
II. Also means that the value remains over time
- Something that promises payment in future would work nicely - like a bond that promises coupons and principal
- But bonds sometimes di cult to transfer - need broker account, $\mathrm{T} + 1$ settlement
- Bank deposit works nicely - bank promises to give you cash, but you can transfer easily
Question about "cash" - why does value remain over time?
- Tricky question
- Partly practical: we can use it to pay taxes (government accepts it)
- Partly "magic": everyone believes it will remain
# Hardest Idea - Unit Of Account - The "Price Of Money"

So what the heck is "price of money"?

Hardest idea - for me to explain, and you to understand

- The overall price level (say the CPI) is the "price of money"
- More precisely, "price of money" = $\frac{1}{\text{CPI}}$ so when $\mathbf{P}_{\mathbf{M}}$ CPI
Easiest way to explain:
- If you want to "buy" a \$1 bill, you have to give "goods" (some combination of cups of coffee, books, computer,…)
 - If $price of basket of “goods” is high, then give small quantity of goods – price of that \$1 bill is low
# Hardest Idea - Unit Of Account - The "Price Of Money" Example:
- Years ago, price index
(CPI) was low - years ago a cup of coee cost \$1
 - To "buy" \$1 from me, you needed to give me 1 cup of coee
 - Now, price index
(CPI) is higher – and cup of co ee costs \$5
- To "buy" \$1 from me now, give me just $\frac{1}{5}$ cups of co ee
- The true price of a dollar bill has gone down, from 1 cup to $\frac{1}{5}$ cups of coee
The "price of money" is $\frac{1}{\mathsf{CPI}}$, inverse of the price level
- Overall P "price of money"
Single most important idea for money & inflation
# "Price Of Money" - Price Index - Books & Co Ee
- Let's work through an example, of a price index and "price of money"
Say you are a student, and you only buy books (to read) and co ee (to stay awake reading)
In year 1 you buy 2 books, 4 cups of co ee
- You spend \$24 - your total expenditure
- Call that an Index $= 100$
# Year 2 - All Price Up $50\%$

How much did prices rise? Easy - 50%

- Don't need any fancy index, but let's do it
How much would you spend if you purchased "original basket"?
- Spend \$36
Create Yr 2 Index: 150 - 100 $\frac{36}{24}$
- Ch: $50\%$
# Year 3 - Books Up, Coffee Not

Here'sa problem: books up $25\%$, co ee $0\%$

- Cannot easily calculate "price change"
- But ask: How much would you spend? §42
Create Yr 3 index: $175 = 100\frac{42}{24}$
- Prices rose by $16.7\%$: $\frac{175}{150} = 1.167 = 1 + \% \mathrm{ch}$
# Price Index:Weighted Prices
- Weighted Prices: $\frac{\text{Thisyearexpenditure}}{\text{Year1 expenditure}} = \frac{\mathsf{P}_3^{\text{B}}|Q_1^{\text{B}} + \mathsf{P}_3^{\text{C}}|Q_1^{\text{C}}}{\mathsf{P}_1^{\text{B}}|Q_1^{\text{B}} + \mathsf{P}_1^{\text{C}}|Q_1^{\text{C}}}$
- The CPI - "fixed weight" or Laspeyres-type index
Overstates inflation. In US, estimated at $1.1\%$ per yr
- Much better: implicit price deflator - used for GDP
- See link to example - module in Canvas
# "Price Of Money"
- Ask: how many books/co ee to "buy" \$1?
- Year 1 basket: 2 books & 4 co ee
- Year 1 costs $24 - can "buy" $1 with 1/24 of basket
- Year 2 costs $36 - can buy $1 with 1/36 of same basket
-Dollars are cheaper, get more dollars with less books/co ee
- Year 3 costs $42 - can buy $1 with 1/42 of same basket
-Dollars are even cheaper, get more dollars with less books/co ee
- "Price of Money" = 1/Price Index

```latex
\begin{document}
\begin{tabular}{|c|c|c|c|c|c|c|c|c|c|}
\hline
 & BOOKS & COFFEE & INDEX & & & & & & \\ \hline
Price & % & Quan & Price & % & Quant & Expend & Index & % & \\ \hline
Year 1 & $8 & & 2 & $2 & & 4 & $24 & 100 & \\ \hline
Year 2 & $12 & 50.0% & & $3 & 50.0% & & $36 & 150 & 50.0%50.0% \\ \hline
Year 3 & $15 & 25.0% & & $3 & 0.0% & & $42 & 175 & 16.7%16.7% \\ \hline
\end{tabular}
\end{document}
```

# 2.3 Currency As Money
# Contents
# Remember What Does Money Do?

What is Money? Hardest question I've come across

- Change from "What is money?" to "What does money do?"
- Money as an idea rather than a thing
From Textbook: Society needs something that is and does the following:
I. Makes payments fast and easy
II. Keeps value over time (store of value - does not decay or melt away like ice or snow)
III. Serves as unit of account
Discuss each of these in turn
# So What Is "Money"? - Start With Currency
- Start with currency - pieces of paper printed by the government
Example: Zimbabwe 20-dollar bill
- No photo of US $ - not legal
-Zimbabwe fun-2nd worst inflation ever
- Anyone my age has used currency
- Worked pretty well as money for many years
![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-15/c441f5b5-2f9d-4e4d-bc65-1301c02e96db/66dc6444f0fd7469afbe2bb0abfc0a4ee312a29fa59a32d039d24298ceef38bb.jpg)
# Currency Is (Or Was) Pretty Good As Money

A piece of paper printed by the government

- Payments: very fast and easy - in 1920s when many transactions face-to-face. (Less now with buying and selling electronically)
- Keeps value: lasts pretty well, does not "melt" like ice
- Unit of account: need to think about this
- Liquid: easy to buy / sell
- Pretty good as "money" for many years
![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-15/c441f5b5-2f9d-4e4d-bc65-1301c02e96db/bb10b98916671e437f704006a9db364f7a7d46641f55fe1e3b1d5a029ed1a054.jpg)

2007-2008, severe hyperinflation

# How Is Currency Issued In Us?
- Issued by Federal Reserve, the US Central Bank
- Liability of the Fed, and thus US Government
Fed Balance Sheet, 2007
- Currency by far largest liability
- Assets: mostly US Gov't bonds
- See Puzzles of Inflation, Money, and Debt by Coleman, Oliver, Siegel (on Canvas Modules)

(mainly) to public

- People use (or used to use) currency as money
- For payments, to store value

# 2.4 More Money - Demand Deposits
# Contents
# More Money - Demand Deposits
- Currency good for face-to-face - not as good for distant and electronic transactions
- Need something that can be easily stored and transferred electronically
- In the late 1800s, banks (at least in US) started issuing demand deposits
- A special type of security, a loan to a bank,
- Bank promises to pay back on demand and at par (1\$ for every 1\$ loaned)
- Special kind of loan, two special characteristics
I. Promise to pay back on demand & at par - $1-per-$1
II. Upon default, pay first-come - first-served
- If you're late, you get nothing
- Strong incentive to get first in line - Bank Runs
- Very different than other loans
(bonds) - Regular bond-holders, upon default, get pro-rata share (assets divided according to payment hierarchy)
- Works nicely as money
- Transfer from one bank to another
- Originally by paper check, now electronically
# Reserves To Back Deposits
- Banks need to be able to pay back deposits
(loans) when demanded
- Will hold currency or reserves
- Only hold
(maybe) \$1 reserves for \$10 deposits
- On any day, only some depositors ask for currency
- Reserves
- Held partly in currency, but mostly on account at Fed
- Fed will deliver the currency if needed
Remember First Republic balance sheet
FRB Actual 2022 ($bn)

```latex
\begin{document}
\begin{tabular}{|c|c|c|c|}
\hline
ASSETS & LIABILITIES & & \\ \hline
Loans & 166.1 & 176.4 & Deposits \\ \hline
Investments & 33.6 & 19.5 & Other \\ \hline
Other & 13.7 & 17.5 & Equity \\ \hline
Total & 213.4 & 213.4 & Total \\ \hline
\end{tabular}
\end{document}
```

Hidden away (probably in that "Other") are reserves - either cash held in their vaults or (more likely) held at their local Federal Reserve Bank

# Narrow Money (M1) - Picture
- Now 2 forms of money: currency + demand deposits
- Called "Narrow Money" or M1
- Fed issues Currency + Reserves - High Powered Money or Monetary Base

Note that the numbers here (from Fed's Z-1) do not match the money supply numbers (from $H_{0}$0.6). Reasons are:
- Currency:
- $H_{0}$0.6 is currency outside U. S. Treasury, Federal Reserve Banks, and vaults of depository institutions
- For Dec 2007 $H_{0}$0.6 says: 760.6, Z-1 (2020;$q_{2}$ Sept 2020) says "currency outside banks" 773.9. Not sure why di erent. Maybe Z-1 includes US Treasury cash?
- For Dec 2019 $H_{0}$0.6 says: 1,1710.9, Z-1 (2020;$q_{2}$ Sept 2020) says "currency outside banks" 1,724.2. Not sure why di erent. Maybe Z-1 includes US Treasury cash?
Deposits (checking accounts)
- For Dec 2007 $H_{0}$0.6 is "demand deposits" = 301.7 + "Other Checkable Deposits" = 304.8. = 606.5 for domestically chartered commercial banks, U. S. branches and agencies of foreign banks, and Edge Act corporations (excluding those amounts held by depository institutions, the U. S. government, and foreign banks and o cial institutions) less cash items in the process of collection and Federal Reserve float.
- I think this excludes credit unions and Foreign Banking O ces in US,
- Z-1 is \$740.8. Foreign Banking O ces in US = \$11.8 and credit unions = \$73.7. So less those is \$655.3, closer to $H_{0}$0.6
- For Dec 2019 $H_{0}$0.6 is "demand deposits" = 1,592.7 + "Other Checkable Deposits" = 674.5. = 2,267.1 for domestically chartered commercial banks, U. S. branches and agencies of foreign banks, and Edge Act corporations (excluding those amounts held by depository institutions, the U. S. government, and foreign banks and oci al institutions) less cash items in the process of collection and Federal Reserve float.
- I think this excludes credit unions and Foreign Banking O ces in US,
- Z-1 is \$2,637.8. Foreign Banking O ces in US = \$188.5 and credit unions = \$212.1. So less those is \$2,235.4, pretty close to $H_{0}$0.6

# Standard Money Creation Story

Fed Issues HPM - Currency + Reserves

- Currency = paper money
- Banks use reserves to create deposit money
U. S. Debt and Monetary Environment, end-2007, $tn
![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-15/c441f5b5-2f9d-4e4d-bc65-1301c02e96db/6533298f664c3f9e95ab6ae3d009cbf2d31b97d7b359b35121d51769c1b670b4.jpg)
Arrows indicate the holdings of bonds issued by the Treasury (for example bonds issued by the Treasury and held by the Federal Reserve) or reserves issued by the Fed. Bonds are acquired by transfer of cash or other assets but those transfers are not shown in this diagram. Abbreviations: GSEs = Government Sponsored Enterprises; Inst = Instruments; Mige = Mortgage; Savg = Savings *21 table $L_{209}$ (&$D_{3}$); **21 table $L_{109}$; ***21 table $L_{110}$-$L_{115}$
# Standard Money Creation Story

Fed controls

(slows) money growth by

I. Controlling issuance of currency

II. Controlling reserves (making reserves expensive, so banks create fewer demand deposits)

Fed makes reserves expensive by increasing interest rate (Fed Funds rate)

- Before 2008, banks earned $0\%$ interest on reserves
- If hold reserves, expensive (relative to lending to customers)
NOW, Ready for Money, Inflation, and Quantity Theory
# 3 Money & Inflation
# 3.1 Quantity Theory Of Money
# Contents
# Quantity Theory Of Money - Standard Theory Of Inflation & Money
- We want to understand causes of inflation
- Quantity Theory is oldest & best-understood
Quantity Equation
$$
\mathbf {M}_{\mathrm{i}} \mathbf {V} = \mathbf {P}_{\mathrm{i}} \mathbf {Q}
$$
$$
\begin{array}{r} \mathsf {M} = \text{qu an ti ty of}" \text{mo ne y}" \\ \mathsf {V} = \text{ve lo ci ty} \end{array}
$$

$\mathbf{P} =$ price level

$$
\mathbb {Q} (\text{or} \Upsilon) = \text{qu an ti ty of ou tp ut}
$$
- Goal: understand how & why of Quantity Theory
# Quantity Equation - Transactions Version - Money In Motion
- Remember Uses of Money
- Payments - focus on this here
-Store of value
- Quantity of money used in transactions
$$
\begin{array}{r l} \mathbf {M} & = \text{qu an ti ty of}" \text{mo ne y}" \\ \mathbf {V} & = \text{ve lo ci ty} \end{array}
$$
$$
\mathrm{M}_{\mathrm{i}} \mathrm{V} = \mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{i}} \mathrm{T}
$$
$$
\begin{array}{c} \mathbf {P} = \text{pricel ev el} \\ \mathbf {T} = \text{tr an sa ct io ns (du ri ng ye ar)} \end{array}
$$
- Think about using currency in 1890
- Each transaction used currency, more T more M
- "Velocity" measures how many times each piece of money used in a year
- If more transactions, need more money
- If $\mathbf{T}$ and not more $\mathbf{M}$, then $\mathbf{P}$
- Similarly, if M and no change in T, then P
# Cambridge Cash Balance - Money At Rest
- Remember Uses of Money - focus now on
- Payments
- Store of value
- Quantity of money as an asset in HH balance sheet
$$
\mathbf {M} = \mathbf {k}_{\mathrm{i}} \mathbf {P}_{\mathrm{i}} \mathbf {Q}
$$

M = quantity of "money"

 k = "Cambridge k" = 1/V

P = price level

$\mathsf{Q}$ (or $\mathsf{Y}$ ) = quantity of output

- "Temporary abode of purchasing power"
- Part of HH balance sheet, portfolio & asset allocation decision
- Focus on di erent aspect of use of money
- Still implies connection money & prices:
- If $\mathbf{Q}$ and not more $\mathbf{M}$, then $\mathbf{P}$
- Similarly, if $\mathbf{M}$ and no change in $\mathbf{Q}$, then $\mathbf{P}$
# Quantity Theory Of Money

Actual Theory of money supply and demand

-Friedman&Schwartz, MonetaryHistoryoftheUS

- Friedman's entry in New Palgrave - on Canvas
- Equation looks the same
$\mathbf{M}^{\mathrm{D}} =$ money demand
$\mathsf{R}^{\mathrm{e}} =$ exp returns on assets
$$
\mathbf {M}^{\mathrm{D}} = \mathbf {P}_{\mathrm{i}} \mathbf {k}_{\mathrm{j}} \mathbf {Q}
$$
$$
\mathbf {M}^{\mathrm{D}} = \mathbf {P}_{\mathrm{i}} \mathbf {f} (\mathbf {y}, \mathbf {R}^{\mathrm{e}}, \mathbf {\mu}, \mathbf {w}; \mathbf {u})
$$

$\mathbf{y} =$ income w = wealth

= expected inflation

$\mathsf{u} =$ all other things

- Combines demand for money due to "payments" and "store of value"
- Retains idea that M & P move 1-for-1 when other things held constant
- Money demand will go up & down as income, expected inflation,… change
# Equilibrium: Demand = Supply - Supply Shift
- Generally, supply set "exogenously" - by outside factors (amount of gold, or choice of central bank)
$$
\mathbf {M}^{\mathrm{D}} = \mathbf {P}_{i} f (\mathbf {y}, \mathbf {R}^{\mathrm{e}},, w; u)
$$
- Better to work with real money demand:
$$
\mathbf {m}^{\mathrm{D}} = \frac{\mathbf {M}^{\mathrm{D}}}{\mathbf {P}} = \mathbf {f} (\mathbf {y}, \mathbf {R}^{\mathrm{e}}, \mathbf {\Delta}, \mathbf {w}; \mathbf {u})
$$
- Keep $y, \mathbf{R}^{\mathrm{e}}$,… (demand curve) fixed
- Shift supply out
- Equilibrate, "price of money"
- "Price of money" = 1/CPI
- So CPI
(prices)
![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-15/c441f5b5-2f9d-4e4d-bc65-1301c02e96db/5c8f2ecf11f6c9a3221f8734e001e18597d1432e81e557bdac39e63430cd881f.jpg)
Quantity of Money
# Equilibrium: Demand = Supply - Demand Shift
- What happens when demand shifts?
$$
\mathbf {m}^{\mathrm{D}} = \frac{\mathbf {M}^{\mathrm{D}}}{\mathbf {P}} = \mathbf {f} (\mathbf {y}, \mathbf {R}^{\mathrm{e}}, \mathbf {\Delta}, \mathbf {w}; \mathbf {u})
$$

Now, $y, R^{\mathbf{e}}$, or… shift, and $M^{\mathbf{D}}$

- Shift Demand out
- What happens to prices?
- Now, "price of money"
- "Price of money" = 1/cPI
- So CPI
(prices) - Incredibly important for 2008
- And any other liquidity crisis (1893,1907,1930)
![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-15/c441f5b5-2f9d-4e4d-bc65-1301c02e96db/142dbf24e9941e420139054a40ad13fc154101bf32d4ac999357e11072bec7fa.jpg)
# 3.2 Puzzle 1: What Is A Liquidity Crisis? Quantity Theory Says: "Money Demand, Prices "
# Contents
# Liquidity Crisis: Demand For Liquid Assets (Money!) Surges

"Liquidity Crisis" is when everyone gets scared and panics

- Get rid of risky assets, try to hold the safest assets
Usually some form of "Money"
In 1900, safest "money" was currency: gold, or (almost as good) Federal notes convertible into gold
- Bank deposits were used as money but during crisis, they became more risky
- Bank might fail, like SVB did in March 2023
Now, safest asset might be insured deposit, or a US Treasury Bill, or a Money Market Fund from a high-quality manager, investing in government bills
- Absolutely not the uninsured deposits of Silicon Valley Bank, or First Republic Bank
# Liquidity Crisis: Demand For Liquid Assets (Money!) Surges Everyone Wants More Money
- Many things happen - prices of risky assets, people try to move out of risky banks
- For money demand, $u$ shifts ( $y$… stay the same) and real money demand $\mathbf{m}^{\mathbf{D}}$.
$$
\mathbf {m}^{\mathrm{D}} = \frac{\mathbf {M}^{\mathrm{D}}}{\mathbf {P}} = \mathbf {f} (\mathbf {y}, \mathbf {R}^{\mathrm{e}}, \mathbf {\Delta}, \mathbf {w}; \mathbf {u})
$$
- Shift Demand out
- What happens to prices?
- Now, "price of money"
- "Price of money" = 1/cPI
- So CPI
(prices)
![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-15/c441f5b5-2f9d-4e4d-bc65-1301c02e96db/e5c1482f089fafeeb45f4d5d28c8a44b71671cc1843652e8aa4e1b52d9ab1a54.jpg)
Incredibly important for understanding liquidity crises
2008. And any other liquidity crisis (1893,1907,1930)
Liquidity Crisis: Demand for Liquid Assets (Money!) Surges During liquidity crises, prices fall

The NBER peak/trough dating is from the NBER web-site (http://www.nber.org/cycles.html). The change in prices and income are based on annual data from [Friedman and SchwartzFriedman and Schwartz1982] table 4.8. Change in prices, income, and money are the compounded annual percent change from the year in which the NBER peak occurs to the year in which the NBER trough occurs. I use per-capita real income and per-capita money stock. Income "refers to annual estimates of net national product, variant III, component method, computed by Simon Kuznets, in current and 1929 prices, 1869-1947, as revised by [Friedman and Schwartz] for 1869-1909,1917-19, and 1942-1945." Prices are "the implicit price index obtained by dividing national product in current prices by national product in 1929 prices." For 2007 and 2020, the price changes are for the PCE price index, FRED PCEPI

For FRED PCEPI as of 30-apr-2023

# The Enduring Puzzle Of Money & Inflation

What is money?

What causes inflation?

Two of the most enduring puzzles in macroeconomics

Particularly relevant today

- And why was it so low post-2008?
- Burst of inflation 2021-2022 - highest in $40+$ years
- Only to surge in 2022?
![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-15/c441f5b5-2f9d-4e4d-bc65-1301c02e96db/014b1b2d11687a149d91f5c6042aa2694541a26caea755f0e8c751ea09be2314.jpg)
![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-15/c441f5b5-2f9d-4e4d-bc65-1301c02e96db/e70b55534bc3d5484e47d5fd4c3503814ebd5c635031846f4ffaa15a9f3a58f9.jpg)
# What I Want To Cover - Facts, Theories, Puzzles Of Money & Inflation

Fact: "Inflation is always and everywhere a monetary phenomenon" (Milton Friedman)

- What is inflation? Some history and a little theory.
- Evidence & Theory say Money & inflation move up & down together. M & P
- Theory
(theories) :
- Quantity Theory of Money
(Friedman) : Money growth causes inflation: M
- New Fiscal Theory of the Price Level
(FTPL) - Puzzle 1: What happens during a "liquidity crisis"?
- In fact, what is a "liquidity crisis"?
- Puzzle 2: Standard Monetary Theory did not work in 2000s
- Post-2008: M, rates low, inflation low (should have been high)
- Pandemic: M, rates low, inflation high (but why did erent from post-2008?)
- Puzzle 3: What happens when traditional "money" disappears?
- How to think about inflation with electronic money, blockchain?
Solution: New Monetary Theory: Fiscal Theory of the Price Level
- NOT MMT (Modern Monetary Theory) - a load of hooey

# 1 Puzzle 2: Standard Monetary Theory Did Not Work In 2000S
# Contents
# Quantity Theory Of Money: Quantity Causes Inflation

This did not work during the 2000s

- 2000-2007: money growth ($M_{1}$ & Reserves) moderate, inflation
(PCE) moderate - OK
- 2007-2015: money growth fast, inflation lower - what happened?
- 2019-2022: money growth also fast, now inflation high - what changed?

```latex
\begin{document}
\begin{tabular}{|c|c|c|c|}
\hline
 & 2000-2007 & 2007-2015 & 2019-2022 \\ \hline
$M_{1}$ & 3.38% & 10.71% & \\ \hline
Reserves & 1.93% & 54.57% & 23.04% \\ \hline
Debt/GDP & 8.46 & 40.5 & 13.31 \\ \hline
PCE & 2.39% & 1.31% & 4.16% \\ \hline
UST 3-mth & 2.90% & 0.27% & 0.85% \\ \hline
\end{tabular}
\end{document}
```

New Keynesian Interest Rate Theory (inflation theory used by central banks) says low rates higher inflation

In 2007-2015, low rates low inflation

In 2019-2022, low rates high inflation

Post-2008 Fast Money, Slow Inflation - QTM vs FTPL

Post-2008: Money (both $M_{1}$ & reserves

(HPM) ) grew faster, prices slower

Pandemic (2021-2022): Money grew faster, and now inflation surged

Something is wrong

Blue = Inflation, Green = Reserves

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-15/c441f5b5-2f9d-4e4d-bc65-1301c02e96db/02043490015d9e960e9a4440f249123e7fac72c7a15c79eb80780e8d7d5548e6.jpg)

Blue = Inflation, Green = Reserves

# 2 Fiscal Theory Of The Price Level
# Contents
# Money In Today'S World

Money in 1800s, 1900s:

- Gold bars, paper currency, bank (checking accounts)
-Pay by giving someone gold, paper, or a check
"Money" today:
- Electronic deposits, electronic funds transfers
- PayPal, Venmo, Alipay
- Quantity Equation not so useful
$$
\mathbf {M}_{\text{i}} \mathbf {V} = \mathbf {P}_{\text{i}} \mathbf {Q}
$$
- What is M? Zero? (we don't need currency) Infinite? (everything is money)
- What is V? Infinite? (no "money") Zero? (everything is money)
Quantity Theory has not worked so well post-2008
- Need something new
Originally developed by Eric Leeper, with John Cochrane (formerly Chicago) doing much work now
# New Idea: Fiscal Theory Of The Price Level

(Ftpl) - Exceedingly Simple: go back to bonds

 - Example: \$6.50 every year for 4yrs, then \$100
- PV today is discounted value of future CFs
- Simple here: $6.5\%$ fixed for 4 years, then 100
- 99.658 = PV(6.5 + 100 @ rate = 6.6%)
- But CFs don't have to be fixed
- Same discounting ideas apply to stocks: dividend discount model
Stock Price $=$ PV(future dividends)
- Apply to government liabilities
![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-15/c441f5b5-2f9d-4e4d-bc65-1301c02e96db/4c38c5ee1e67a84c0dcf0b75719a8397e102eb135c5c1083eeaab6abe0471d0b.jpg)
# Ftpl: Discounting Future Revenue
- Apply idea of discounting future CFs to gov't
- Present Gov't Liabilities: Money + Bonds: $\frac{\mathsf{M} + \mathsf{B}}{\mathsf{P}}$
- Future Gov't Revenue: Primary Surplus: Revenue less Spending
- Primary surplus - excluding interest payments
- Conditioned to think of ordinary surplus, but wrong (for this)
- Need to think about CFs that can pay o liabilities
Real Primary Surplus: measured in quantity of goods & services
- Valuation (just like bond, equity):
Present Liabilities $=$ EPV(Future Revenue)
$$
\frac{\mathrm{M} + \mathrm{B}}{\mathrm{P}} = \mathrm{EP V} (\mathrm{Pr im Su rp})
$$
# Ftpl: Discounting Future Revenue

Present Liabilities = EPV(Future Revenue)

$$
\frac{\mathbf {M} + \mathbf {B}}{\mathbf {P}} = \mathbf {E P V} (\mathbf {P r i m S u r p})
$$

Same as a share of IBM or Apple:

- Money (and bonds) Valuable today because get future payments
- For IBM: get dollars
- For $\mathbf{M} + \mathbf{B}$: real goods & services the gov't extracts in taxes
(PrimSurp) # Quantity Theory Vs Fiscal Theory
QTM: Real Balance Size of Economy
$$
\frac{M}{P} = \frac{Q}{V} \text{or} M V = P Q
$$
- If economy grows
(Q) & money doesn't, prices down
(P) - Money supply up $(\mathsf{M}^{\mathsf{S}})$, prices up $(\mathsf{P})$
Money growth causes inflation
Real money demand up $\left(\frac{\mathbf{M}^{\mathbf{D}}}{\mathbf{P}}\right)$, prices down
(P) unless $\mathbf{M}^{\mathbf{S}}$
- Liquidity crises (as in 1907,1930s, 2008)
FTPL: Present Liabilities = EPV(Future Revenue)
$$
\frac{\mathrm{M} + \mathrm{B}}{\mathrm{P}} = \text{EP V (Pr im Su rp)}
$$

Balance today vs tomorrow matters

- Trading money for bonds does not matter $(\mathbf{M} + \mathbf{B}$ matters)
- If $M + B$ today but expected future surplus, maybe no inflation
- EPV
(PrimSurp) can go up because
- Expected future growth and increased taxes
Today discount rate
# Primary Surplus: Government Revenue

(Taxes) Less Spending

- Do not include interest payments (ordinary surplus / deficit)
# Primary Vs Ordinary Surplus
- Everyone worries
(rightly) about surplus
(deficit) - US deficit 1947-2007: $1.6\%$ of GDP (negative surplus)
- Primary Surplus 1947-2007: +0.3%
![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-15/c441f5b5-2f9d-4e4d-bc65-1301c02e96db/c4125f769b0cf8bd9647b59673d62e4e8260b8f8b9f5bb29310e60c3ac6aff19.jpg)
FTPL Helps Understand Post-2008 and Pandemic
Now, look at all gov't liabilities (not just money): $\frac{\mathsf{M} + \mathsf{B}}{\mathsf{P}} = \mathsf{EPV}(\mathsf{PrimSurp})$
Post-2008: money, but inflation low
- Money & Reserves (reserves, green)
Inflation low (PCE, blue)
![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-15/c441f5b5-2f9d-4e4d-bc65-1301c02e96db/12a9d169a5f50cc3c091db73adf24a3eadf5d5a31dcaccc4ae15c480c62a2cd4.jpg)
Likely explanation: switch bonds for money (& liquidity demand )
- M+B matters, not M alone
Post-2008: debt, but inflation low
- Debt (debt-to-GDP, red)
Inflation low (PCE, blue)
![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-15/c441f5b5-2f9d-4e4d-bc65-1301c02e96db/7fc299b9dafad5e113b2c46359ddc1a4f862385311afaacfa3c043d53f894529.jpg)
Likely explanation: debt but used to repair financial system
- Markets expect PrimSurp will go up in future
Pandemic: debt again, now inflation high
- Debt (debt-to-GDP, red)
Inflation low (PCE, blue)
![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-15/c441f5b5-2f9d-4e4d-bc65-1301c02e96db/0f7b441ed77c7f2c444c349eab3a1a043cf2c9e18b3aa6f7c35c57d986d85a47.jpg)
Likely explanation: Gov't borrowed (created money) directly into our bank accounts
- No expectation PrimSurp
(taxes) up in future
FTPL: Balances Borrowing Today vs Payback Tomorrow FTPL is about balance between current liabilities & future cash flow
$$
\frac{\mathbf {M} + \mathbf {B}}{\mathbf {P}} = \mathbf {E P V} (\mathbf {P r i m S u r p})
$$

With FTPL we see two possibilities (two scenarios for monetary & fiscal authorities to coordinate):

I. Bonds & Future Surplus together: nothing

- Liabilities $(\mathsf{M} + \mathsf{B})$ balance future cash flows (EPV(PrimSurp))
- Ricardian Equivalence
II. Bonds, Future Surplus (no change): prices have to rise
- Liabilities go up but real value of future CFs do not change
- Something has to happen: P
Why is this useful? Helps understand 2008 & 2021
2008: money & bonds, inflation low
2021: money & bonds, high inflation
# 3 Puzzle 3: How Does Ftpl Help Understand Money & Blockchain?
# Contents

FTPL Works With No "Money"

FTPL says

$$
\frac{\mathrm{M} + \mathrm{B}}{\mathrm{P}} = \text{EP V (Pr im Su rp)}
$$
- What happens in $\mathbf{M} = 0$? Nothing. Gov't bonds B still there
Bonds set value of a "dollar" (price level) because bonds are paid back in "dollars"
- Cryptocurrencies change nothing about FTPL
- May make payments easier (good thing) but prices and dollars still set by gov't debt and promise to pay back (EPV
(PrimSurp) )

- Quantity & Price both up

# Problem: Applying To Aggregate Supply & Demand Have Same Picture For Supply & Demand
- If Demand shifts, Q Growth
Appealing (and long-lived) idea:
- Growth Prices(inflation)
Seems it has to be right - Supply & Demand Mistake we make: Confusion Over Prices
- Absolute vs Relative Prices
- "Prices" (overall price level) not "price of aggregate output"
- Overall price level is the "price of money"
Confusion of words, confusion of concepts
![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-15/c441f5b5-2f9d-4e4d-bc65-1301c02e96db/25c5b4b3232085b46173e627bb394c653098770aaa1f8a56711039a47d3be078.jpg)
AGGREGATE Demand Shifts Out
# Why Are "Prices" (Overall Price Index) The "Price Of Money" We Report Prices In Dollars, But What Matters Is What We Have To Give Up - Relative Prices Back To Shoes - We Say "\$150"
- But care about how many other things we give up
- The "price" of \$150 tells us how much we give up
So what is "price of money"
- What do we give up to get \$1?
Go pack to measuring "price index"
![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-15/c441f5b5-2f9d-4e4d-bc65-1301c02e96db/6858310854179f2cd9fea71fe96a1034d9876b993982591a487e206c08ba7ced.jpg)
Demand Shifts Out
Why are "Prices" (overall price index) the "Price of Money"
- Ask: how many baskets of books/co ee to "buy" §1?
- Use "year 1 basket": 2 books & 4 co ee
- Year 1: basket costs $24 - can "buy" $1 with 1/24 of basket
- Year 2: basket costs $36 - can buy $1 with 1/36 of basket
- Dollars are cheaper, get more dollars with less books/coe
- "Price of Money in Terms of Goods" = 1/PriceLevel
Di cult idea to accept
- Many people say "interest rate is the price of money" - but wrong
But it is right - Priceofmoney Pricelndex
But if "prices" (price index) is "price of money" it cannot be "price of aggregate output"

# But – Some Evidence For Short-Term Phillips Curve Original Work By William Phillips

(Weak) evidence that Unemployment Prices

- Britain, 1861-1957
- Short step from Unemployment to Growth
Formed foundation for Keynesian economics in 1950s, 1960s, 1970s
- Economic policies that generated high inflation in US, Britain, Europe
In 1960s, Milton Friedman & Edmund Phelps argued
(showed) no long-term Phillips curve
# And Two Remaining Puzzles

I. If Theory says Growth & Prices don't go together long-term, why do we see they do short-term?

- Friedman, expectations & unexpected inflation
- Next slides
II. If Theory (and evidence) says no long-term Phillips Curve, why do people still use it?
- On this, I have no possible answer
# Friedman: Theory Says No Long-Term W &Ru

Natural Rate of Unemployment

- Unemployment depends on real variables: productivity, real wages, worker preferences
- Nothing to do with nominal wages
- Not "Natural" as in "Good" - depends on real variables
Strong economic theory, was no Phillips Curve
# But: Short-Term W &Ru

Unexpected Inflation

Firms: assume P means real price of their product up

- Di cult to separate inflation (all prices) from real price rise (only firm's product price up)
- Thinking their price up, raise wages to hire workers
Workers: assume W means real wage up
- So work more (increase labor supply) and unemployment down
# Unexpected Inflation Short-Run Phillips Curve

Unexpected inflation causes both firms and workers to make mistake

- Firms think price of their good up, increase output, raise wage
- Workers think their real wage up, increase working
But the unexpected inflation only fools people short-term
Eventually, back to Natural Rate of Unemployment
- Determined by productivity, etc, not inflation
# Friedman And Phelps

Milton Friedman, 1976 Nobel Prize lecture

Edmund Phelps, 1967

Friedman's argument for why short-term P Unempl

- Employers: see price of their products, mistakenly think it is relative price rather than inflation. Increase their wages to get more labor (increase labor demand)
- Workers: see their wages go up, mistakenly think it is relative wages and not inflation. Work more (increase labor supply)
- But eventually, firms and workers learn they are mistaken, go back to original labor demand and supply
Long-term, no Phillips curve - no Inflation-Output relation

- FRB shareholders lose everything

# Frb Is Not Insolvent
# Solvency Risk

Depositors worried investments may go down more

- Regular debts (like for Apple) cannot withdraw early
Deposits can, and rational to do so
- Get out now, before things get worse
# Liquidity Risk

Even if knew for sure assets would not go down

- Assets may be illiquid – take a few months or year to sell o
- Depositors may need money back sooner
Rational to get first in line, take money out now
First Republic Bank was not insolvent - but subject to liquidity problems and a bank run
- Bank run – where customers withdraw deposits because they are worried the bank will get into trouble in the futures
This is exactly what happened to First Republic

# Crises We Focus On

1893,1907 - Banking panic, suspension of convertibility

1920s - Bank failures

1930-33 - Great Depression

These all exhibit the fragility of the unit banking structure

I. Lack of diversification within banks (geographic and type of loan)

II. Concentration of reserves in New York City (country banks put reserves on deposit with NY banks)

III. Fragmented industry of small independent banks cannot coordinate response to liquidity shock

1980s - S&L crisis, unwinding of the unit banking structure

- Perverse deposit insurance incentives, changing demographics, inflation, technology - all conspired to undo unit banking
Digression on Deposit Insurance
# 1.2 Pre-History (Before 1830)
# Contents
# Pre-History (Before 1830) Over-View
- Prior to independence, US banking & finance limited, haphazard
- The War of Independence of 1775-1783 expensive (4-5x inc)
- By late 1780s the US free but e ectively in default
- Fundamental problem: Under Articles of Confederation States could tax, the central government not
- More fundamental, subtle, and important problem - British trade, discrimination, and playing o one state vs another
# Constitution As Fiscal "Grand Bargain"
- Move from weak central government (Articles of Confederation) to strong Federal government (US Constitution) was grand bargain:
- States gave taxation to the Federal government,
- Federal government took over state debts (bailed out the states)
War of Independence 1776-1783
Articles of Confederation
1777: finished drafting
1781: ratified
1789: superseded by Constitution
Debt in 1790, roughly $79\mathrm{mn}$, of which states owed \$25\mathrm{mn}\ (1/3)\. 40% of GDP (taxes small)
Problem central gov't no authority to tax, no source of revenue to pay debt
New Constitution March 4,1789
Authority to tax taken from states and given to Federal
Government. In return, Federal gov't took over state debts:
 bailed out the states
# Hamilton, 1790, & Six Components Of Modern Financial System

Hamilton in 1789-1792 engineered a financial revolution

Sylla: When Hamilton left o ce in 1795, his adopted homeland harbored six critical institutional components that characterize modern financial systems:

I. stable public finances and debt management;

II. stable money;

III. an e ective central bank;

IV. a functioning banking system;

V. active securities markets;

VI. a growing number of business corporations, financial and non-financial

# Hamilton & Us Financial Revolution - Long-Term Banking Failure

Hamilton restructured US debt, founded 1st Bank of the US But in a real sense banking reform failed

- 1st Bank of the US charter not renewed - closed 1811
2nd Bank of the US 1816-1836
By 1830s, state control of bank charters well-established
1830s - 1990: state control, no national banking
US banking fragmented, inciient, fragile
Coalition of populist agrarians (distrusted large east-coast banks) and small bankers (wanted protection from large banks) - controlled banking for 160 years
# Remember Discussion From Lecture 4:

There was a high-level financial policy debate of sorts in the mid-1790s. On one side was Albert Gallatin, a Republican congressman from western Pennsylvania and future treasury secretary in the Jeerson and Madison administrations. Gallatin was the Republican opposition's financial expert, a role that corresponded to Hamilton's role in the Federalist Party. ([Sylla(2010)]p 74)

Like other Republican leaders, Gallatin thought most public debts were bad, and his principal charge against Hamilton's policies was that the assumption of state debts in 1790 had made the national debt larger than it needed to be by at least \$10.9 million. (p 75)

Gallatin closed his Sketch with a challenge to the view that the debt funding plan "had created a large productive capital which did not exist before." His view was that "every nation is enfeebled by a public debt," and that the best policy was to extinguish public debt as quickly as it was feasible to do so by cutting spending and increasing taxes. He lamented the fact that foreign investors had purchased so much of the U. S. debt and other American securities, which only led the American sellers "to consume, to spend more, and they have consumed and spent extravagantly."

Taking in the great number of elegant houses which have been built within a few years in all the large cities, and which, however convenient to the inhabitants, a ord no additional revenue to the nation, it may be asserted that the greater part of the capital thus drawn from Europe for purchases of stock has been actually consumed, without leaving in its stead any other productive capital, and thus as the nation still owes the whole, it has been impoverished even by the only consequence of the funding system that has made any temporary addition to the apparent wealth of the country. That wealth is, in a great degree, consumed and destroyed, and the whole debt remains to be paid. (Adams 1960, III, 149)

Substitute China for Europe in this passage, and Gallatin's message would seem rather similar to arguments one often hears today, more than two centuries later. Other passages in his essay indicate that he would subscribe to "crowding out"—the idea that public borrowing and spending reduces private investment—as well as Ricardian equivalence—the notion that people react to increases in public debt by increasing their savings in order to be able to pay the increased taxes necessary to service a larger public debt. (p 79)

# 1.3 Precis Of Banking History 1830-1990 - Us Unit Banking
# Contents
# Time-Line Of Unit Banking History, 1830-1990

1791-1811 1st Bank of the United States

1816-1836 2nd Bank of the United States

- Up to 1836: Substantial limits on number of banks, states extract revenue
- 1836-1860: State chartering, "free banking", revenue by "reserves"
1860s: Introduction of "national banks" but still unit banks
1860-1913s: Expansion of unit banks
- 1907: Failures, some move to loosen unit banking rules
1913: Federal Reserve established
- 1920s, 1930-33: Failures, some move to loosen unit banking rules
# Time-Line Of Unit Banking History, 1830-1990

1933: Glass-Steagall Act

- Deposit Insurance
- Outlawed interest on deposits (Reg Q)
- Restricted "groups" of banks
- 1980-1990s: Collapse of unit banking, cross-state branch banking
1980s: S&L crisis - 1400 S&Ls and 1300 other banks failed
 1980: deposit insurance raised to \$100k/account - to protect S&Ls
1982: Congress amended Bank Holding Act of 1956
- 1994: Riegle-Neal Interstate Banking and Branching E ciency Act
# Us Unit Banking

From about 1830-1980s banking e ectively controlled at state level

Most banks no branches

In 1914 - 27,349 banks in US, $95\%$ no branches

US di erent from rest of world

- Only country with this kind of unit banking structure. Eg Canada: 38 banks in 1890,22 in 1922
- Only country experiencing recurring panics by end of 19th c (next slide)
- According to Calomiris & Haber. Reinhart & Rogo would disagree. But C&H certainly right in comparing US & Canada
US 1914: 27,349, $95\%$ no branches: [Calomiris and Haber(2015)] p 181
# Us Unit Banking

Result of political coalition between agrarian populists & local bankers

- Possible because banking laws set at state level
- Groups within state (with tacit consent of state gov't) could act in their own interest, contrary to national interest
-Benefits local bankers (earn monopoly rents)
- Potentially (particularly in early 1800s) benefitted states – revenue from bank charter fees
Unit banking system vulnerable due to 3 weaknesses
I. Lack of diversification within banks (geographic and type of loan)
II. Concentration of reserves in New York City (country banks put reserves on deposit with NY banks)
III. Fragmented industry of small independent banks cannot coordinate response to liquidity shock
# Post Civil War Growth In Number Of Banks

Number of banks really grew after Civil War

- In 1914 - 27,349 banks in US, $95\%$ no branches (C&H Figure 6.2)

# 2 Financial Crisis Narratives 1893,1907,1920S
# 2.1 Financial Crisis Caused By Surge In Liquidity Demand
# Contents
# Financial Crisis Caused By Surge In Liquidity Demand Each Crisis Followed Pattern:
- Environment: Fragile unit banking system with many small fragile banks, ripe for bank run
- Something triggers concern among customers, starts bank runs, contagion across banks
- Liquidity demand, Money supply relatively fixed, Quantity of Money
- $\mathbf{M}^{\mathsf{D}}$, $\mathbf{P}$
(money) means Price Level
- Banks start to contract, sometimes suspend
(close) Economy starts to collapse
-Sometimesbadly(1897,1903), sometimesreallybadly(1930s)
# Liquidity Crisis: Cash

(Money) Demand, Supply Flat Results When Cash Demand Spikes Up And Supply Can'T Keep Up: Banks

- Customers ask for cash,
- With small, fragile banks
(US) some banks don't have enough extra and fail
- Produces cascading failures - contagion, panic
# Prices

CPI (price level or inflation) is the "price" of money

- Prices have to adjust
# Output & Employment
- Recession
(depression) pushes prices
# Most Important Part Of "Supply":

![](Https://Cdn-Mineru. Openxlab. Org. Cn/Result/2025-11-15/C441F5B5-2F9D-4E4D-Bc65-1301C02E96Db/5Ea434Ab72Ec0B73Fc785B18Cae6430047C7B967C9E695Df122E4A4Cc70A42Fc. Jpg)

- HPM = cash held by public + "cash" held by banks
(reserves) # 2.21893-94,1907-08
# Contents
# Narrative
# 1893-94

Early 1893 saw a panic, touched o in May by a failure of a "stock-market favorite". (National Cordage Company - from Ahamed) Failure and suspension of banks in early 1893 was followed by restriction by banks on the convertibility of deposits into cash. Convertibility resumed September

1893. Two fundamental economic disruptions / shocks:

I. World gold quantity fell 1891-97 (relative to demand): M & MV = PY P

II. Agitation over silver (US politicians wanting silver in addition to gold)

Tended to pull gold away from US

Both tended to lower $M_{\mathbb{S}}$ relative to $M_{\mathbb{D}}$, pushing $P$ down

	- Could have gone o gold standard (allowing HPM ) but not politically possible

Also Barings Crisis in London 1890

# 1893-94 Cont'D
# Two Arguments:

I. Panic was mechanism forcing prices down: "The panic… was at bottom simply the way in which an adjustment [lower prices], forced by other considerations, worked itself out…." (F&S pp 110-111)

II. Without way to increase HPM, and with fragile banking system, banks failed and made recession much more severe

# Bank Runs & Restriction Of Payments

Panic and suspension of banks early 1893 - problems of getting cash

- Small, independent banks - requesting money (back to West and Southwest from NY)
Many of these banks were perfectly solvent and were ebarrassed on account of runs only for the length of time it took shipments of money from New York to reach them.
Caused problems in NYC, led to "Restriction of Payment"
- Banks banded together to stop conversion of deposits to currency
- "Restriction of cash payments brought to an end the stream of bank failures"
- Dual currency: cash was trading at premium - Di erent "price" for cash vs deposits - two types of money
I. Deposit-dollars
II. Currency-dollars, trading at up to $4\%$ premium (1907)
- I would pay you \$104 by check to get \$100 in currency
Many banks that suspended during 1893 had to do so because of problems with moving cash around, not because of underlying solvency:
Out of 360 state and national banks suspended during 1893, with liabilities of \$110 million, 343 suspensions with liabilities of\$ 96 million occurred west or south of Pennsylvania. In addition, 250 private and savings banks with liabilities of \$42 million suspended, with 224 of them with liabilities of \$36 million outside of New England and the Middle Atlantic States (Noyes, "The Banks and the Panic of 1893," pp. 15-16). Many of these banks were perfectly solvent and were embarrassed on account of runs only for the length of time it took shipments of money from New York to reach them. ([Friedman and Schwartz(1963)] fn 31 p 109)
# Discussion Of
- How restriction of payments brought bank runs to a halt
- How restriction of payments introduced dual currencies (cash & deposits) with exchange between them
- Mean of "restriction of payment" vs "suspension" (of bank)
Restriction of cash payments brought to an end the stream of bank failures. But it also in e etc created a dual monetary system - currency and deposits not interchangeable at a fixed rate. Currency sold at a premium for certified checks from the beginning of August to the beginning of September, when the currency premium ended, as enormous gold imports restored reserves. ([Friedman and Schwartz(1963)] p 110 - plus fn 32)
How panic was result of other more fundamental underlying forces - deflation required by fall in price of gold
The panic… was at bottom simply the way in which an adjustment, forced by other considerations, worked itself out…. ([Friedman and Schwartz(1963)] p 110-111)
- p 104: "world gold prices fell sharply [1891-97]" - is this a mis-speak? I think gold output fell and this pushed goods prices down (prices of goods denominated in gold). But I think goods prices down is gold price up. NP - 1889 to 1897 prices down $13.90\%$ or $1.85\%$ pa. p 119 - "by that time [1897] rising gold output
# 1907-08

October 1907 saw a banking panic, (Knickerbocker Trust Co.) again with restriction of convertibility of deposits into cash (lifted in early 1908).

# Time-Line:
- Bad recession May-Sept, but not disastrous (remember April 1907 SF earthquake)
- October: Knickerbocker, banking panic, and things got really bad
- Knickerbocker - related to speculation in copper
- Run-up seems an example of speculative mania
- After e etc liquidity crisis and fragmented unit banking
- NY Clearing House banks (and then others) restricted payments
- You could not convert your deposits into cash (could not cash a check)
- Stock market plunged, JP Morgan organized \$25mn support, banking seized up
# Key Issues From 1907-08

Banking fragility & bank runs:

- Weeks Oct 14 & Oct 21 - example of fragmented banking, customer worry, & chain reaction
Banking system was trying to coordinate
- Trying to increase and redistribute reserves
- Any small mistake could lead to problem - a run
In a unit banking system with some 20,000 independent banks, the impact was bound to be uneven, to force some banks into suspension, and to threaten a chain reaction involving a cumulative increase in the desire on the part of the public to convert deposits into currency. (F&S p 169)
# Banking Panic & Solution

Week of Oct 21-25 was bank run or panic in NY - subsided by end of week

US Treasury deposited \$25mn in NY banks

 - JP Morgan (the man) - \$25mn pool to support brokers
- NY Clearing House restricted payments
Some things to note:
- No Federal Reserve that could provide currency to banks
-Fedfounded1913, in response to 1907 panic
- Deposit Insurance also seen as solution
- Goal: forestall runs, provide pool of resources to solve mismatch
- But induces risk-taking - playing with "OPM"
- Pushed 8 states to pass deposit insurance
- All 8 failed in 1920s
- And failed banks in those states had worse losses
# 1907 Panic
- Severe economic recession
- Net National Product
(output) down by $11\%$ or more, 1907-1908
- For contrast, 2008-2009 recession, GDP down $2.8\%$, and that was worst since WWII
Time-line:
- Bad recession May-Sept, but not disastrous
- October: Knickerbocker, banking panic, and things got really bad
- NY Clearing House banks (and then others) suspended convertibility
You could not convert your deposits into cash (could not cash a check)
- Stock market plunged, JP Morgan organized \$25mn support, banking seized up
October 1907
Week of October 14
- 5 NYCH banks, and 3 others, required assistance
Related to stock market speculation in copper
- NYCH provided assistance, problem seemed to have been solved
- Seems to me more an example of speculative mania than C&H's thesis about fragmented unit banking
- Knickerbocker Trust and week of October 21st
- Now we get to what I see as the nub of C&H's unit banking argument
- Knickerbocker Trust Co lost reserves because of "connections with the banks originally in trouble" (Friedman & Schwartz 159)
Customers started to worry, run on Oct 22
Knickerbocker (as were all banks) too small to call on resources elsewhere. Not a member of NY CH. Bank suspended Oct 22
- Oct 23 - run on another trust co. And yet another Oct 24
- People worried that problems would carry over
# Response Of Ny Ch Banks
- Here is fragmentation problem - produces "fragility":
- Every bank needs to build up their own reserves, tries to hold extra
- There may be enough total currency
(reserves) but lumpiness means there may be some banks that have more than needed, a few with less
- Any bank with too little subject to a run
- Once runs start, it can carry over from one bank to another and necessary reserves increases - lumpiness problem becomes worse
- NY CH Banks had 3 drains on their currency:
I. Trust cos who had deposited reserves (trust cos paying out customers)
II. Interior banks (worried about panic in NY, trying to get back reserves)
III. Their own customers
Banking system was trying to coordinate
- Trying to increase and redistribute reserves
- Any small mistake could lead to problem - a run
- Fragile system
# Banking Panic And Solution
- Week of Oct 21-25 was bank run or banking panic in NY
 US Treasury deposited \$25mn in NY banks
- Some things to note:
- No Federal Reserve that could provide currency to banks (Fed founded 1913, in response to 1907 panic)
- Even with a Fed, might have been di cult to create "currency"
Gold standard: cannot really create new "money" - have to import gold from overseas or somehow induce people to give to Fed or Treasury (in return for what? bonds?)
- Deposit Insurance: purpose is to forestall run like this, to provide a pool of resources that backstop deposits and solve mismatch problem of
BUT – also induces risk-taking and moral hazard, palying with Other People's Money
(OPM) We will turn to this with Calomiris & Jaremski
Additional Steps
- JP Morgan (the man) organized \$25mn pool to support stock exchange brokers from failing
- Savings banks demanded legal notice for withdrawals
- By end of Oct 21-25 week, NY run seems to have subsided
- But still substantial problems in both NYC and rest of country
- NYC: 1) potential steel company bankruptcy (would bring down NYSE broker); 2) Possible NYC bankruptcy
- Rest of country: country banks withdrawing reserves from NY banks, losing confidence in NY banks
- NY CH did two things
I. Clearing House Loan Certificates (Monday May 28): used by members to settle balances, allowing them to use currency for customers
Essentially increase the "money" CH members had - helps solve liquidity
II. Restricted convertibility of deposits
# Restriction Of Payment
- Pretty extraordinary to think about
- You have money in the bank, you can pay bills, but you cannot get currency
(cash) - Suspension of Convertibility spread to other parts of the US
- Di erent "price" for cash vs deposits - two types of money
I. Deposit-dollars
II. Currency-dollars, trading at up to $4\%$ premium
- I would pay you \$104 by check to get \$100 in currency
# Other Problems
- Disruption of stock market, near-bankruptcy of brokerage houses (speculation in stocks)
- Standard speculative crisis
- Key issue: Fragility results from fragmented unit banking
- Lumpiness in distribution of reserves meant that banking system was vulnerable to runs
- Building systemic risk from the fragility of the fragmented banking system
# Outcome Of 1907 Panic & Crisis
- Founding of Federal Reserve in 1913
- To provide liquidity that fragmented unit banking system could not
  - But we will see that in 1930-33 Fed failed to supply liquidity – they "caused" the Great Depression
  - In contrast with 2008 when Fed supplied liquidity and almost certainly saved the world from something far worse
- Pushed 8 states to pass deposit insurance
- All 8 failed in 1920s
- And failed banks in those states had worse losses
# 2.3 Federal Reserve 1913
# Contents
# Federal Reserve 1913

Restriction of payments so serious, Federal Reserve as solution Mechanism to expand HPM when necessary

- With Fed, create "currency" by "Open Market Operations"
- Gold standard: seems hard to create new "money"
-Fed issues "Federal Reserve Notes": $40\%$ Gold, $60\%$ "Eligible Paper"
- Feb 27,1932 - Glass-Steagall removed restrictions
How does Federal Reserve increase HPM under Gold Standard?
- I think "open market operations": Fed would buy US gov't bonds, bills, "eligible paper" in return for Federal Reserve notes. See "Free Gold" section in F&S, p 399
- Fed Res notes issues required backing: $40\%$ gold, $60\%$ "eligible paper" given by bnaks (commercial, ag. industrial loans…) or gold
- P 404: "If free gold had been a serious handicap… Bills could have been purchased instead of government securities, since they were eligible as collateral for Federal Reserve notes."
- I think "Bills" = "banker's acceptances"? cf p 400
- Glass-Steagall Feb 27,1932 relaxed $40\%$ gold requirement
# 1920-21

Engineered by Fed: raising rates & pushing down HPM

- Rates rose from $4\%$ to $6\%$ between Octobe 1919 & January 1920 - big rise
# 2.41920S & 1930S: Wavering Unit Banking Support
# Contents
# 1920S & Early 1930S: Failures Led To Reform

Failures in 1920s (5,712) eroded support for unit banking and Deposit Insurance

- By 1930,8 states (West & South) permitted statewide branching
- Another 13: branching but limited (to protect rural banks)
- From 1921-1930, banks operating branches rose from 530 (1,281 branches) to 751 (3,522 branches)
During 1930s, "wave of 1920s failures became torrent"
1930-1933, more than 9,100 bank failed (38%)
- Depositors came to view unit banks more prone to failure
- Failures made Depression much worse - failure wipes out business & wealth
By end of 1935,13 of 27 states repealed "no branch banking"
# Fewer Banks, More Branches

In 1920s and early 30s, number of banks fell, branches rose

# 3 1930S, Great Depression, Glass-Steagall And Renewed Support For Unit Banking
# Contents
# 1929-33

The Great Depression. Maybe the most important financial crisis ever

- Crash of 1929 was a "trigger" - and an increase in liquidity demand (for currency & reserves)
- 1929-1930 was severe recession but no worse than 1893-94 or 1907-08
Bank failures turned it from "depression" to "The Great Depression" Three phases:
- First Banking Crisis: October 1930 F&S p 308
November 256 banks failed and in December 352
- Bank of United States
(private) December 1930
- Restriction of payments might have stopped failures - But not with Fed
Second Banking Crisis: March 1931
- October 1931,552 banks closed Bernanke. F&S p 317?
Third Banking Crisis: 1933
- Week-long "holiday" - 1/3 did not re-open Statistics on bank failures
# Why Was Policy So Bad?

Why not restrict payments

(convertibility) ?

- Friedman & Schwartz argue banks no longer had incentive or responsibility
Why didn't Fed expand HPM?
- Institutional problems
- Benjamin Strong died October 16,1928
Maybe worst single death for a country
- Move of power from NY to DC (Fed Banks vs Board of Governors)
- Lack of knowledge & expertise
# 1929-1933

The period 1929-33 is particularly important because the Great Depression is by far the most severe economic downturn in US economic history, at least for the past 150 years. Not only was it deeper and more prolonged than any other US downturn, it was also international in nature and counts as the most widespread and severe international downturn of modern times. Kindleberger dates the peak of the speculative cycle to September 1929 and NBER dates the peak of the business cycle to August

1929. The stock market crashed in October. Whatever the reason for the downturn (and it does seem have started decisively even prior to the October crash) declines in real income, consumer spending, and other real variables were substantial through 1930, with the pace accelerating after the October 1929 crash.

The October 1929 crash was essentially a widespread desire to switch from more to less risky assets. This was accomplished partly by a fall in price of the risky assets (the crash) and partly by, in essence, creating money to satisfy the demand for less risky assets: New York banks increased loans to brokers, thus increasing deposits and the stock of money.<sup>1</sup> The New York Fed supplied additional reserves (increased high-powered money) allowing New York commercial banks to increase their deposits (New York commercial banks were unwilling to increase their deposit-to-reserve ratio as it was already lower than in other parts of the country). These actions were e ective: "Despite the stock market crash, there were no panic increases in money market rates such as those in past market crises, and no indirect e ects on confidence in banks." ([Friedman and Schwartz(1963)] p. 339)

Although real variables were falling through 1930, the situation significantly deteriorated with the banking crisis in October 1930, and the subsequent crises of March 1931 and March

1930. With the Federal Reserve now in existence, in contrast to 1893-94 and 1907-08, banks did not restrict convertibility. Restrictions might have broken the cycle that led from the desire for liquidity, to conversion of deposits to currency, through to bank runs and failure and yet more desire for liquidity. Friedman and Schwartz express the opinion that restriction in 1930 would have prevented the subsequent waves of failures. Friedman and Schwartz argue that the Federal Reserve could have supplied currency and reserves to the banking system that would have helped alleviate the pressure on banks to shrink deposits, thus helping to forestall the waves of bank failures. During the period after October 1930, however, high-powered money increased by only $18\%$ (December 1930 to March 1933) and not sufficiently to o set banks' increased desire for reserves (as evidenced by the declining deposit-to-reserve ratio).

The net result is that during the 1929-1933 downturn the money stock fell by over $40\%$, due to substantial falls in the deposit-to-reserve and deposit-to-currency ratios and only partially o set by an increase in high-powered money.

In October 1930 the first banking crisis started, apparently as failures in some mid-west states "led to widespread attempts to convert deposits into currency." ([Friedman and Schwartz(1963)] p 308). In November 256 banks failed and in December

1931. The most important single failure was the Bank of United States (a private bank in spite of the name). With the Federal Reserve now in existence, banks did not restrict convertibility as they did in 1907 and
1932. Restrictions might have broken the cycle that led from the desire for liquidity, to conversion of deposits to currency, through to bank runs and failure and increased desire for liquidity. Friedman and Schwartz express the opinion that restriction would have prevented the subsequent waves of failures. High-powered money increased somewhat after October 1930, but not su ciently to o set banks' increased desire for reserves (as evidenced by the declining deposit-to-reserve ratio).

Glass-Steagall & Deposit Insurance: Reversed Reforms

Reforms of 1930s - particularly DI and FDIC (Federal Deposit Insurance) - reversed reform trend civics textbooks used by just about every American high school portrait deposit insurance as a necessary step to save the banking system, all of the evidence indicates otherwise: it was the product of lobbying by unit bankers who wanted to stifle the growth of branch banking (C&H p 190)

[Calomiris and Haber(2015), 190]

# Glass-Steagall & Deposit Insurance: Reversed Reforms Important Components:
- FDIC deposit insurance: subsidy to small and in-cient banks (vs large banks)
- Section 5144 making "groups" of unit banks under holding company more di cult
- Regulation Q - prohibition against paying interest on deposits
-Benefitted small (ine cients) banks relative to larger e cients banks
- Split commercial and investment banking
- Often touted as increasing safety of banking - removal in 1999 often cited as cause of 2008 crisis
- "No evidence linking securities underwriting or lending to securities dealers with the Great Depression"
Every banking crisis, people blame securities trading or CDOs or derivatives or…
- But it's always old-fashioned bank lending
Usually mortgage or real-estate lending
# 4 1980S - Collapse Of Unit Banking
# Contents

1980s - Collapse of Populist Agrarian / Unit Banking Coalition Five forces led to demise of the old coalition (4 long-term, 1 tipping)

I. Demographics: rise of urbanization

1900, $60\%$ rural. 1970, $30\%$

II. Technology: computers & ATMs

- Computers made information about customers easy to acquire at a distance
ATMs broke branching strangle-hold
III. Price inflation: Regulation Q meant deposits left banks
- Reg Q (from 1933) restricted checking to $0\%$, savings to $5\%$. OK with low inflation, problem with high inflation
IV. Large banks losing global market share
V. Tipping point: 1980s S&L crisis
# Various 1980S Problems Pushed Out Small Bankers 1980S Problems

1980s spike in inflation & interest rates - S&L lost deposits

- Regulation Q restricted $0\%$ on deposits, $5\%$ on deposits
- Non-banks figured out how to do non-bank "checking accounts" (money-market funds)
- Collapse in agricultural prices: rural banks closed
- Oil & gas price down: Texas & Oklahoma banks hit
Banking distress of 1980s even convinced many unit bankers it would be better to support branch banking
- At least get something for their bank, rather than closing
# Inflation And Us 10 Treasury Yields

![](Https://Cdn-Mineru. Openxlab. Org. Cn/Result/2025-11-15/C441F5B5-2F9D-4E4D-Bc65-1301C02E96Db/Ff38F39D1E8C4914Bf3333B575D169Dc454D0423Abecf10936D0A116E74Dc6E7. Jpg)

# 1980S Savings & Loan Crisis
# S&Ls: Traditional Business
- Borrow short-duration deposits, lend on long-term mortgages
- Depended on low inflation, discipline from threat of deposit withdrawal
- In 1980s, high inflation, and deposit insurance removed threat
1980 - deposit insurance raised to \$100k/account
- Intended to protect S&Ls.
Instead, changed incentives for S&L managers - incentive to take on more risk
- Merton: idea of equity as call on firm assets
Regulatory forbearance - allowing S&Ls to take risk
- Regulators looked the other way when S&Ls were having problems
Prudential regulatory failure, according to this theory, should not be conceived as a mistake or a consequence of the inability to devise better incentives… but rather as an equilibrium regulatory outcome, designed by those who created deposit insurance as a means of increasing risk (Calomiris & Jaremski:)
# Simple Merton Model Of Equity As Call

Equity is call on assets of company

- When near bankruptcy, incentive for owners to take big bets
![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-15/c441f5b5-2f9d-4e4d-bc65-1301c02e96db/a4aefcf0cfeca701a4dfe9435bcd6da03e25146c5c732039fb4c73707c87cee9.jpg)
# Consolidation & Branching
- Consolidation (within & across states) limited cost to FDIC
- From 1979 to 1990,15 states relaxed branching restrictions
- 1982 amendment to Bank Holding Act of 1956 overrode state laws, allowing acquisition by any holding company
- Early 1980s, banks operating national networks $10\%$ of system. By mid-1990s, $70\%$
- 1994, Riegle-Neal Interstate Banking and Branching E ciency Act allowed branch banking.
# End Of Unit Banking Era

# 1 Calomiris & Haber – Fragile By Design – Us Subprime Crisis

Property Rights; Political Economy; Law & Economics; Public Choice

I. This is a set of related terms & concepts, around the idea of applying Price Theory (incentives, budget constraints, preferences) to large social questions

II. Chicago has a long tradition in this, although interestingly with this specific area (Calomiris & Haber on Banking & Finance; Acemoglu & Robinson on di erential growth of nations) the interesting work has been done outside of Chicago. (Although now, with Robinson here, some of the important work is again being done here at Chicago)

(A) Aaron Director - came to Chicago Law School in 1946, trained "generations of law students and even his colleagues on the faculty in this then-new way of thinking about the law" (UofC press release on Director's death - http://www-news.uchicago.edu/releases/04/040913director.shtml)

Law and Economics as a field attempts to apply the scientific methods of economics—including statistics and price theory—to behaviors that in the past had been analyzed solely by appeal to the history and intuitions of the law. With coherent theory, precise hypotheses and a willingness to subject those hypotheses to empirical tests, it has transformed legal thinking in the United States and in many nations around the world. (UofC press release on Director's death - http://www.news.uchicago.edu/releases/04/040913director.shtml)

1. Aaron Director also has the distinction of helping to bring Milton Friedman to Chicago (see paper by David Mitch in October 2016 JPE - Director was not in the economics department but was an ally of Knight and some of his students – Simons, Mints), and of being Friedman's brother-in-law (Rose Director sat next to Friedman in economics and they eventually married)
(B) George Stigler - Regulatory Capture
2. "Stigler is best known for developing the Economic Theory of Regulation, also known as capture, which says that interest groups and other political participants will use the regulatory and coercive powers of government to shape laws and regulations in a way that is beneficial to them."
3. Ideas that are crucial for Calomiris & Haber's argument, but they actually do not cite Stigler very extensively
4. Another important name for government regulation: Sam Peltzman (business school - noted for his distinctive sartorial style)
(C) Ronald Coase - Externalities & the "Coase Theorem"
5. The idea of externalities – that costs (or benefits) from an activity may be born by people who do not choose the activity – is central to understanding many issues in public policy, pollution, and many others aspects of economics.
6. Coase had the central insight - an insight that had escaped earlier economists - that externalities should disappear in a world of perfect information and no transactions costs. In such a world agents can negotiate side-payments and return to equilibrium.
7. This is not to say that we actually live in such a world, but it forces us to think about why externalities matter – not because of the externalities per se but because of the transactions costs or informational asymmetries
8. Coase Theorem says that in the absence of transaction costs the assignment of property rights has no effect on the economically efficient outcome. It will obviously have equity effects.
9. It also forces us to think carefully about property rights, and is closely connected with the area of Law and Economics that has been important at Chicago since at least the 1940s.
10. "The Problem of Social Cost" published in the Journal of Law and Economics,
11. Apparently "the most-referenced article in both legal and economics scholarship." (http://www.law.uchicago.edu/alumni/magazine/fall11/lawandecon-history - also has the story of dinner at Director's house after the workshop where the group went from 20-1 against the Coase theorem to all in favor)
12. Example of cattle rancher & farmer (from paper, this quote from Wikipedia):
Coase argued that without transaction costs the initial assignment of property rights makes no di erence to whether or not the farmer and rancher can achieve the economically e cient outcome. If the cost of restraining cattle by, say, building a fence, is less than the cost of crop damage, the fence will be built. The initial assignment of property rights determines who builds the fence. If the farmer is responsible for the crop damage, the farmer will pay for the fence (as long the fence costs less than the crop damage). If the rancher is responsible for the crop damage, the rancher will build the fence. The allocation of property rights is primarily an equity issue, with consequences for the distribution of income and wealth, rather than an e ciency issue.
