```latex
\begin{document}
\begin{tabular}{|c|}
\hline
Economics of Regulation, Property Rights, and Coalitions
Calomiris &amp; Haber consider groups and coalitions interested in banking post-1994:
Ⅰ. Financial Industry: banking, mortgage lending
    • Goals: profit, which means managing regulation to their benefit
Ⅱ. GSEs: Fannie, Freddie - part of financial industry, but a special part
    • Goals: profit, which means managing regulation to their benefit
Ⅲ. Regulators: Fed, OCCC, FHFA (Federal Housing Finance Authority, Fannie &amp; Freddie regulator), FDIC, NCUA, OTS,...
    • Goals: job promotion; good job conditions; social welfare (good regulation)
Ⅳ. Politicians
    • Goals: donations from industry; getting elected by voters
Ⅴ. Other interest groups (Urban Activists)
    • Goals: jobs, political power &amp; influence, social welfare
Ⅵ. Public
    • Goals: Good and fair regulations; benefits (e.g. cheap &amp; easy loans)
    • May not understand or have enough time \\ \hline
\end{tabular}
\end{document}
```

I highlight the "benefits" to the public because I think this is largely overlooked and ignored, but I think it is maybe the central piece of the story. Reduction in mortgage lending standards benefited middle class borrowers the most, and politicians had strong incentives to support benefits for the public (through lower lending standards).

# Subprime Crisis Of 2007-2009

Various people have proposed various explanations

- Internal culture of banks - malfeasance of bankers and other finance professionals, and incompetence of regulators
- Low Federal Reserve policy rates post 2000 dot-com bubble
- Mortgage-backed Securities
(MBS) & derivatives
- Bush-era push for free-markets, deregulation, and repeal of Glass-Steagall
- Fannie & Freddie
Not underlying cause - Calomiris & Haber point out need two conditions
I. High Risk
II. Low Capital
Try to construct narrative for how banks took more risk, less capital
# Outline

I. This is based on Calomiris & Haber's Fragile by Design

II. Financial

(subprime) crisis of 2007-2009

(A) Why the US banking system, which broke out of the fragile and crisis-prone Unit Banking structure in the 1990s, nonetheless brought on the largest global financial crisis in a couple generations

III. Various people have proposed various explanations

(A) Internal culture of banks - malfeasance of bankers and other finance professionals, and incompetence of regulators

(B) Low Federal Reserve policy rates post 2000 dot-com bubble

(C) Mortgage-backed Securities

(MBS) & derivatives

(D) Bush-era push for free-markets, deregulation, and repeal of Glass-Steagall

(E) Fannie & Freddie

IV. These are not, in themselves, convincing. C&H (p 206-207) point out that "banking systems are susceptible to collapse only when two conditions are met in combination: banks must take on sufficient risk in their loans and other investments, and they must have inadequate capital on their balance sheets to absorb the losses associated with these risky loans and investments."

# Reminder: High Risk & Low Capital

Banking systems are susceptible to collapse only when banks both expose themselves to high risk in making loans and other investments and have inadequate capital on their balance sheets to absorb the losses associated with those risky loans and investments. (p 4)

Low risk & high capital

```latex
\begin{document}
\begin{tabular}{|c|c|c|c|}
\hline
ASSETS & LIABILITIES & & \\ \hline
Loans & $80 & $90 & Deposits \\ \hline
Cash & $20 & $10 & Equity \\ \hline
Total & $100 & $100 & Total \\ \hline
Loans fall by 7% & & & \\ \hline
Loans & $74.40 & $90 & Deposits \\ \hline
Cash & $20 & $4.40 & Equity \\ \hline
Total & $94.40 & $94.40 & Total \\ \hline
\end{tabular}
\end{document}
```

High risk & low capital

(Insolvency) ```latex

\begin{document}

\begin{tabular}{|c|c|c|c|}

\hline

ASSETS & LIABILITIES & & \\ \hline

Loans & $90 & $94 & Deposits \\ \hline

Cash & $10 & $6 & Equity \\ \hline

Total & $100 & $100 & Total \\ \hline

Loans fall by 7% & & & \\ \hline

Loans & $83.70 & $94 & Deposits \\ \hline

Cash & $10 & $0.00 & Equity \\ \hline

Total & $93.70 & $94 & Total \\ \hline

\end{tabular}

\end{document}

```
Left: lower credit risk (less loans) and more capital
- Either higher risk or lower capital alone - bank still solvent
Right: higher risk and lower capital - bank insolvent
• \$80 of loans & \$6 capital: Assets $94.40 ($0.40 extra)
 • §10 of capital & §90 of loans: Assets down to §93.70 (§3.70 extra)
C&H are trying to construct an account of how banks did these two: took on more risk, with insu cient capital
Let's look at what these two things look like by examining a very simple bank balance sheet
I. Assets of high-risk loans and low-risk cash. Take on more risk by more loans, less cash
II. Liabilities of Deposits
(borrowing) and Equity
(capital) III. Low risk, high capital - OK
• Assets: \$20 capital = > low risk, so lose only \$5.60
 • Liabilities: \$10 equity (10% capital ratio) => plenty of bu er to absorb \$5.60 loss
```latex
\begin{document}
\begin{tabular}{|c|c|c|c|}
\hline
ASSETS & LIABILITIES & & \\ \hline
Loans & $80 & $90 & Deposits \\ \hline
Cash & $20 & $10 & Equity \\ \hline
Total & $100 & $100 & Total \\ \hline
Loans fall by 7% & & & \\ \hline
Loans & $74.40 & $90 & Deposits \\ \hline
Cash & $20 & $4.40 & Equity \\ \hline
Total & $94.40 & $94.40 & Total \\ \hline
\end{tabular}
\end{document}
```

IV. Higher risk, high capital - Still OK

- Assets: Only \$10 capital => higher risk so loans fall by 7% -> lose \$6.30 (more than above), assets down to \$93.70
 - Liabilities: \$10 equity (10% capital ratio) => still enough but to absorb loss

```latex
\begin{document}
\begin{tabular}{|c|c|c|c|}
\hline
ASSETS & LIABILITIES & & \\ \hline
Loans & $90 & $90 & Deposits \\ \hline
Cash & $10 & $10 & Equity \\ \hline
Total & $100 & $100 & Total \\ \hline
Loans fall by 7% & & & \\ \hline
Loans & $83.70 & $90 & Deposits \\ \hline
Cash & $10 & $3.70 & Equity \\ \hline
Total & $93.70 & $93.70 & Total \\ \hline
\end{tabular}
\end{document}
```

(A) Bank loses rise because taking on more risk, more capital is eaten away, but there is still enough to protect depositors. No default or insolvency

V. Lower risk, low capital - Still OK

- Assets: \$20 capital => low risk, so lose only \$5.60
- Liabilities: \$6 equity, lower capital (6% capital ratio) => still enough but to absorb loss because losses are not large

```latex
\begin{document}
\begin{tabular}{|c|c|c|c|}
\hline
ASSETS & LIABILITIES & & \\ \hline
Loans & $80 & $94 & Deposits \\ \hline
Cash & $20 & $6 & Equity \\ \hline
Total & $100 & $100 & Total \\ \hline
Loans fall by 7% & & & \\ \hline
Loans & $74.40 & $94 & Deposits \\ \hline
Cash & $20 & $0.40 & Equity \\ \hline
Total & $94.40 & $94.40 & Total \\ \hline
\end{tabular}
\end{document}
```

(A) The bank losses are the same as in base case but the capital is less so there is less of a bu er. But the bu er is still enough to cover the losses.

VI. Higher risk, low capital - Default & INSOLVENCY

- Assets: \$10 capital => high risk, so lose \$6.30, assets down to \$93.70
- Liabilities: \$6 equity, lower capital (6% capital ratio) => not enough to absorb loss: loss is \$6.30 vs capital of \$6, so some of the loss comes out of deposits and the bank has to close or there is a bank run

```latex
\begin{document}
\begin{tabular}{|c|c|c|c|}
\hline
ASSETS & LIABILITIES & & \\ \hline
Loans & $90 & $94 & Deposits \\ \hline
Cash & $10 & $6 & Equity \\ \hline
Total & $100 & $100 & Total \\ \hline
Loans fall by 7% & & & \\ \hline
Loans & $83.70 & $93.70 & Deposits \\ \hline
Cash & $10 & $0.00 & Equity \\ \hline
Total & $93.70 & $93.70 & Total \\ \hline
\end{tabular}
\end{document}
```

(A) Now there is default & insolvency - failure of the bank. Losses are higher and there is less equity capital so all the capital is wiped out - the bank cannot pay back all depositor money.

# Outline Of The Story

Chapter 7: increased risk - erosion of mortgage lending standards by 2007,2 out of every 5 new mortgages granted had a down payment of 3 percent or less. Amazingly, a 2006 survey done by the National Association of Realtors found that 46 percent of first-time home buyers put down no money at all, and the median first-time buyer put down only 2 percent of the purchase price. (p 256)

Chapter 8: lower capital requirements

- Two forces that push banks to hold capital - both failed
I. Markets - failed because of subsidies & guarantees
II. Regulators - failed for various political reasons
Narrative: change of one coalition to another
Not fully convincing: Worldwide crisis, C&H explain US crisis
# Outline Of The Story

Broad outline:

- Policy goal: Mortgage lending & home ownership
Coalition: Large banks and urban activists
- Politicians: Allowed - and encouraged - mispricing of mortgage risk
- Result: Low price of risk led to overconsumption and crisis
- Everyone (bankers, shareholders, but mainly middle-class home buyers) had incentive to load up on risk
Crisis as simple economics (basic price theory): set price low, over consume
- btw - I think Calomiris & Haber do excellent job on the finance side, less well on "political science" side of explaining development and durability of political coalitions
# Reminder: Implicit Bargains

Banking valuable to society, and benefits flow to many groups

- Politicians, bankers, taxpayers, shareholders, taxpayers, voters
Think carefully about the economic incentives and goals of di erent actors
Move away from idea that public o cials (or others) are perfectly rational and always doing the right thing.
- Not necessarily corrupt, just fallible and responding to incentives our goal… is not to blame…. People pursue their self-interest, and one of the ways they do that is by exercising their political rights. When they are able to form a political coalition in the pursuit of mutual advantage, they will do so. (p 213)
 deals are intended to improve the welfare of the members of those coalitions and of the group in control of the government, not of society at large…. This situation implies that many parties may be excluded from beneficial deals. (p 38)
# Old Political Coalition

Agrarian Populists and Small-town Bankers

- An old American story - Strong echoes of today and Trump's populism
- Unit banking - each state controlled banks, many allowed only one branch
- Lasted in the US from roughly 1830 to 1980s - 150 years or more.
Important points for now:
- Started falling apart in the 1980s & early 1990s
- Consolidation - from many small state & local to a few large national banks
Key - the process of consolidation was not simple
# Old Political Coalition

I. Agrarian Populists and Small-town Bankers

II. Agrarian Populists (farmers, rural politicians) distrust bankers, east coast & big city-types in general.

(A) Strong echoes of today and Trump's populism

III. Small-town bankers - clearly in their interest to keep out large, ecient, low-cost bankers

(A) The same dynamic that one sees with Walmart vs local or small mom-and-pop stores

IV. We have discussed this - how this coalition started and why it was so durable. But it lasted in the US from roughly 1830 to 1980s - 150 years or more.

(A) Bank chartering was given to states under the constitution, and the populist / banker coalition was able to win on a state-by-state basis

(B) Costly, dysfunctional, a system prone to bank runs and systemic crisis

(C) It died when it became too dysfunctional - following the S&L crisis of the 1980s.

(D) C&H's comparison with Canada - similar in many ways to the US (agrarian, low population density, expanding westward) is very good

# New Political Coalition

Broad outline:

- Policy goal: Mortgage lending & home ownership
- Coalition: Large banks and urban activists
- Politicians: Allowed - and encouraged - mispricing of mortgage risk
- Result: Low price of risk led to overconsumption and crisis
- Everyone (bankers, shareholders, but mainly middle-class home buyers) had incentive to load up on risk
Crisis as simple economics (basic price theory): set price low, over consume
I. New Political Coalition
(A) Confluence of various factors led to political coalition that produced systemic mispricing of risk
(B) Collapse of agrarian-populist / small bank coalition (due to high costs) that allowed growth of large banks with reduced costs: rents to could go to i) Shareholders ii) Customers, iii) Others
(C) Rise of urban activists concerned with urban poverty
1. I di er with C&H who talk as if activists were concerned with redistribution – better to think of concern over urban poverty
(D) Politicians and policy-makers concerned with reducing urban poverty, who
2. Believed increased access to credit could address a root cause
3. Had ability to influence legislation & regulation to both push banks to lend to new groups and provide subsidies to pay them to do so (carrot & stick)
Broad Narrative: Rent-Sharing, Mispricing of Risk
Political bargain that emerged in the 1980s - large banks, GSEs, politicians, urban activists
	- Replaces former agrarian populist / small bank coalition
Banks and GSEs allowed to grow into enormous enterprises
Rents to share with i) Shareholders; ii) Customers; iii) Others
	- In the end, much directed towards urban activists
Final key element: politicians & policy-makers directing credit towards urban poor - through mispricing of risk
# "Income Redistribution" Vs "Combating Poverty"

Calomiris & Haber focus on "Income Redistribution" as prime goal of new coalition

Rising inequality 1980s-to-present

- American political culture and institutions make it very di cult to pass explicit income redistribution
I would say
(somewhat) the same from a di erent - maybe less judgmental - perspective
- "Combating Poverty"
- Opening opportunities to many - particularly urban poor - who did not have access to banking and credit
- Improvement in welfare and life outcomes
Many advocating these policies (bankers, activists, politicians) did not understand or recognize the negative consequences
- Much easier to look back and understand the stupidity of the policies
- At the time, many of the policies seemed smart and reasonable
- And most people were not economists who would understand the unintended consequences
Does not excuse the bad policies, but makes it easier to understand how and why
# Implied Tax & Transfer Rate

Simplest way to express: define Implied Tax & Transfer Rate:

Pretax - Aftertax

Pretax

- With taxes only, Pretax $>$ Aftertax, a regular tax rate, like 15%
- With transfers, we can have Aftertax $>$ Pretax and this rate can be negative
Figures: Spatio-temporal net redistribution rate
- Bottom quintile (20%): from -37% in 1966, to -150% in 2019
- For every \$100 of pre-tax income, receive \$150 more in transfers
Even middle quintile zero since 2008
![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-15/c441f5b5-2f9d-4e4d-bc65-1301c02e96db/05fa1b6277234439d3b0c28fe46e3bb4a80ad1ece25f51e04a95c8ad561dd205.jpg)
# 2 Mortgage Lending As Policy Goal
# Contents

Mortgage Lending & Home Ownership: Policy Goal

Long history in US as policy goal

- Federal land banks (1916)
- Originally to support agricultural lending and borrowing
# Mortgage Lending As Policy Goal

I. Mortgage lending has long history in US as policy goal

(A) Stretching back to Federal Housing Administration & Federal Home Loan Banks (Great Depression) and federal land banks (1916)

(B) Originally to support agricultural lending and borrowing

II. Federal National Mortgage Agency (FNMA, now known as Fannie Mae) chartered in 1938

(A) Owned by the gov't

(B) Role was to repurchase loans from banks, to provide liquidity in mortgage market

(C) Actually pretty important in highly fragmented and localized unit banking system - allowed unit banks to sell mortgages to wider market, access investors from across the country. Provide diversification not available to unit banks

III. Subsidized market in various ways

(A) Major: FNMA borrowed money at gov't rates - lower than commercial rates

# Fnma, Fannie Mae

Federal National Mortgage Agency (FNMA, now known as Fannie Mae) chartered in 1938

- Owned by the gov't
- Repurchase loans from banks, provide liquidity in mortgage market
- Important in highly fragmented and localized unit banking system - diversification
By 1960s, debt large, politicians wanted it "o balance sheet"
- "Privatized" 1968 - but not truly private
GSE: Government Sponsored Enterprise
- Also Freddie Mac
(FHLMC) , started 1971, privatized 1989
E orts - totally unsuccessful - to reform GSEs
- Subsidized in various ways
- GSEs borrow at gov't rates - lower than commercial rates
# Mortgage Lending - Fannie Mae

I. By 1960s, debt of FNMA large - gov't debt and it was crowding out other gov't debt

II. Solution? Move o balance sheet (a well-used trick). 1968

(A) "Privatized" in

1968. Split in two: GNMA (Ginnie Mae) gov't-owned, FNMA (Fannie Mae) "private"

III. GSE: Gov't Sponsored Enterprise

IV. Private shareholders, but not truly private. Many gov't benefits:

(A) Exempted from State & Local Taxes

(B) Exempt from SEC regulation

(C) Lax banking regulation

(D) Credit line from US Treasury

(E) Implicit guarantee of debt

1969. Gov't said it was not gov't debt. Nobody believed it. And indeed, in 2008, it became gov't debt

V. E orts to improve GSEs

VI. Freddie Mac - founded as "competition" for Fannie Mae

(A) 1971, FHLMC (Freddie Mac, Federal Home Loan Mortgage Corporation) started as government-owned

(B) 1989, Freddie Mac "privatized" but with same gov't privileges & subsidies

VII. Continuing e orts to reform GSEs

(A) There was widespread recognition and concern about GSE privileges and the distortions they introduced to the market

(B) When I was in mortgage market, easy to see the implicit subsidy

(C) Combination of forces (GSE managers, GSE shareholders, urban activists who benefited from GSE credit, politicians who benefited from support for their constituents - and campaign contributions) managed to defeat substantive reform - for decades, and still today

(D) 1992 GSE Act - instead of reforming, introduced problems

VIII. GSE Subsidies - Some Thoughts

(A) When I was in the mortgage market (1990s) everyone recognized the subsidy of and advantage for GSEs (Fannie & Freddie)

1970. Hard to miss

(B) Their debt was trading close to Treasuries rather than corporate bond or bank spreads

1971. The market was assessing their risk as gov't risk - implicit guarantee
1972. Result - they could borrow more cheaply than banks, could make more profit than private banks

(C) Inherently unfair, but not necessarily dangerous

1973. I recognized that it was unfair. I did not recognize the inherent danger
1974. Subsidy was unfair - enriched private shareholders at expense of taxpayers
1975. Inherent danger came from push by politicians to support credit expansion, lowering of credit standards

# 3 Coalition Between Large Banks & Urban Activists
# Contents
# Coalition Between Large Banks & Urban Activists

Collapse of populist / unit bank coalition, banks expand nationally

Cost savings. Benefits

(rents) from

Economies of scale (lower costs than unit banks)

Economies of scope (multiple products)

- Market
(monopoly) power
- Implicit (too-big-to-fail) and explicit (deposit insurance) protection that provides subsidies and distorts costs
- Large banks doubly-subsidized - both deposit insurance and bailout
Benefits to be shared with: i) Shareholders; ii) Customers; iii) Others
- Banks ended up having to share rents
(partially) with urban activists
Coalition Between Commercial Bankers & Urban Activists
I. With collapse of old populist / unit bank coalition, banks could expand and open branch networks
(A) Banks could reap cost savings from mergers and branching. Created rents to be share with: i) Shareholders; ii) Customers; iii) Others
II. Rents from
(A) Economies of scale (lower costs that unit banks)
(B) Economies of scope (multiple products)
(C) Market
(monopoly) power
(D) Implicit (too-big-to-fail) and explicit (deposit insurance) protection that provides subsidies and distorts costs
1. Large banks doubly-subsidized - both deposit insurance and bailout
III. Banks ended up having to share rents
(partially) with urban activists
# How Did "Urban Activists" Gain Leverage?

How did "urban activits" groups have leverage over banks?

Mergers required approval by Federal Reserve

- Banks must be "good citizens"
- Activist groups could bless or stall merger
CRA - Community Reinvestment Act
- Passed in
1977. Starting 1990s: CRA revisions, mergers.
	- Groups could use poor CRA rating to block.
One way to get CRA ratings - partner with urban activist groups
C&H document (pp 216-226) cooperation between large banks and urban activist groups
# Cra - Community Reinvestment Act

I. Passed in 1977, partly in response to claims banks discriminated against borrowers in low-income, inner-city areas

II. Before about mid-1990s, CRA was not very important

III. Starting 1990s, with revisions of CRA and increasing mergers, it started to become important

IV. For merger approval, banks had to prove they were "good citizens" - in practice meant they had to get good CRA ratings.

V. One way - partner with urban activist groups and direct lending towards low-income and inner-city areas

VI. C&H document (pp 216-226) cooperation between large banks and urban activist groups

# "Redistribution" & "Urban Activists"
# Calomiris & Haber Argue:
- Rising inequality in 1970s (really 1980s) - political pressure
- US political institutions make it di cult to explicitly redistribute.
- Serious and on-going concerns about urban poverty
- Solution: redistribute through banking and GSEs
- "let them eat credit" (from Rajan's Fault Lines)
Nice overview of political structures and di erences from, e.g., Europe
US political institutions require "supermajority" for big changes
- Senate engineered to give power to small states
(rural) - First-past-post discourages fringe parties
# Redistribution

I. Calomiris & Haber talk about "redistribution" & "urban activists". They argue that:

(A) Rising inequality starting in 1970s (really more the 1980s) meant political pressure to redistribute income

(B) US political institutions make it di cult to explicitly redistribute. Nice overview of political structures and di erences from, e.g., Europe - pp 213-214

US political institutions require "supermajority" for big changes

- Senate engineered to give power to small states
(rural) - First-past-post discourages fringe parties
(C) Serious and on-going concerns about urban poverty
(D) Solution: redistribute through banking and GSEs - "let them eat credit" (from Rajan's Fault Lines).
# Digression On Inequality

Starting in 1980s, income inequality in US started to rise

- Late 20th c rise mirrored early 20th c fall
People focus on top $10\%$ or $1\%$
- More interesting: Wage & education gap: College 1.9x high-school - in 1980 1.3x
- Wage gap reflects simple supply & demand for skills
- Skill demand: Technological change - robust early & late 20th c
- Skill supply: Grew fast early, slowed late 20th c
![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-15/c441f5b5-2f9d-4e4d-bc65-1301c02e96db/6c205e89de3e7bdd83aa7434ddcb5aba6a100341c356b52473f7c85e1109326b.jpg)

# Digression On Income Inequality

I. Starting in 1970s, income inequality in US started to rise back up. Interesting that this rise in the latter part of the 20th c mirrored the fall in the early part of the century.

(A) People often focus on top $10\%$ or $1\%$ - see picture from Saez (work by Piketty & Saez)

(B) Turns out there was a fall in the early 20th c, and the rise starting in the 1980s is a rise back to levels we saw in the early 20th c

(C) But more informative and more interesting is the wage gap - the split between earnings for high-school graduates and college graduates.

(D) Good evidence that both the early-20thc-fall and the late-20thc-rise have been due to the gap between highly-educated versus less-highly-educated workers: the inequality in earnings due to di erent worker skill level. (Other things clearly going on - urban-rural gaps, black-white gaps, capital-labor earnings - but the main issue appears to be wages & education. cf [?,?]

(E) This wage gap reflects simple supply & demand for skills

(F) Technological change – in both the early part and the late part of the century – increased demand for skilled workers.

(G) Early part of 20th c: education and human capital investment grew so much that it pushed down the wage gap. Later part of 20th c: we as a nation simply stopped making large investments in education and human capital.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-15/c441f5b5-2f9d-4e4d-bc65-1301c02e96db/b8d593062f8212d02f8996cc10e9c00d3f2914e54b910cec711e841e42a05101.jpg)

"Striking it Richer" Emmanuel Saez, January 23,2013. FIGURE 1, The Top Decile Income Share, 1917-2011 (from earlier joint work with Piketty)

# Alternate Take On "Redistribution" & "Urban Activists"

Calomiris & Haber make this move toward more banking for urban poor sound slightly malign I have slightly di erent take

- "Better Banking" or "Addressing urban poverty" instead of "redistribution"
- Older unit-banking system harmed urban poor – inadequate credit
C&H Fig 1.1 shows US low credit (R&R vs C&H)
- Good: policy goal to provide better access to credit
Bad: lower credit standards
- inform public policy with good economics
- Simple price theory: artificially low price $= >$ over-consumption
![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-15/c441f5b5-2f9d-4e4d-bc65-1301c02e96db/5003d4840523bcb9a44a3c24c3037856379b2a86951d6ee2ac1b6446efa455ac.jpg)
Table 1.1 Average private credit from deposit money banks as a percentage of GDP, 1990-2010, by World Bank Income classification
Source:WorldBank, 2010
Notes: For reasons of readability, only selected country figures are shown on the table.
Alternate take: Calomiris & Haber talk about "redistribution" and "urban activists" - make it sound like something malign. I have a slightly di erent take on the issue
I. I think of "addressing urban poverty" rather than "redistribution"
(A) It is certainly true that low-income and inner-city groups were poorly-served by unit banking system
(B) System was structured to serve goals of rural populations (even if serving those expensively)
(C) Under unit banking, banking was expensive and opening in a low-income inner-city area was often not a profitable business decision
(D) As one example, in Chicago in the 1980s (and even now) there were many check-cashing services - where someone without a bank could cash a paycheck or government check. Chicago was poorly-banked, and inner city areas particularly so
II. Providing banking services to low-income and inner city populations is itself a good thing, and something not done well under unit banking structure
(A) To my mind, perfectly reasonable that groups in 1980s & 1990s (C&H's "urban activists") would push for better banking services
III. My sense is that, at least some of the "urban activists" and the politicians in the 1980s-2000s, saw better banking as addressing a root cause or urban poverty and inequality
(A) Not a foolish idea that poor access to credit could be a root cause for urban poverty and rising inequality
(B) C&H argue that a part - major part - of motive for political bargain was desire to ameliorate income inequality, largely among urban populations
(C) Problem was that policies adopted – reducing credit standards – produced terrible outcomes
IV. Distinction between equality of opportunity and equality of outcome
V. Whether motive is "redistribution" or "urban poverty", end result for the policies adopted was the same
(A) Misguided policies that
(B) Distorted incentives so that individuals - following incentives - built up large and unsustainable risks and
(C) Led to outcomes where everyone, low-income in particular, worse o
VI. Example of necessity of informing public policy with good economic theory and evidence - here arguing that reducing underwriting standards (mispricing risk) will increase systemic risk for banks without improving outcomes for urban poor.
(A) Simple price theory: artificially low price $= >$ over-consumption.
(B) With risk, over-consumption has serious consequences
# Urban Activists - Conclusion

My sense: coalition between banks & urban activists alone not enough to cause systemic breakdown

Calomiris & Haber spend much time laying out the coalition - and the rent-sharing (transfer to urban activist groups & constituents)

- pp 216-226
In the end I think this is valuable, but not the key ingredient
Expansion of coalition to bring in additional powerful groups is key

"Unit Banking" Crises Easy to Understand:

I have more trouble with 2008 Sub-Prime

1992 GSE Act - Started Reducing Credit Standards
Federal Housing Enterprise Safety and Soundness Act
Didn't remove subsidies – direct credit towards home ownership
Consistent with long-term government goals
Act Required HUD to develop a ordable housing goals
The sponsors of the GSE Act understood that the purpose of the mandates was to force Fannie and Freddie to lower their underwriting requirements and thereby lead private banks into originating loans to low-income urban constituents
The market influence of Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac extends well beyond the number of loans they buy or securitize; their underwriting standards for primary loans are widely adopted and amount to national underwriting standards for a substantial fraction of all mortgage loans [1991 HUD report]
Congress therefore mandated that Fannie and Freddie consider repurchasing mortgages with down payments of 5 percent or less and approving borrowers with histories of delinquent credit. [C&H p 235]

# Starting In 1990S, Credit Standards Down
# Fannie & Freddie Were The Mechanism

1992 - Fannie & Freddie purchased 1 out of every 4 new mortgages

- Fannie & Freddie set the standard for mortgages in private market
- Congress (1992?) directed Fannie & Freddie to purchase $5\%$ -down mortgages, approve borrowers with bad histories
- $5\%$ -down mortgages did not then exist in conventional market
1994, Fannie & Freddie started buying $3\%$ down

# Digression - Some Mortgage Concepts & Terms Loan-To-Value

(Ltv) And % Down

- Borrowers generally have to put own money in - say $20\%$
This is $80\%$ LTV - higher LTV is more risky
- Provides cushion (for lender) if house price falls - house buyer absorbs first $20\%$ of loss
- During 1990s and 2000s, LTV went up from $80\%$ to (close to) $100\%$.
# Subprime & Alt-A - Various (Slightly Di Erent) Meanings

FICO (credit score) below 660

- Alt-A: generally characteristics of mortgage contract, such as high LTV, no documentation
- Key factor: default risk much higher than conventional mortgages
# Conventional Mortgage (Or "Conforming") - Lower Risk
- Meets requirements for guarantee or purchase by Fannie and Fredie
- Originally (pre-2000) a relatively safe (low LTV, well-documented) mortgage
# No-Doc Mortgage - Higher Risk
- Mortgage without income verification (e.g. pay stub, W-2)
- Extreme: NINJA: No Income, No Job, No Assets
# Starting In 1990S, Credit Standards Down High Risk Loans Exploded By 2006

# Lower Standards - Open To Everyone

Most important point - lower standards applied to everyone (non excludable good)

- Everyone, middle-class and wealthy, took on extra risk
- Subsidy larger for middle-class and wealthy
Homeowners subsidized through tax system and GSEs (lower mortgage rates)
- Deduct mortgage interest from taxes, pay lower rates (due to GSE subsidies)
Lower down-payment with large loan would increase the subsidy
- Say rate subsidy takes rate from $8\%$ to $5\%$
(exaggerated) • \$200 home, 80% LTV: \$40k down, \$160k mortgage, \$4.8k subsidy (160*0.03)
- Now lower to $5 \%$ down, $$ 40\mathrm{k}$ down can buy $$ 800\$ home, $$ 760$ mortgage (95% LTV), $$ 22.8\mathrm{k}$ subsidy
Subsidy to risk means borrowers have strong incentive to borrow more
- Bad for society (too much debt) and for individual (too much debt)
# Lower Standards - Open To Everyone

Borrowers doing nothing "wrong" - behaving according to incentives

- Importance of not interfering with incentives
(prices) # Important Points
- Opening credit to under-served groups not bad
Method of opening credit (reducing standards) was problem
- C&H example: Australia, first-time buyers receive subsidy toward down payment.
- Reduces LTV (makes loans less risky) in contrast with US in 2000s (increasing LTV, making loans risky)
The lunch might not have looked entirely free, but because taxpayers were picking up part of the tab, borrowers had an incentive to supersize their meals. The size of the interest-rate subsidy received by the borrower was a function of the size of the loan: the more a household was allowed to borrow at a favorable interest rate, the larger the subsidy it received. Thus borrowers seeking to expand the size of the implicit subsidy they received from the government faced strong incentives to maximize loanto-value ratios and loan-to-income ratios. [Calomiris and Haber(2015), 229]
 fn 50 For example, if a prospective mortgage borrower without government policy interventions would have had to pay, say, an 8 percent interest rate on a mortgage with a 97 percent loan-to-value ratio, but could borrow that amount at a 5 percent interest rate in the presence of government subsidies, then the implied subsidy would be nearly 3 percent of the value of the borrower's home per year. [Calomiris and Haber(2015), 229]
 fn 51 There are other ways to encourage home ownership that are less distortionary. For example, in Australia, first-time home buyers receive a government subsidy toward their down payments. The Australian housing subsidy program therefore helps first-time buyers to qualify for loans by reducing their loan-to-value ratios
(LTVs) —the opposite of the U. S. system. [Calomiris and Haber(2015), 229]
# Result: By 2006, Us Mortgages Hugely Risky
- By 2007,2 out of 5 new mortgages had down payment $3\%$ or less (LTV $97\%+$ )
- A 2006 survey found $46\%$ of first-time buyers put down no money at all, median buyer only $2\%$ (98% LTV)
As we discuss in the previous chapter, by 2007,2 out of every 5 new mortgages granted had a down payment of 3 percent or less. Amazingly, a 2006 survey done by the National Association of Realtors found that 46 percent of first-time home buyers put down no money at all, and the median firsttime buyer put down only 2 percent of the purchase price. [Calomiris and Haber(2015), 256]
# 4.3 Reducing Cost To Creditors, Increasing Return To Shareholders Contents
# Incentivizing Bank Shareholders – Lax Capital Standards Reducing Credit Standards Was One Part Of The Story
- Mandated (ordered by politicians)
- Embraced by public (you and me)
But why would banks take on higher-risk mortgages?
- Shareholders not foolish, recognized that mortgages more risky
Must pay shareholders more
- Will take on more risk only in return for higher return
One way to increase return is to allow banks to use more borrowed money (more leverage – lower capital)
- If loses pile up, just walk away
(default) and get nothing
- But if there are profits, get same dollar profit with less investment, higher return
- Incentive for banks to take risk: reduce the cost of taking on risk
- More borrowed money means lower capital ratio
Very crude example of shareholders borrowing:
- Put in \$100, get out either \$80 or \$120
- Case 1: borrow \$80, shareholders put in \$20.
Creditors: always get back \$80
- Shareholders: get either \$0 (lose 100%) or \$40 (win + 100%)
- Case 2: borrow \$90, shareholders put in \$10
- Creditors: get back \$80 or \$90
- Shareholders: get either \$0 (lose 100%) or \$30 (win +200%)

Left: more capital,
- Return on equity is $-80\%$ or $+80\%$
Right: lower capital
- Loans fall, bank insolvent, debtors absorb some of losses
- Loans rise, return on equity is $133\%$
# Incentivizing Bank Creditors - Subsidies & Guarantees But Why Would Creditors Ever Lend More?
- If creditors more likely to lose, will lend less (or charge higher rate)
Subsidies & guarantees - government
(taxpayers) pay to protect creditors
Bank deposit insurance
- Bank "too-big-to-fail" implicit guarantee
- Fannie & Freddie implicit debt guarantee
Value of subsidies varied by two criteria
- Larger banks got larger subsidies (large banks "too big to fail", smaller banks allowed)
- More debt, larger subsidy
- More debt higher chance of default more likely to use subsidy
In post-1990 America, banks and GSEs enjoyed the protection of government safety nets that guaranteed or otherwise reduced the de-fault risk of their debts. Those safety nets took the form of deposit insurance, access to Fed and FHLB lending, implicit guarantees of GSE debts, and implicit too-big-to-fail guarantees of bank debts that were not covered by deposit insurance. These safety nets should be thought of as subsidies: they allowed banks and GSEs to hold less capital and pay lower interest rates on their debts than they would have had to otherwise, because they reduced the risk of default on their debts and weakened the incentives of their creditors to monitor them and demand increases in equity capital.
The value of the subsidies provided by these government safety nets varied according to two criteria, producing some rather perverse incentives. First, the larger the bank or GSE, the larger the value of the safety net, and hence the larger the value of the subsidy. A small bank, one that was not too big to fail, received protection only from the deposit-insurance system. Indeed, in the case of insolvency, it could be taken over by the FDIC, which would guarantee its deposits and then arrange the sale of the bank to a competitor, thereby wiping out the shareholders and perhaps the bank's uninsured creditors.
Second, the more debt that a bank or GSE took on relative to its equity capital, the larger the value of the safety net, and hence the larger the subsidy. A metaphor from the circus perhaps makes the intuition clear: a safety net is not of much value to a high-wire performer if the wire is three feet above the ground. The higher the wire, the higher the value of the net. For a bank or GSE, the higher the leverage, the higher the default risk being borne by the government from protecting the debts. As the value of the safety-net subsidy rose with leverage, so did the share price of the bank or GSE that enjoyed that subsidy: the share price reflected the ability of the bank or GSE to borrow at an artificially low rate compared to the amount it would have had to pay on its debts without the government's backing0.2 Shareholders of banks and GSEs therefore were rewarded by the government safety net for having as little skin in the game as possible. In such a situation, shareholders may choose to maximize their leverage, pay a low rate of interest to their creditors, and then pocket the profits. This is a risky strategy because it is possible that shareholders will end up with nothing (if the value of the bank's assets declines sufficiently), but on average, higher leverage increases the rate of return to shareholders.
# Simple Merton Model Of Equity As Call
# Equity Is Call On Assets Of Company
- With low capital ratio, incentive for owners to take big bets
- Keep profits if win, but don't lose (creditors left with losses)
Creditors
(partially) protected by gov't subsidies & guarantees
![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-15/c441f5b5-2f9d-4e4d-bc65-1301c02e96db/e4bab80695414bfb0a186cf0c2476588118cc9f666fe21e5fa9895f66944535a.jpg)

# 5 Result - 2007-2009 Financial Crisis
# Contents
# Result - 2007-2009 Financial Crisis
2007 - Mortgages hit, not yet crisis (Kindleberger "Financial Distress")
- Housing crisis deepens. Banks and hedge funds left with worthless subprime assets as foreclosures rise.
- April 2: New Century Financial (mortgage lender) bankruptcy
- July 31: Bear Stearns liquidates hedge funds
(subprime) - Aug. 6: American Home Mortgage Investment,
(ARMs) bankruptcy
- Aug. 16: Fitch Ratings downgrades Countrywide Financial
- Sep. 13&14: Run on Northern Rock
(UK) . Sep & Oct: German & Swiss banks announce losses on
(US) mortgages
Dec. 19: S&P downgrades monolines (insurers of mortgage bonds)
# 2008 - Financial Distress Continues, Until September Crisis & Panic
U. S. economy in recession. Subprime mortgages infect the credit markets.
Jan. 11: Bank of America, buys (bails out) Countrywide Financial
- March 16: Fed guarantees Bear Stearns' assets - gov't-sponsored sale to JPMorgan Chase.
- July 11: Federal regulators seize IndyMac Federal Bank
# September 2008 - Crisis
# September - Crisis Hits
7: Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac taken over by the government.
 • 15: Bank of America agrees to purchase Merrill Lynch for \$50 billion.
- 15: Lehman Brothers files for bankruptcy-court protection.
• 16: American International Group – \$85 billion bailout, gov't gets a 79.9% stake
- 21: Goldman Sachs and Morgan Stanley, the last two independent investment banks, will become bank holding companies subject to greater regulation by the Federal Reserve.
25: Feds close Washington Mutual, sold to JPMorgan - biggest U. S. bank failure
- 29: Congress rejects \$700 billion Wall Street rescue package (Troubled Asset Relief Program, TARP) - Dow Jones down 778 points
# October, November, December - Aftermath
- Oct. 3: Congress passes TARP, Bush signs. Wells Fargo & Co. buys Wachovia
- Nov. 18: Ford, General Motors, Chrysler testify in Congress, request TARP loans
- Nov. 23: The Treasury Department, Federal Reserve and Federal Deposit Insurance Corp. agree to rescue Citigroup with a package of guarantees, funding access and capital.
- Dec. 19: The U. S. Treasury authorizes TARP loans for General Motors & Chrysler
# 2007
- Housing crisis deepens. Banks and hedge funds that invested big in subprime mortgages are left with worthless assets as foreclosures rise. The damage reaches the top echelons of Wall Street:
- Feb. 27: Mortgage giant Freddie Mac says it will no longer buy the most risky subprime loans.
- April 2: Subprime mortgage lender New Century Financial files for bankruptcy-court protection.
- July 31: Investment bank Bear Stearns liquidates two hedge funds that invested in risky securities backed by subprime mortgage loans.
- Aug. 6: American Home Mortgage Investment, which specializes in adjustable-rate mortgages, files for bankruptcy protection.
- Aug. 16: Fitch Ratings cuts the credit rating of giant mortgage lender Countrywide Financial to its third-lowest investment-grade rating.
# 2008
- The U. S. economy is in recession. The crisis in subprime mortgages infects the credit markets.
- Jan. 11: Bank of America, the biggest U. S. bank by market value, agrees to buy Countrywide Financial for about \$4 billion.
- March 16: The Federal Reserve agrees to guarantee \$30 billion of Bear Stearns' assets in connection with the government-sponsored sale of the investment bank to JPMorgan Chase.
- July 11: Federal regulators seize IndyMac Federal Bank after it becomes the largest regulated thrift to fail.
- September:
- 7: Mortgage giants Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac are taken over by the government.
- 15: Bank of America agrees to purchase Merrill Lynch for \$50 billion.
- 15: Lehman Brothers files for bankruptcy-court protection.
- 16: American International Group, the world's largest insurer, accepts an \$85 billion federal bailout that gives the government a 79.9% stake in the company.
- 21: Goldman Sachs and Morgan Stanley, the last two independent investment banks, will become bank holding companies subject to greater regulation by the Federal Reserve.
- 25: Federal regulators close Washington Mutual Bank and its branches and assets are sold to JPMorgan Chase in the biggest U. S. bank failure in history.
- 29: Congress rejects a \$700 billion Wall Street financial rescue package, known as the Troubled Asset Relief Program or TARP, sending the Dow Jones industrial average down 778 points, its single-worst point drop ever.
- Oct. 3: Congress passes a revised version of TARP and President Bush signs it. Wells Fargo & Co., the biggest U. S. bank on the West Coast, agrees to buy Wachovia for about \$14.8 billion.
- Nov. 18: Ford, General Motors and Chrysler executives testify before Congress, requesting federal loans from TARP.
- Nov. 23: The Treasury Department, Federal Reserve and Federal Deposit Insurance Corp. agree to rescue Citigroup with a package of guarantees, funding access and capital. Citigroup will issue preferred shares to the Treasury and FDIC in exchange for protection against losses on a \$306 billion pool of commercial and residential securities it holds.
- Dec. 19: The U. S. Treasury authorizes loans of up to \$13.4 billion for General Motors and\$ 4.0 billion for Chrysler from TARP.
# Calomiris & Haber Best I Have Seent
Calomiris & Haber's explanation best I've seen so far Based on a consistent view of the world, that looks at
- Long history
- Consistent framework for US 1830-1980 (unit banking) and 2000s (push to help underserved groups led to mispricing of risk)
- Across di erent regimes
- Britain - democracy
- Canada - democracy - with stable & ecient banking (relative to US)
- Brazil - decentralized autocracy for much of history
- Mexico - centralized autocracy for much of history
Comparison with Canada particularly important (chapter 9)
- Canada no systemic banking crises since
1840. US 12
    - Political institutions - that forestall populist coalitions - produce such an outcome
# Side Points
C&H least good in their "political coalitions" argument (economists not political scientists) They focus on "redistribution" whereas I would focus on "poverty reduction"
Their story cannot be complete - too US-centric:
- The 2007-2009 crisis was worldwide, with real estate problems in Britain, Ireland, Spain, Portugal... Still, it is the best I've heard so far.
# Not All Banks Took On Risk
A friend at JP Morgan in 2000s
- They wanted to buy 15&30yr conventional mortgages (relative safe) but had trouble finding
Goldman Sachs moved away from mortgages around 2006
How then do we account for that story that made the rounds in the summer of 2007? It concerns Goldman Sachs, the one Wall Street firm that was not, at that time, taking a hit for billions of dollars of suddenly devalued mortgage-backed securities. Reporters wanted to understand how Goldman had somehow sidestepped the disaster that had befallen everyone else. What they discovered was that in December 2006, Goldman's various indicators, including VaR and other risk models, began suggesting that something was wrong. Not hugely wrong, mind you, but wrong enough to warrant a closer look. (Joe Nocera, "Risk Management", NY Times Magazine, http://www.nytimes.com/2009/01/04/magazine/04risk-t.html?_r=1&ref=business)
# Not All Banks Took On Risk
Easy to see in 2005 & 2006 that things were o
- spreads for AAA mortgage CDOs were $\mathrm{L} + {80}$ while AAA corporates $\mathrm{L} + {20}$.
People in the market knew the mortgages were risky. cf C&H $p_{266}$-269
C&H $p_{268}$ discuss Moody's data published 2005, showing Baa-rated mortgages default rates 10-times higher than Baa corporates
- I've mentioned a couple times my finding in 2005 that market spreads on AAA mortgage CDOs much wider than AAA corporates – everyone knew mortgages more risky
Any financial professional protesting that they purchased AAA-rated mortgage securities because they were convinced they were safe instruments either was disingenuous or not paying attention, arguably because they were blinded by the high pay they received in exchange for not questioning the ratings. (C&H p 268, quoting Barth, Caprio, Levine 2012)
# "Political" Problems
Don't down-play di culty of regulators imposing standards before any of us becomes too self-righteous about regulatory failure, ask yourself this: if you had been a prospective homeowner, how would you have reacted to the prospect of mortgage rates rising in 2006 or 2007 as a consequence of aggressive actions by regulators? Regulators are aware of the political environment in which they operate. (p 273)

# 1 Deposit Insurance
# 1.1 Review & Overview
# Contents
# Remember Bank Runs, Diamond & Dybvig, Deposit Insurance
# Standard Story
- Banks inherently unstable
- Deposit
(liability) insurance "solves" problem of instability
# Diamond & Dybvig (1983) Classic Story Of Bank Runs
- Banks transform long-run illiquid assets into short-run liquid assets
- Inherently unstable and subject to runs
# Standard View - Deposit Insurance
(Di) Is:
- "part of a financial system safety net that promotes financial stability."
# "Heretical View": Di Serves Private Interests Of Banks, Borrowers, Depositors
- Introduced in US in 1933 to support & subsidize small & fragile banks
# Diamond & Dybvig Vs Calomiris & Jaremski
Diamond & Dybvig are the standard ("good") story about Deposit Insurance Calomiris & Jaremski present an alternative - dissenting - story
- Somewhat heretical - deposit insurance adopted pretty-much world-wide
- We have all been taught DI solves "bank run" problem
- Alternative - a "political story" to support weak and fragile banks
- I have become increasingly disenchanted with "Standard Story"
I'm afraid Calomiris & Jaremski are better supported by evidence Two theories of deposit insurance
I. "Economic": eliminates liquidity risk
(runs) . What we see in 1907,1930s
- May produce incentives to take too much risk - moral hazard
II. "Political" approach: outcome of political coalitions and bargaining, to shift rents and costs towards or away from bankers, depositors, borrowers, taxpayers
Theory, plus evidence from mid 1800s & 1910-1920s
[Calomiris and Jaremski(2016)] and [Calomiris and Haber(2015)] pp 174 &
187. Also [Calomiris and Jaremski(2019)]
# Diamond & Dybvig Model Produces Runs
Diamond & Dybvig p 14
Bank runs are a common feature of the extreme crises that have played a prominent role in [US] monetary history. During a bank run,
I. depositors rush to withdraw their deposits because they expect the bank to fail.
II. the sudden withdrawals can force the bank to liquidate many of its assets at a loss and to fail.
III. During a panic with many bank failures, there is a disruption of the monetary system and a reduction in production.
Why not Canada? Fragmented US banking with small banks
# 1.2 Diamond & Dybvig (1983) Classic Story - Two Equilibria Contents
# Diamond & Dybvig (1983) Classic Story - Two Equilibria
There is an Illiquid "Loan" - technology that increases investment, but takes time A "Bank" creates Liquid "Deposit" Asset - people can use it as money if they need
```latex
\begin{document}
\begin{tabular}{|c|c|c|c|}
\hline
Bank Assets & Period 0 & Period 1 & Period 2 \\ \hline
Illiquid – “Loan” & $1 – & $1 – & $2 \\ \hline
Liquid – “Deposit” & $1 – & $1.28 – & $1.813 \\ \hline
\end{tabular}
\end{document}
```

- Small fraction of population (25%) want to consume early
- At beginning nobody knows, so everybody wants a "liquid contract"
- "Liquid Contract" - pays below "fair" ($1.28, $1.813) but allows early withdrawal
- As long as $25\%$ withdraw, works. But two equilibria:
I. Good Equilibrium: Few (say $25\%$ ) use liquid asset at $t = 1$, The rest $(75\%)$ get more by waiting until $t = 2$.
II. Bank Run: Everybody (100%) tries to use liquid asset at $t = 1$. Only 78.125% get paid, the rest get nothing.
0% Early Withdraw - Fine: "Loan" Worth More Than "Deposit"

```latex
\begin{document}
\begin{tabular}{|c|c|c|c|}
\hline
0% Early Withdraw & Period 0 & Period 1 & Period 2 \\ \hline
Initial Investment & $1 – & $1 & \\ \hline
Payment to “early” & & less $0.0*$1.28 = $0.00 & \\ \hline
Remainder invested & & $1 – & $2 \\ \hline
Payment to “late” & & & less 1.0*$1.813 = $1.813 \\ \hline
Remainder & & & profit $0.187 \\ \hline
\end{tabular}
\end{document}
```

25% Early Withdraw - Good Equilibrium

```latex
\begin{document}
\begin{tabular}{|c|c|c|c|}
\hline
25% Early & Period 0 & Period 1 & Period 2 \\ \hline
Initial Investment & $1 – & $1 & \\ \hline
Payment to “early” & & less 0.25*$1.28 = $0.32 & \\ \hline
Remainder invested & & $0.68 – & $1.36 \\ \hline
Payment to “late” & & & less 0.75*$1.813 = $1.36 \\ \hline
Remainder & & & profit $0 \\ \hline
\end{tabular}
\end{document}
```

50% Early Withdraw - Failure - Not an Equilibrium

```latex
\begin{document}
\begin{tabular}{|c|c|c|c|}
\hline
50% Early & Period 0 & Period 1 & Period 2 \\ \hline
Initial Investment & $1 – & $1 & \\ \hline
Payment to “early” & & less 0.5*$1.28 = $0.64 & \\ \hline
Remainder invested & & $0.36 – & $0.72 \\ \hline
Payment to “late” & & & less 0.5*$1.813 = $0.91 \\ \hline
Remainder & & & loss $0.19 \\ \hline
\end{tabular}
\end{document}
```

78.125% Early Withdraw - Failure - "Run" Equilibrium

```latex
\begin{document}
\begin{tabular}{|c|c|c|c|}
\hline
78.125% Early & Period 0 & Period 1 & Period 2 \\ \hline
Initial Investment & $1 - & $1 & \\ \hline
Payment to &quot;early&quot; & & less 0.78125*$1.28 = $1 & \\ \hline
Remainder invested & & $0 - & $0 \\ \hline
Payment to &quot;late&quot; & & & less 0.21875*$1.813 = $0.40 \\ \hline
Remainder & & & loss $0.40 \\ \hline
\end{tabular}
\end{document}
```

- Proposed ways to stop run:
I. Suspend "liquid" contract if more than $25\%$ try to withdraw
- 1893,1907 solution
3
II. Deposit Insurance
- Serious questions remain
[Diamond and Dybvig(1983), Diamond(2007)]:
Here's the set-up:
- Illiquid Asset exists
- T=1 pays \$1, T=2 pays \$2
- Consumers at Time 0 don't know their type:
- $25\%$ chance at $\mathbf{T} = 1$, need to consume, $75\%$ chance can wait until $\mathbf{T} = 2$
 - Consumers would prefer to have a "liquid asset" that pays T=1, $1.28, T=2, $1.813 (so they can consume \$1.28 if they turn out to be Type 1)
- Bank "creates" liquid asset, promising to pay §1.28 at T=1, §1.813 at T=2
- Everything works fine if $25\%$ actually use the contract at $\mathbf{T} = 1$:
Bank has \$1
25% of people want \$1.28, bank has to give away \$0.32 = 0.25*1.28.
Bank has \$0.68 left, this grows to 2*0.68 = \$1.36. Enough to pay \$1.813 to 75% (1.36/0.75 = 1.813)
- If $56.25\%$ of people get at $\mathbf{T} = 1$, this leaves only §1.28 for the rest at $\mathbf{T} = 2$.
- If $78.125\%$ of people get at $\mathrm{T} = 1$, the bank goes under: 0.78125*1.28 = 1.00
- A small fraction of the population want to consume early. But at the beginning nobody knows who that is, so everybody wants a "liquid contract"
- $25\%$ of population wants to consume early, but you only find out at period 1 (not 0)
- "Liquid Contract" allows anyone to get money early (less money) or late (more money).
- As long as not too many people use the "liquid contract" this works ok
- Bank may hold some cash, some loans (as in balance sheet above) or may sell o some loans (losing some but not too much money)
- But if too many people want liquid payout bank goes insolvent.
- So there are two equilibria:
I. Few (say 25%) use liquid asset at t=1 (get \$1.28), The rest (75%) get more by waiting until t=2 (\$1.36). Everything fine
II. Everybody tries to use liquid asset at $t = 1$. Only $78.125\%$ get paid, the rest get nothing. Bank goes insolvent
- Bank agrees to pay 1.28 for each contract at $t = 1$, but it only has 1.00 total.
- If more than $78.125\%$ of people do liquid contract, bank insolvent. 1.28*0.78125 = 1.00
- If more than $56.25\%$ of people get liquid payout, the rest get less than 1.28 (waiting until $t = 2$ get payout 2, and $1.28^{*}0.5625 = 0.72$, which leaves only $2^{*}0.28 = 0.56$ to be earned by the remaining $43.75\% = 100 - 56.25\%$ ). So everybody should try to get liquid payout. But only $78.125\%$ can do so
# 1.3 Calomiris & Jaremski - Heresy Re Deposit Insurance
# Contents
# Calomiris & Jaremski - Heresy Re Deposit Insurance

Standard (Diamond & Dybvig) view:

- "part of a financial system safety net that promotes financial stability."
Calomiris & Jaremski - Political theories: serving private interests of banks, borrowers, depositors
- Potentially at the expense of the public interest.
- Empirical evidence supports the private-interest approach
- Generally associated with increases, rather than decreases, in systemic risk.
- Prudential regulation generally not very ec tive in limiting systemic risk
- Likely reflects purposeful failures in regulation
- Little point to removing subsidies through strict regulation.
- The politics of liability insurance not narrowly interests of bankers - in many countries, pass-through subsidy targeted to bank borrowers.
# Why Deposits? "Delegated Monitoring"

What are deposits?

- Debt, but immediately redeemable: "on deposit"
- Importantly: first-come, first-served
- Unlike most corporate debt: upon default assets split among debt-holders
- For deposits, you get full amount as long as you are early in line
We have focused on use3fulness of Deposits as "Money" & "Liquidity" but Calomiris & Jaremski also focus on "Delegated Monitoring"
- C&J argue that principal-agent conflicts – bankers select loans and manage risk, but depositors cannot immediately observe banker behavior and e ort – can make deposit structure valuable
- Short-dated debt gives the right to depositors to pull out at any time
- Longer-dated debt would mean depositors might get stuck
- But, why doesn't this apply to other companies as well?
- Maybe because banks deal with money, not steel or cars or iPhones, and money is so much easier to steal and hide and misuse
"Delegated Monitoring": Depositors want to pull out to quickly to escape trouble
- This imposes discipline on banks and bankers
# Deposits Can Be Socially Costly - Liquidity Runs

Deposits as mechanism to impose discipline has social costs

- Bank runs impose big costs - collapse of banking system
1907 exhibits those costs – recession was bad but not horrible before the bank run in October 1907
- Same thing in Great Depression 1930-33: it was bad but nothing horrible until banks started failing: 1st Banking Crisis (Oct 1930), 2nd Banking Crisis (March 1931), 3rd Banking Crisis (1933, January-March)
# Mechanisms To Protect Against Liquidity Risk

Reserves as Basic Protection.

- Holding some fraction of cash as bu er to pay out immediately
To be e ective, must stabilize & diversify
Two ways to stabilize & diversify:
I. Large diversified bank - internal stabilization & diversification
- Stabilization: many depositors (law of large numbers smooths depositor shocks)
- Diversification: Depositors from di erent regions (reduce correlation of asset shocks and depositor shocks)
- Reduces lumpiness of cash and can move cash around the country
II. Deposit insurance - external stabilization & diversification
- Smaller banks pooling resources across banks
But deposit insurance does not allow e cient stabilization & diversification - playing with Other People's Money
# Deposit Insurance: Moral Hazard & Averse Selection

Moral Hazard: playing with other people's money

- Deposit insurance pools money across different banks
- Allows me and my bank to play with money of other banks (or taxpayers)
- I can take on risk and if I lose, the pool of outside money bails me out
- Introduces incentive to take on risk
Adverse Selection: bad banks take insurance
- Those with poor risk & high costs want insurance
- Specifically small, in cient banks with high risk of runs
Deposit Insurance: Competing forces
- Reduces social cost of runs
- Pool of money reduces chance of runs
- Introducing moral hazard / adverse selection and too much risk
- May increase probability of runs
Not clear which will win
- Evidence seems to indicate Moral Hazard & Adverse Selection
# 1.4 Evidence For Increased Costs & Instability
# Contents
# Pre Civil War Liability Insurance

Calomiris & Haber Fragile by Design Chapter 6, p. 174

Six Northern states - two di erent approaches

I. "Good" system: Indiana, Ohio, Iowa

- Small number of banks, unlimited liability, self-regulatory oversight
- Provided both incentive and means to monitor and enforce good behavior – not allowing members to take excessive risk
II. "Bad" system: New York, Michigan, Vermont
Large number of banks, limited liability, weak external regulation
- Provided incentive for members to take more risk (moral hazard)
- Removed cost of more risk (insurance scheme would pay back depositors)
- Did not impose costs (say regulatory constraints) for taking risk
No surprise:
(2) collapsed with outstanding claims, surviving members exited
(1) survived, providing low risk and mutual protection. Survived until new laws in 1860s pushed banks to convert to national bank charters
# Evidence 1920S

Calomiris & Haber Fragile by Design Chapter 6, p. 187, Calomiris & Jaremski

(NBER) , Calomiris & Jaremski

(JFinance) - Fundamentally di erent approach from Fragile by Design - quantitative econometrics (and good)

1908-1917, eight states created deposit insurance of "bad" type

During 1920s, many bank failures

1921-1929,5,712 failures

- Losses worse for deposit insurance states
"Despite being subject to similar exogenous shocks, the insured banking system collapses exhibited much higher loan losses than national banks within the same states or state-chartered banks operating in adjacent states"
[Calomiris and Jaremski(2016)] p. 19 and [Calomiris and Haber(2015)] p.
188. Also [Calomiris and Jaremski(2019)]
# Description Of Bank Insurance

Deposit

(Liability) Insurance - The "bad" kind

- Including many (all or most) State Banks
National vs State Banks
- National Banks: Chartered by Federal, regulated by O ce of the Comptroller of the Currency
- Generally larger, better regulated, better capitalized, more urban
- State Banks: Charter by states, regulated by states
- Generally smaller, less well-capitalized, rural
- National Banks not eligible for state insurance schemes
Eight states passed laws, between 1907-1917
Texas, Kansas, Nebraska, Oklahoma, South Dakota, North Dakota, Mississippi, Washington
![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-15/c441f5b5-2f9d-4e4d-bc65-1301c02e96db/e02b744a3fd22a8a4c60589a24b1bf6896f524588a410f2cab837d7887b30101.jpg)
# 1.5 Digression On Causation & "Identification"
# Contents
# "Identification" Issues: Unobserved Or Confounding Variables

Fundamental issue in economic and all scientific reasoning

Need variation in observables to measure e etc

Here, insured vs non-insured, to see where failures higher

But need variation unrelated to underlying causes

- Maybe a state chooses insurance because the state is inherently more risky (agricultural vs industrial)
Two "levels" of concern:
- Observables: variables that we can measure that are correlated
Slides from summer program
# Correlation: What We Measure
# Causation: What We Care About
- We measure correlation: how one thing moves with another
- We often care about causality: how one thing causes another
# Correlation Does Not Imply Causation

# We Need Causation To Change The World

The reason we seek causal explanations is in order to intervene, to govern the cause so as to govern the e etc: "Policy-thinking is and must be causality-thinking." (Tufte & Dahl, Tufte p 6)

# Examples Will Explain This

The reason we seek causal explanations is in order to intervene, to govern the cause so as to govern the e etc: "Policy-thinking is and must be causality-thinking." ([Tufte(1997a), Tufte(1997b)] p. 6 / 28, quoting Robert A. Dahl, "Cause and E etc in the Study of Politics," in Danial Lerner, ed., Cause and E etc.)

# Cats, Rats, And Plague

Plague came to London in 1665

- Recurrence of Bubonic Plague (the "Black Death") that originally arrived in Britain in 1348 and killed roughly $40 - 60\%$ of the population
Caused by bacteria Yersinia pestis:
- Infects rats - fleas
- Fleas get bloated, bite someone, spit up balls of bacteria infection
- When rats die, fleas jump to humans - plague among humans
![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-15/c441f5b5-2f9d-4e4d-bc65-1301c02e96db/2c0140bf16f33d72c5938543236828a27de2919ba8745eed1ce3d1c3c1f464a3.jpg)
Today we have a theory, a causal mechanism for the how & why of plague
- When rat population increases, plague likely to increase
- To reduce infection, reduce rats (& bites by fleas)
![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-15/c441f5b5-2f9d-4e4d-bc65-1301c02e96db/3d4be29b4d1679831ef19ba0296461fbc01a86f5a82e922906535c6699f55ee7.jpg)
Oriental rat flea (Xenopsylla cheopis) infected with the Yersinia pestis bacterium which appears as a dark mass in the gut. The foregut of this flea is blocked by a Y. pestis biofilm; when the flea attempts to feed on an uninfected host, Y. pestis from the foregut is regurgitated into the wound, causing infection.
Date 27 April 2006
Source http://www.niaid.nih.gov/labsandresources/labs/aboutlabs/1zp/plaguesection/Pages/hinnebusch.aspx
Author National Institute of Allergies and Infectious Diseases-photographer not listed
# Drawing The Cats, Rats, And Plague Relationship

But now think about cats: Mutual causality

- Cats hunt rats, so more rats means more food
- When rat population cat population
- Causal e etc can go other way: cats hunt rats, control population
Let's draw a simple diagram of the cat, rat, and plague relationship
Rats & fleas
![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-15/c441f5b5-2f9d-4e4d-bc65-1301c02e96db/9f5a463a1121093a06fa47ab8cc877bafefe870dbedc629cee333b14d06d51f8.jpg)
Rats & fleas cause plague
- We know this, studying rats, fleas, bacteria
- We put Rats & fleas at the top
Solid line Rats Plague: represents causality
Mutual causality (both ways) between cats & rats
- More rats more cats
- But cats help control rat population
# Correlation Vs Causation: Bad Outcome

Rats & fleas

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-15/c441f5b5-2f9d-4e4d-bc65-1301c02e96db/312c6041fe6df8d97f8cf1ec9abbfa7004ad6bcf05d9faaaf1ad53cc36d3a43b.jpg)

Rats & fleas cause plague

- We know this, studying rats, fleas, bacteria
- We put Rats & fleas at the top
Solid line Rats Plague: represents causality
Mutual causality (both ways) between cats & rats
- More rats more cats
- But cats help control rats
Creates correlation between cats & plague (through rats)
- Cats below, dotted line for observed correlation
In 1665, no understanding of the causal mechanism, no correct theory
Some smart person saw correlation between cats & plague:
- More cats, more plague
- Decided cats caused plague, so killed the cats
# Kill The Cats, Increase The Plague

Wherefore were we ordered to kill all the dogs and cats…. It is incredible… what a prodigious number of those creatures were destroyed. (Daniel Defoe, wonderful English author, Journal of the Plague Year)

What do you think happened?

- No cats to hunt rats
Rat population grew fast
- Plague worse
Wonderful story showing the disasters that occur, the unintended consequences, when we mistake correlation (cats & plague) for causation (rate & fleas Plague)
# Cats & Rats

Correlation alone is not su cient

- Need an understanding plague as a disease with fleas as the vector
- Except maybe it's not fleas. [Greshko(2018), Bates and Salkeld(2011)]
# Comment On Daniel Defoe

Merchant, spy. Spent time in prison for political agitation and also for debts. And one of the great English novelists. Author of *Robinson Crusoe*, "second only to the bible in translations". Journal of the *Plague Year*, an amazing chronicle of the Great Plague of London in

1665. And one of my favorite novels, *Moll Flanders* – the story of the truly amoral but wonderful title character: "the fall and eventual redemption, both material and spiritual, of a lone woman in 17th-century England."

# Minor Problem - Story Not True (But It Should Be)
- It'sa great story, but probably not true
- No evidence that anyone measured correlation between cats & plague
- Cats & dogs killed ("according to the advice of physicians") but probably for other reasons ("carrying the infectious streams even in their furs and hair")
# But It Illustrates The Whole Point Of This Course
- We can easily observe correlation (cats & plague move together)
- Much harder to uncover causation (discover bacteria, study flea life-cycle and feeding behavior, map and study epidemic outbreaks)
- But causation matters - sometimes literally life & death
# Path To Causation Hard:
- True story - John Snow and cholera - to learn
# 1.6 Variation & Confounding In Calomiris & Jaremski
# Contents
# Three Identification

(Causality) Issues

1. DI vs non-DI states: structural di erences
	- Variation: DI states had more failures, worse losses
	- Confounding: Maybe DI states were more risky - agricultural vs industrial
	- Control: Compare DI vs non-DI states prior to enacting DI laws and find similarities
2. Unobserved shocks to DI states causing DI laws
	- Variation: DI states had more failures, worse losses
	- Confounding: Maybe specific events occurred in the DI states that pushed them to pass liability laws
Control:
	- Observables: economic and demographic variables (including time fixed ects) that would "soak up" changes
	- Unobservables: Compare DI State Banks with non-DI National Banks and non-DI State Banks in the same state, that would experience the same shocks. Similar
3. National vs State Bank structural di erences
	- Variation: Within DI states, insured banks had more failures, worse losses versus uninsured banks
	- Confounding: National Banks could not insure, and maybe they were inherently less risky than State Banks – we incorrectly interpret National vs State Bank differences as non-DI vs DI differences
	- Control: Compare DI vs non-DI State Banks across states to control for (measure di erences due to) unobserved variables specific to State Banks vs National Banks.
Econometrically, called "Di erence-in-Di erences-in-Di erences"
# Results - Deposit Insurance Increased Risk

Calomiris & Jaremski

(JoF) focus on two hypotheses:

I. Insured banks should "steal" deposits from uninsured banks (able to o er more attractive terms)

II. Insured banks should use deposits to fund more risk lending (because DI subsidizes risky lending)

1900-1920: Start and Growth of DI Systems

- Laws passed in 1908
(Oklahoma) 1909 (Kansas, Texas), 1911
(Nebraska) , South Dakota (1916), North Dakota (1918)
- Period of growth in agricultural prices, growth in lending
- Evidence that insured banks both grew faster (more deposits and loans) and with more risk (lower capital-to-assets and lower cash-to-assets)
# Results - Worse Collapse

When the Bubble Burst

- Agricultural prices fell, starting 1920
Deposit Insurance could have either e etc:
I. Cushion state banking system by reducing incentive for runs
II. Make collapse worse by encouraging higher risk (also when depositors realize fund out of money, worse runs – funds not state-guaranteed)
Deposit Insurance did not cushion state banks
- All systems suspended during 1920s
- All except Texas produced depositor losses
- Insurance states had worse contraction of deposits and loans
- Many banks left the insurance system (converted to National banks)
C&H page 188
[Calomiris and Jaremski(2019)]
# Deposit Insurance Conclusion - Subsidy For Small Banks
- Calomiris & Jaremski conclude that deposit insurance should be viewed as a political mechanism that transfers to particular groups
- For unit banking in 1800s & 1900s, it was a mechanism to fix the problem of fragile unit banks
- Allowed unit banks to survive
- Could lead to increased failures and larger losses
Modern Deposit Insurance schemes often di erent
- State backing (unlike 1900-1920 systems) so don't run out of money
- More recently, better risk-weighting (higher premiums for more risk activity)
But Calomiris & Jaremski
(NBER) convincingly argue that "political theory" better supported by evidence than "economic theory"
- Argue that DI as subsidy and targeted support (borrowers, depositors) helps explain adoption around the world (since 1930s)
